Title: Stench (Ability)

This entry details the Ability Stench. For information pertaining to the field move Stink, please consult Field Move (Ranger). Known as あくしゅう (Stench) in Japanese, this Ability first appeared in Generation III. Before Generation IV, it served as the signature Ability for Grimer and Muk. When a Pokémon with Stench connects with a damaging move, there is a 10% chance the target will flinch. This flinching effect does not stack with items such as King's Rock or Razor Fang, nor does it combine with moves that already possess an inherent chance to flinch. Importantly, each individual strike of a multistrike move carries its own independent opportunity to cause flinching. Outside of combat, if a Pokémon with Stench leads the party (even if fainted), the probability of encountering wild Pokémon is halved (decreased by 50%). This particular effect is shared with Quick Feet, White Smoke, and, since Generation VIII, Infiltrator. Within the Battle Pyramid, the wild Pokémon encounter rate sees a 25% reduction. Exclusively in Pokémon Sword and Shield, this Ability's function shifts to reduce the chance of hidden encounters by 87.5%. However, in Pokémon Scarlet and Violet, Stench provides no effects whatsoever outside of battle. Should a Pokémon with Stench be attacked by an adjacent foe, the aggressor may become terrified for a brief duration. In the episode Sliding Into Seventh!, Jeremiah's Skuntank was confirmed to possess Stench as its Ability, though its activation occurred off-screen. Visually, its effect manifests as a lavender, foul-smelling explosion. Geoff's Trubbish was also revealed to have Stench as its Ability. Regarding its index number sequence, Stench is the inaugural Ability.

Title: Drizzle (Ability)

Known in Japanese as あめふらし (Rainfall), Drizzle is an Ability first appearing in Generation III. Before Generation V, it served as Kyogre's exclusive Ability. When a Pokémon possessing Drizzle enters combat, it immediately conjures rain. Initially, this weather endured throughout the entire battle (unless displaced by other weather), mirroring the move Rain Dance but without a limited turn count. However, the rain it currently invokes typically lasts for only five turns. Holding a Damp Rock can prolong this effect to eight turns. Should a Drizzle-bearing Pokémon be deployed concurrently with another Pokémon whose Ability influences weather, the slower Pokémon's Ability will take precedence, overriding that of the faster one. Interestingly, the rain generated by Drizzle does not facilitate Sliggoo's evolution into Goodra. Drizzle's activation is suppressed if its bearer is a Kyogre poised for Primal Reversion; in such instances, Primal Reversion takes precedence, Kyogre's Ability transforms into Primordial Sea, and only Primordial Sea will then become active. Furthermore, Drizzle fails to take effect if heavy rain, extremely harsh sunlight, or strong winds are already influencing the battlefield.

Within the spin-off titles Red Rescue Team, Blue Rescue Team, and Explorers of Time, Darkness and Sky, Drizzle causes rain immediately upon entering a new floor, and subsequently every 36 turns thereafter. In Super Mystery Dungeon, Drizzle's effect is to summon rain immediately upon a floor's entry. This rain persists for a variable number of turns (occurring in multiples of 66) before dissipating. Once the weather clears, Drizzle will reactivate after another waiting period of a certain number of turns (also in multiples of 66).

A notable instance of Drizzle in lore involves a Kyogre-like entity, composed of seawater and conjured by Guile Hideout's wish to Jirachi, which possessed Drizzle as its Ability. In the Pokémon Trading Card Game, Drizzle first appeared as an attack on Azumarill, part of the EX Sandstorm expansion. Upon its introduction, this attack enabled players to attach any number of Water Energy cards from their hand to their Active Pokémon. However, as of the Legends Awakened expansion, the current version of the Drizzle attack permits attaching up to two Water Energy cards from hand to a single one of the player's Pokémon. A compilation of Pokémon cards featuring the Drizzle attack follows.

Title: Speed Boost (Ability)

Known in Japanese as かそく (Speed Up), Speed Boost is an Ability that made its debut in Generation III. At the close of every turn, a Pokémon carrying this Ability experiences a one-stage elevation in its Speed stat. This beneficial effect will not trigger on the turn the Pokémon is sent into battle, even if the Ability is gained immediately after switching in. Nevertheless, if the Pokémon enters the field because a preceding ally fainted, Speed Boost will indeed activate. Beginning with Generation V, the Ability's activation at the end of a turn is prevented if the user was unsuccessful in fleeing a wild encounter during that same turn.

Specifically in Generation V, the Pokémon Venipede, Whirlipede, and Scolipede possessed Quick Feet as their Hidden Ability, rather than Speed Boost. Any statistical data provided alongside this information represents exact figures, not the Pokémon's base stats. These numerical values are custom-set and are not calculated from typical factors like base stats, level, Nature, Individual Values (IVs), and Effort Values (EVs).

In Mystery Dungeon titles released before Super Mystery Dungeon, Pokémon with Speed Boost would receive an enhancement to their Movement Speed every 250 turns, which then endured for the entirety of the current floor. In Super Mystery Dungeon, the mechanism is altered: a Pokémon wielding this Ability gains a one-point increase in Travel Speed after spending one turn on a floor, with this bonus reapplying every 20 turns thereafter. Each instance of this Travel Speed boost fades after five turns.

Title: Battle Armor (Ability)

Battle Armor (Japanese: カブトアーマー Kabuto Armor) is an Ability that first appeared in Generation III. This capability ensures that the Pokémon possessing it cannot receive critical hits. Its protection holds even against moves specifically designed to always land critically, such as Frost Breath, Storm Throw, Wicked Blow, Surging Strikes, and Flower Trick. Moreover, Battle Armor negates critical hits from any move boosted by Merciless (if the Pokémon is poisoned) or Laser Focus. The PokéSPedia data-book reveals Blake's Kabutops, Kabutott, to possess Battle Armor as its Ability. It was additionally disclosed that Brock's Kabutops and Casey's Falinks, Giga, also had Battle Armor.

Title: Sturdy (Ability)

Known in Japanese as がんじょう (Ganjō), Sturdy is an Ability that made its debut in Generation III. This Power renders a Pokémon immune to one-hit knockout attacks. Beyond this initial function, Sturdy now behaves similarly to a Focus Sash: if an opposing Pokémon's attack or self-inflicted confusion damage would reduce the user from full health directly to 0 HP, Sturdy ensures it survives with 1 HP instead. Sturdy activates only when the Pokémon has maximum HP before taking damage, allowing for multiple activations if its health is fully restored, or if its maximum HP is naturally 1. Should a Pokémon with Sturdy also be equipped with a Focus Sash, Sturdy's effect triggers first. However, a Pokémon possessing Abilities like Mold Breaker will disregard Sturdy. When subjected to a multi-strike move, Sturdy's effect only applies to hits received while the user retains full HP. Moves such as Destiny Bond and Perish Song do not cause Sturdy to activate.

In Pokémon UNITE, Sturdy serves as Crustle's unique Ability. In this context, it progressively boosts the user's Defense and Special Defense (scaling with level) for every 7% of HP they are missing, up to a total of 70% missing HP. Regarding the manga, Emerald's Pokédex confirmed Lucy's Shuckle possessed Sturdy in 'Just My Luck...Shuckle'. Volume 25 revealed Yellow's Golem, Gravvy, utilized Sturdy. Diamond's Bastiodon, Don, was shown to have Sturdy in volume 40, and Gold's Sudowoodo, Sudobo, in volume 42. The PokéSPedia data-book also indicated Giovanni's Golem had Sturdy. Additionally, Sturdy has been identified as the Ability of the Pokémon Association's Pineco, Carr's Forretress, Burgh's Dwebble, Marshal's Sawk, and Cheren's Gigalith. Every subsequent generation has introduced a new Pokémon or a new form of an existing Pokémon capable of manifesting this Ability. Sturdy holds the distinction of being the most prevalent Ability across all Pokémon species, with 47 of them able to possess it.

Title: Damp (Ability)

The term "Moisture" leads to this page; for details on Berries in Generation IV, please consult the Berry entry. Damp (Japanese: しめりけ Moisture) is an Ability that was first introduced in Generation III. This Ability prevents all Pokémon from initiating Self-Destruct, Explosion, Mind Blown, and Misty Explosion. Should a Pokémon attempt to use one of these particular moves, the action will fail, and the user will neither faint nor suffer any damage.

Beyond preventing self-destructive moves, Damp also stops the Aftermath Ability from inflicting damage upon the Pokémon with Aftermath fainting. In the context of Triple Battles, Damp's effects extend to Pokémon that are not directly next to the user. Within the Mystery Dungeon game series, Damp causes the floor to become defused, thereby nullifying all explosions, which includes both the Explosion move and hazards like the Explosion Trap. Furthermore, beginning with Super Mystery Dungeon, items such as the Blast Seed and Blast Wand will likewise become ineffective. Crasher Wake's Quagsire notably possesses Damp as its Ability. It is crucial to understand that, despite their explosive nature, Damp does not hinder the use of Blast Burn, Powder, Pollen Puff, or Shell Trap.

Title: Limber (Ability)

Limber (Japanese: じゅうなん, meaning Flexible) is a Pokémon Ability first introduced in Generation III. This inherent characteristic completely prevents a Pokémon from experiencing paralysis. Should a Pokémon become paralyzed while engaged in battle—perhaps by acquiring Limber mid-fight through effects like Skill Swap, or after being afflicted by a Pokémon with an Ability such as Mold Breaker—Limber immediately nullifies the status condition.

Furthermore, if a paralyzed Pokémon possessing this Ability is simultaneously holding either a Cheri Berry or a Lum Berry, Limber's effect takes precedence, removing the paralysis before the Berry has a chance to be consumed. However, if a paralyzed Pokémon with Limber enters battle as an initial combatant, replaces a fainted ally, or gains Limber via the Trace Ability, its paralysis will not be healed until after another Pokémon has completed an action (e.g., used a move or switched out). Conversely, if such a Pokémon is substituted into battle to replace a Pokémon that has switched out (including instances involving Baton Pass), its paralysis is immediately cured.

A paralyzed Pokémon that *originally* possessed Limber but *currently* has a different Ability will remain paralyzed upon being switched out. Nevertheless, if a paralyzed Pokémon whose original Ability was Limber (but not its current one) is either active at the battle's conclusion or switched out, it will be relieved of its paralysis without any accompanying message. When a paralyzed Pokémon with Limber *simply* enters battle, it is cured of its paralysis. Should a paralyzed Pokémon acquire Limber through Evolution or the use of an Ability Capsule, its paralysis will persist until it enters battle.

Pokémon equipped with Limber are immune to paralysis from the Gentleman's Kirlia encountered in the Battle Pike, as well as the Battle Arcade's game board effects. Regarding notable instances, Green's Ditto, Ditty, was revealed to possess Limber in volume 24 of the manga. White's Stunfisk, Dorothy, was also confirmed to have this Ability in the PokéSPedia data-book. Additionally, Sird's Persian has been shown to have Limber. Given that Electric-type Pokémon gained immunity to paralysis starting from Generation VI, Stunfisk's Limber Ability typically serves no purpose unless its type is altered by moves like Camouflage, Reflect Type, or Soak.

Title: Sand Veil (Ability)

First appearing in Generation III, Sand Veil (Japanese: すながくれ, meaning 'Hidden in Sand') is a specific Pokémon Ability. During a sandstorm, any move used against a Pokémon possessing this Ability will have its accuracy modified by a factor of 4/5, a value more precisely approximated as 3277/4096 since Generation V. Furthermore, a Pokémon with Sand Veil is immune to sandstorm damage that it would otherwise incur. Beginning with Pokémon Emerald, if a Sand Veil-user is placed at the front of the party (even if fainted) in an area experiencing a sandstorm (for example, Route 228 in Sinnoh), the wild Pokémon encounter rate is decreased by 50%. This Ability, however, seemingly exerts no influence on the encounter rate in Pokémon Sword and Shield, nor does it in Pokémon Scarlet and Violet. When activated, Sand Veil grants a two-stage increase to the user's evasion. Notably, Sand Veil does not provide protection from sandstorm damage in the games Red Rescue Team and Blue Rescue Team. Volume 40 confirmed Pearl's Diglett, Digler, to have Sand Veil as his Ability. The PokéSPedia data-book additionally revealed that Red's Diglett and Sandshrew, along with Giovanni's Dugtrio, were endowed with Sand Veil. Wally's Cacturne and Cynthia's Garchomp were also noted to possess this Ability. Before its establishment as an Ability, Sand Veil existed as an Attack within the Pokémon Trading Card Game.

Title: Static (Ability)

The Ability known as Static (Japanese: せいでんき, meaning Static Electricity) was first introduced in Generation III. When a Pokémon with this Ability is struck by a contact-making move, there is a one-in-three chance that the attacking Pokémon will become paralyzed. Notably, this effect can even be applied to Ground-type Pokémon. Should a Pokémon possessing Static be hit by a multistrike move that makes contact, each individual hit carries an independent chance to trigger the Ability. The likelihood of Static inducing paralysis is subsequently reduced to 30%.

From Pokémon Emerald onwards, if a Pokémon holding Static leads the party (even if fainted), there is a 50% probability the game will force an encounter with an Electric-type Pokémon, if one is available. In Pokémon Sword and Shield, Lightning Rod also began to share this particular effect. Exclusively within Pokémon Sword and Shield, this chance is elevated to 51%. However, in Pokémon Scarlet and Violet, Static holds no effect outside of battle, with its prior field utility effectively being superseded by Meal Powers. For the Generation V games, Zapdos was listed with Lightning Rod as its Hidden Ability instead of Static, though it was unobtainable through any legitimate means.

In Pokémon UNITE, Pikachu utilizes Static as its Ability. When Pikachu takes damage, Static briefly paralyzes and slows all nearby enemies. A 30-second cooldown is enforced following the activation of this Ability.

Within various manga and data-books, several Pokémon are depicted with Static. Yellow's Pikachu, Chuchu, was disclosed to possess Static as her Ability in Secrets from Sneasel. Volume 23 confirmed that Red's Pikachu, Pika, also had Static. Scarlet's Pikachu was revealed to have Static as her Ability in Volume 4 of the Scarlet & Violet arc. The PokéSPedia data-book identifies Gold's Pichu, Pibu, as having Static. Furthermore, Static is the Ability attributed to Crystal's Flaaffy, Lt. Surge's Electabuzz and Raichu, and Jasmine's Ampharos, Amphy.

Title: Volt Absorb (Ability)

Known in Japanese as ちくでん (Electricity Storage), Volt Absorb is an Ability that debuted in Generation III. Should a Pokémon possessing Volt Absorb be struck by a damaging Electric-type move, it gains HP equivalent to one quarter of its maximum, and the attack itself becomes nullified. (If the Pokémon's HP is already full, the Electric-type move will merely have no consequence.) However, Volt Absorb does not activate if the Pokémon is protected from the Electric-type move. As of recent changes, Volt Absorb also triggers when its bearer is struck by an Electric-type status move. Only a single activation of this Ability occurs for multistrike moves converted to Electric-type, for example, by Electrify or Galvanize. Should a Pokémon with Volt Absorb be holding a Cell Battery, Volt Absorb's absorption of the move prevents the Cell Battery's effect from triggering. Within the Generation V and VI titles, Raikou possesses Volt Absorb as its Hidden Ability; however, no legitimate method exists to acquire it. In Generation VII, Inner Focus became its replacement Hidden Ability. Across Red Rescue Team and Blue Rescue Team, Explorers of Time, Darkness and Sky, and the Mystery Dungeon (WiiWare) installments, if a Pokémon with Volt Absorb is struck by an Electric-type move, its HP is recovered by the precise amount of damage that attack would have dealt. Conversely, in Gates to Infinity and Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, Volt Absorb instead replenishes 20% of the Pokémon's total HP upon receiving an Electric-type attack. In the game Pokémon UNITE, Zeraora's Ability is Volt Absorb; this unique version converts 8% of incoming damage Zeraora takes into Attack, capped at 200 Attack. The associated buff dissipates if Zeraora avoids damage for 4 seconds. Red's Eevee, known as Vee, is shown to acquire Volt Absorb as his Ability when he evolves into a Jolteon.

Title: Water Absorb (Ability)

Known in Japanese as ちょすい (Water Storage), Water Absorb is an Ability that first appeared in Generation III. Should a Pokémon possessing Water Absorb be struck by a damaging Water-type attack, its HP is restored by one-fourth of its maximum, and the move will not affect the Pokémon. If the Pokémon is already at full HP, the Water-type move will simply do nothing. Water Absorb will not activate, however, if the Pokémon is protected from the Water-type move. The Ability also now functions when a Pokémon with it is hit by a Water-type status move, though none targeted an opponent until Soak's introduction in Generation V. Multistrike attacks, such as Water Shuriken, will only trigger the Ability once. If a Pokémon bearing Water Absorb is holding an Absorb Bulb or Luminous Moss, the Ability takes precedence in absorbing the move, thus preventing the item's effect from activating.

During the Generation V and VI games, Suicune possessed Water Absorb as its Hidden Ability, though it was unobtainable through legitimate means. In Generation VII, its Hidden Ability was replaced by Inner Focus. Within the games Red Rescue Team and Blue Rescue Team, Explorers of Time, Darkness and Sky, and the Mystery Dungeon (WiiWare) series, a Pokémon bearing Water Absorb that is struck by a Water-type move will have its HP restored by the precise amount of damage the attack would have caused. Conversely, in Gates to Infinity and Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, Water Absorb instead replenishes 20% of the Pokémon's maximum HP upon being affected by a Water-type move.

In volume 42, Gold's Politoed, Polibo, was disclosed as possessing Water Absorb as his Ability. Gold's Mantine, Tibo, is also shown to have Water Absorb as his Ability. Red's Eevee, Vee, is depicted with Water Absorb as his Ability when it transforms into a Vaporeon. Among its variations, Water Absorb holds the distinction of being the first Ability showcased in the animated series.

Title: Oblivious (Ability)

The subject of this text is the Ability known as Oblivious, which grants immunity to infatuation. For information regarding an Ability with a similar meaning, refer to Unaware (Ability). Originating in Generation III, Oblivious (Japanese: どんかん, translated as Thickheaded) is a specific Ability. Any Pokémon possessing Oblivious gains protection from both infatuation and the move Captivate. Should a Pokémon, already infatuated, obtain this Ability during a battle (for instance, through a move like Skill Swap), its infatuation status will instantly be nullified. In cases where an Oblivious-bearing Pokémon is holding a Destiny Knot, the item's effect will trigger prior to Oblivious removing the infatuation condition. Previously, Oblivious offered no defense against Intimidate or Taunt. If a Pokémon afflicted by infatuation and equipped with a Mental Herb also has Oblivious, the Ability will nullify the infatuation before the Mental Herb can be used. Conversely, should a Pokémon with this Ability be infatuated and holding a Mental Herb, the Mental Herb would instead be expended before Oblivious resolves the infatuation. Currently, Oblivious actively prevents the Pokémon from falling under the effects of Taunt. Furthermore, if a Pokémon already subjected to Taunt acquires Oblivious during combat (e.g., via Skill Swap), it will immediately be freed from the taunting condition. However, should a Pokémon possessing Oblivious be targeted by a Taunt from a foe with an Ability such as Mold Breaker, the taunting effect will initially succeed, yet it will be dispelled once the Pokémon's Oblivious effectively reactivates. In scenarios where the Pokémon holds a Mental Herb while experiencing Taunt, the item will be consumed before Oblivious has a chance to alleviate the Taunt. Within Pokémon UNITE, Oblivious serves as the designated Ability for Slowbro, Bounsweet, and Steenee. Slowbro's rendition of Oblivious enables the user to accumulate a portion of lost HP for a brief duration; upon striking an opposing Pokémon with an attack, the user recovers some of this stored HP, and simultaneously reduces the target's Special Defense by 4% (cumulative up to 20%) for a short period. For Bounsweet and Steenee, this Ability causes hindrances to persist for a reduced length of time. Upon their evolution, this specific Ability transforms into Queenly Majesty. It has been confirmed that Oblivious is the Ability possessed by Crystal's Smoochum, Chumee; Sapphire's Wailord, Lorry; Pryce's Swinub; and Carl's Piloswine. A glitch present in Generation IV resulted in the following behavior: if a Pokémon suffering from a non-volatile status condition had its infatuation removed by Oblivious, the icon representing that status condition would vanish from its HP bar during battle; nevertheless, the icon would still be visible in the party menu and would reappear if the Pokémon was withdrawn from battle and then sent back out. This identical glitch phenomenon also applies to instances of confusion when paired with the Own Tempo Ability.

Title: Cloud Nine (Ability)

Cloud Nine (Japanese: ノーてんき, or No Weather) is an Ability that made its debut in Generation III. Before the release of Generation V, it was exclusively associated with Psyduck and Golduck. When a Pokémon possessing Cloud Nine participates in a battle, all impacts of active weather conditions are nullified, though the weather itself continues to persist on the field. The message "The effects of weather disappeared." is displayed when a Pokémon with Cloud Nine enters the fray.

In situations involving extremely harsh sunlight, heavy rain, or strong winds, specific moves—namely Sunny Day, Rain Dance, Sandstorm, and Hail—will still be unsuccessful if attempted, even if Cloud Nine is active and negating weather effects. Similarly, Abilities like Drought, Drizzle, Sand Stream, and Snow Warning will still fail to trigger. However, Cloud Nine successfully suppresses all other consequences stemming from these extreme weather phenomena.

Within the framework of Pokémon Red Rescue Team, Blue Rescue Team, and the Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky titles, Cloud Nine immediately converts the current weather to Clear upon entering a floor, and subsequently every 36 turns. This Ability overrides the effects of Drizzle, Drought, Sand Stream, and Snow Warning. Furthermore, it prevents the execution of moves such as Sunny Day, Rain Dance, Hail, and Sandstorm. Items like the Sunny Orb, Rainy Orb, Sandy Orb, and Hail Orb are rendered useless (though attempting to use them will still expend the item). Should the dungeon naturally feature specific weather, it will resume once Cloud Nine's influence ceases.

In Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, Cloud Nine operates by negating the effects of weather rather than altering it to Clear. Abilities like Drizzle and moves such as Rain Dance will still activate, but their resulting weather effects will consistently remain suppressed.

Blue's Golduck was depicted with Cloud Nine as its Ability in volume 23 of the manga. The Japanese designation for this Ability, ノーてんき, functions as a double entendre. While it literally translates to "No Weather" (ノー天気), it is also homophonous with 能天気 (nōtenki), meaning "carefree" – a term also used for the Lax nature. This dual meaning alludes to Psyduck, which, alongside Golduck, originally possessed this Ability. The English name "Cloud Nine" directly conveys this "carefree" sentiment, while also retaining a subtle play on words connected to meteorological conditions.

Title: Compound Eyes (Ability)

The Ability known as Compound Eyes (Japanese: ふくがん Compound Eyes) first appeared in Generation III. Before the release of Pokémon X and Y, its name was styled as Compoundeyes. For a Pokémon possessing this Ability, Compound Eyes provides a boost to the precision of its moves, multiplying their base accuracy by a factor of 1.3. Consequently, a move like Thunder, which normally possesses 70% accuracy, instead achieves 91% precision (calculated as 70 × 1.3 = 91%) when performed by a Pokémon with Compound Eyes. From Generation V onwards, this accuracy multiplier is precisely approximated as 5325/4096. However, it has no effect on one-hit knockout moves. Further information specifies which moves are enhanced by Compound Eyes, along with the specific Pokémon capable of both possessing this Ability and learning those particular moves. Despite being impacted by Compound Eyes, certain moves are not presently accessible to any Pokémon that natively possesses this Ability. Nonetheless, these moves may still be executed through actions like Metronome, Mirror Move, Copycat, or Mimic. Alternatively, a Pokémon could leverage them if it acquired Compound Eyes via an Ability like Trace or Receiver, or a move such as Skill Swap or Role Play.

Beginning with Pokémon Emerald, when a Pokémon possessing Compound Eyes heads the party (even in a fainted state), the probability of encountering a wild Pokémon carrying an item improves from 50%/5% to 60%/20%. Within Generation V's dark grass, these probabilities further rise from 50%/5%/1% to 60%/20%/5%. By Generation VIII, this particular effect became shared with the Ability Super Luck. However, in Pokémon Scarlet and Violet, Compound Eyes no longer influences the items carried by wild Pokémon. Across the Red Rescue Team and Blue Rescue Team titles, as well as Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky, the Ability Compoundeyes elevates accuracy by two stages. Volume 25 notably disclosed that Yellow's Butterfree, named Kitty, possessed Compound Eyes as his designated Ability.

Title: Insomnia (Ability)

Insomnia (Japanese: ふみん Insomnia) is an Ability first introduced in Generation III. This capability completely safeguards the Pokémon from falling asleep and from the effects of Yawn. Any attempt by a Pokémon with Insomnia to use the move Rest will fail. Should a Pokémon find itself asleep during a battle, it will awaken immediately upon acquiring Insomnia. This applies if the Ability is gained prior to battle, through effects such as Skill Swap, or even if sleep was induced by a Pokémon with an Ability like Mold Breaker. When a Pokémon possessing this Ability is asleep and also holds either a Chesto Berry or a Lum Berry, Insomnia will alleviate its sleep condition before the Berry can be activated or consumed. Even if a Pokémon under the influence of Yawn becomes drowsy, Insomnia will activate at the precise moment sleep would normally occur, thereby preventing the Pokémon from succumbing to it. The technique known as Worry Seed possesses the capacity to transform a target Pokémon's Ability into Insomnia.

However, if a sleeping Pokémon with Insomnia enters the fray as a lead or a replacement for a fainted ally, or if it acquires Insomnia via Trace, its sleep status will persist until after another Pokémon performs an action (such as using a move or switching out). Conversely, if such a Pokémon is switched in to replace an active Pokémon that has departed the battle (including through Baton Pass), its sleep will be instantly remedied. If a sleeping Pokémon has Insomnia as its natural Ability but not its currently active one, it will continue to sleep upon being switched out. Upon a sleeping Pokémon with Insomnia entering battle, its sleep condition is immediately resolved. Furthermore, if a sleeping Pokémon has Insomnia as its original Ability but not its current one, and it is either still active at the battle's conclusion or switched out, its sleep will be cured silently (without an on-screen message). Despite having Insomnia as its inherent Ability, if a sleeping Pokémon's active Ability is different, it will stay asleep when switched out of battle. Should a sleeping Pokémon acquire Insomnia through Evolution or by using an Ability Capsule, its slumber will persist until it is deployed in battle.

Pokémon equipped with Insomnia are impervious to sleep-inducing effects from the Gentleman's Kirlia in the Battle Pike and the Battle Arcade's game board. Volume 42 disclosed that Silver's Honchkrow possessed Insomnia as its Ability. The PokéSPedia data-book confirmed that Cyrus's Honchkrow also utilized Insomnia. Janine's Ariados was shown to possess Insomnia. Roxanne's Cradily was also noted to have Insomnia as its Ability.* This Ability stands as one of merely three that completely avert sleep, with Vital Spirit and Sweet Veil being the other two. Remarkably, Delibird has Insomnia as its Hidden Ability, even though it already possesses Vital Spirit as a standard Ability, both of which share the identical sleep-preventing effect.

Title: Color Change (Ability)

Known in Japanese as へんしょく Color Change, this Ability debuted in Generation III. It serves as Kecleon's signature capability. Upon being struck by a damaging attack (with specific exclusions detailed later), a Pokémon possessing Color Change will have its type(s) altered to match the type of the incoming move. Regardless of whether the Pokémon initially holds a dual-type classification, the Ability's activation leaves it with only a single type. Color Change does not trigger if the Pokémon is hit by an attack sharing its own type, a rule that holds true even for Pokémon with two or three types (such as those affected by Forest's Curse or Trick-or-Treat). Furthermore, Color Change remains inactive if a Substitute takes the hit instead of the Pokémon itself. When a Pokémon with this Ability is struck by Weather Ball during shadowy aura, its type will transform into Normal. It's crucial to note that status ailments are applied prior to Color Change's activation. Consequently, a Pokémon can still suffer from a status condition, even if Color Change would subsequently make its type immune to that very condition; for example, a Pokémon with Color Change hit by Poison Sting may still be poisoned before transitioning to a Poison-type. Specific moves that do not trigger Color Change include Struggle, Shadow moves, Stellar moves, Pain Split, and Revelation Dance when it lacks a type. Similarly, activation is suppressed if the Pokémon is under the influence of Forest's Curse or Trick-or-Treat and is then struck by a Grass-type or Ghost-type move, respectively.

In past iterations, Color Change would trigger following the first hit of a multistrike move, and Future Sight and Doom Desire were incapable of activating it. However, as of recent changes, Color Change now activates after the final hit of a multistrike move. Furthermore, Future Sight and Doom Desire now successfully activate Color Change upon impact (though not when initially used). The Japanese designation for Color Change is identical to that of a Pokémon Power exhibiting a comparable effect. Notably, Color Change stands as the inaugural Ability showcased in Pokémon animation, and it was the sole Ability depicted within the original series. Beyond animation, it was also the first Ability to be demonstrated in the Pokémon Adventures manga.

Title: Immunity (Ability)

The Ability known as Immunity (Japanese: めんえき Immunity) made its debut in Generation III. This valuable Ability completely safeguards the Pokémon possessing it from becoming afflicted by the poison status condition. Should a Pokémon with Immunity somehow become poisoned during a battle—for example, by gaining the Ability outside of combat, through an effect like Skill Swap, or being poisoned by an attacker with an Ability such as Mold Breaker—it will immediately be cured of the poison.

In scenarios where a poisoned Pokémon also possesses Immunity and is holding a Pecha, Lum, or Drash Berry, Immunity’s effect takes precedence, removing the poison before the Berry has a chance to be consumed. However, if a poisoned Pokémon with Immunity is deployed as an initial combatant, substituted for a defeated ally, or gains Immunity via Trace, its poison will persist until *after* a Pokémon has completed an action (such as executing a move or withdrawing). Conversely, if such a Pokémon is switched in to replace another Pokémon that has already switched out (this includes entries facilitated by Baton Pass), it will be immediately cured of poison.

Should a poisoned Pokémon possess Immunity as its original Ability but not its currently active one, it will remain poisoned upon being withdrawn from battle. Generally, a poisoned Pokémon entering battle with Immunity active will be cleansed of its poison. If a poisoned Pokémon has Immunity as its original Ability but not its current Ability, and it is still active at the end of the battle, or it was switched out, it will be cured of poison (with no message). If a poisoned Pokémon has Immunity as its original Ability but not its current Ability, it will remain poisoned when switched out.

An attacking Pokémon using Sunsteel Strike while possessing Poison Touch can still inflict poison on a Pokémon with Immunity, though the affected Pokémon will recover from the status instantly. Notably, in Generations III and IV, the harmful effects of poison persisted outside of battle for Pokémon, even those endowed with Immunity. Should a poisoned Pokémon acquire Immunity through means like Evolution or an Ability Capsule, its poison will not be removed until it is deployed into battle.

Neither the Gentleman's Kirlia in the Battle Pike nor the Battle Arcade's game board are capable of poisoning a Pokémon with Immunity. In volume 23, it was revealed that Red's Snorlax, known as Snor, possesses Immunity as his Ability. Additionally, Sidney's Zangoose is confirmed to have Immunity as its Ability. The likely rationale behind Zangoose's Immunity Ability is its basis on the mongoose, an animal often resistant or immune to snake venom.

Title: Flash Fire (Ability)

This entry details the Ability. For information concerning the TCG expansion, refer to Flashfire (TCG). Known in Japanese as もらいび (Catching on Fire), Flash Fire is an Ability that was introduced in Generation III. It grants the Pokémon immunity to Fire-type moves and becomes active upon being struck by one. Once triggered, the power of the Pokémon's Fire-type moves is increased by 50%. While subsequent hits from Fire-type moves will not confer further power augmentations, the Pokémon retains its immunity to them. Flash Fire will not activate if the Pokémon is protected from the Fire-type move. Historically, Flash Fire would not function while the user was frozen. Rather than directly boosting Fire-type moves' power, Flash Fire now technically enhances the Pokémon's Attack or Special Attack by 50% during damage calculation when a Fire-type move is employed, resulting in an identical practical outcome. Flash Fire is presently functional even if the user is frozen, though it does not thaw the Pokémon. In Pokémon Sword and Shield, positioning a Pokémon with Flash Fire at the front of the party (even if fainted) carried a 51% chance for the game to force an encounter with a Fire-type Pokémon, if such an encounter was possible. In Pokémon Brilliant Diamond and Shining Pearl, this chance was 50%. However, in Pokémon Scarlet and Violet, this Ability has no effect outside of battle, with its former field function effectively superseded by Meal Powers. Across the Generation V and VI games, Entei possessed Flash Fire as its Hidden Ability, yet it could not be obtained via any legitimate means. Its Hidden Ability was subsequently replaced by Inner Focus in Generation VII. Within Pokémon Legends: Arceus's internal code, Hisuian Typhlosion's Hidden Ability is identified as Flash Fire instead of Frisk. In the Mystery Dungeon series, specifically Red Rescue Team, Blue Rescue Team, and the Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky titles, Flash Fire neutralizes Fire-type moves and increments the Pokémon's "Flash Fire value" by 1 for the rest of the floor; this effect is stackable twice. If the Pokémon then performs a Fire-type move, their Special Attack is temporarily raised by that amount of stages (but cannot increase past +10). In the manga Clash! Yū vs. The Wandering Player, Yū's Heatran was depicted with Flash Fire as its Ability, though its activation was not shown. It was gained by his Bronzong through Skill Swap and subsequently transferred to his Scizor. Volume 40 revealed Platinum's Rapidash as having Flash Fire as his Ability. In Volume 4 of the Scarlet & Violet arc, Scarlet's Armarouge was revealed to possess Flash Fire as his Ability. Blue's Ninetales was confirmed to have Flash Fire as his Ability in the PokéSPedia data-book. Blaine's Growlithe, Bianca's Lampent, and Charon's Heatran were all noted to have Flash Fire as their Ability. Red's Eevee, Vee, is shown to gain Flash Fire as his Ability upon transforming into a Flareon. Of the Abilities that negate damage and provide a stat boost when hit by a specific type of attack, Flash Fire is unique in that it does not offer stackable boosts or additional benefits from subsequent hits. Rolycoly is the sole non-Fire-type Pokémon to have access to Flash Fire.

Title: Shield Dust (Ability)

Making its debut in Generation III, Shield Dust (りんぷん Scales in Japanese) is a Pokémon Ability. Any Pokémon endowed with Shield Dust is immune to the secondary impacts of damaging moves used by other Pokémon. This protection extends to supplementary effects originating from Poison Touch, Toxic Chain, Stench, King's Rock, and Razor Fang. For instance, a Pokémon benefiting from Shield Dust won't be frozen by Blizzard's secondary effect or made to flinch by Fake Out. Crucially, Shield Dust does not influence status moves (e.g., Thunder Wave, Leech Seed) or any Max Moves. Additionally, the Ability offers no safeguard against additional effects that target the move's user; thus, a Pokémon employing Flame Charge will still experience its Speed stat increase, even if its opponent has Shield Dust.

A unique interaction exists with Sparkling Aria: Shield Dust prevents a Pokémon's burn status from being removed *if* that Pokémon is the sole target of the move (this applies even in formats like Double Battles, as long as Sparkling Aria hits no other Pokémon). Conversely, if Sparkling Aria affects multiple Pokémon, all of them will have their burn cured, even those with Shield Dust. (It should be noted that Wake-Up Slap and Smelling Salts, despite curing status ailments, are not categorized as having secondary effects and are consequently not impacted by Shield Dust.) Such an interaction is unique to Sparkling Aria; for instance, Shield Dust consistently blocks paralysis from Discharge, irrespective of the number of targets.

The Ability Shield Dust was confirmed for Yellow's Caterpie, Kitty, and Green's Weedle. Effectively, Shield Dust grants immunity to the Freeze status in battle against any Pokémon (excluding those with Mold Breaker or its equivalents), given that the Freeze condition is exclusively a secondary effect of an opponent's move and cannot be relayed via Baton Pass or Synchronize. Correspondingly, Shield Dust safeguards the Pokémon from flinching, a state that also consistently originates as a secondary effect of an opponent's attack. All Pokémon that are able to have Shield Dust as an Ability share the Bug typing. Within any three-stage evolutionary family possessing Shield Dust, the middle evolution's Ability is replaced by Shed Skin. Significantly, these intermediate evolutionary forms are all thematically linked to cocoons or, in Spewpa's instance, cased larvae.

Title: Own Tempo (Ability)

Known in Japanese as マイペース (My Pace), Own Tempo debuted as an Ability in Generation III. This Ability renders the Pokémon immune to confusion, including self-inflicted instances from moves such as Thrash or items like the Iapapa Berry. Should a Pokémon possessing Own Tempo become confused during a battle (e.g., by acquiring the Ability via an effect like Skill Swap, or being confused by an opponent with Mold Breaker), the confusion will be immediately alleviated. Should an Own Tempo Pokémon become confused via Baton Pass, it will be instantly cured upon entry. If a Pokémon with Trace is sent into battle via Baton Pass and is confused as a result, if it gains Own Tempo via Trace it will not be cured of confusion until after a Pokémon takes its turn (uses a move, switches out, etc.). When a Pokémon with Own Tempo is confused while holding a Persim, Lum, or Touga Berry, Own Tempo's effect will activate and remove the confusion prior to the Berry being consumed. Conversely, if a Tracing Pokémon enters the fray via Baton Pass and is confused, acquiring Own Tempo through Trace will result in an immediate cure. Exclusively in Generation IV, if a confused Pokémon also has an active status condition, and Own Tempo resolves the confusion, the status condition icon will vanish from its HP bar for the duration of its presence in battle. Nevertheless, the underlying status condition will remain functionally active. In a different scenario, if an Own Tempo Pokémon receives confusion through Baton Pass, it will not be healed until after a Pokémon completes its turn (e.g., uses a move, switches out). Should an Own Tempo Pokémon be confused while clutching a Persim or Lum Berry, the Berry's effect will precede Own Tempo's confusion-curing properties. Rockruff possessing Own Tempo is regarded as a distinct form, uniquely able to evolve into Dusk Form Lycanroc. Neither an Ability Capsule nor an Ability Patch can alter Rockruff's Ability to or from Own Tempo. In accordance with breeding's form inheritance regulations, if a female Rockruff with Own Tempo or a Dusk Form Lycanroc is the parent, or if either is bred with Ditto, the offspring will consistently be Rockruff with Own Tempo. As this form, alongside Dusk Form Lycanroc, was introduced in Pokémon Ultra Sun and Ultra Moon, it is incompatible for trading to Pokémon Sun and Moon or through Wonder Trade. In the narrative of Rayquaza Redemption I, Norman's Spinda demonstrated Own Tempo as its Ability, though it was subsequently Skill Swapped for his Slaking's Truant. During Well Met, Weepinbell, Diamond's Lickilicky, named Kit, was shown to possess Own Tempo, but its activation was thwarted by Sebastian's Weepinbell's Gastro Acid. A Generation IV glitch caused the icon for a non-volatile status condition to vanish from a Pokémon's HP bar in battle if Own Tempo cured its confusion; despite this visual disappearance, the icon persisted in the party menu and would reappear upon switching out and returning. This peculiar behavior also applies to infatuation and the Oblivious Ability.

Title: Suction Cups (Ability)

The Ability known as Suction Cups (Japanese: きゅうばん Suction Cups) was first introduced in Generation III. Its effect prevents opposing Pokémon from compelling the holder to withdraw or escape combat through specific moves like Roar or Circle Throw, or via held items such as the Red Card. However, Suction Cups does not interfere with the Pokémon's own voluntary withdrawal, nor with its self-initiated switching or fleeing actions triggered by its own moves (for instance, Baton Pass, Teleport) or items (like an Eject Button). This protection applies even if the Pokémon is a Ghost-type, or if it is equipped with a Shed Shell or Smoke Ball; it remains immune to being forcibly switched out or made to flee. Should the Pokémon be under the influence of Ingrain, Suction Cups' preventative power overrides Ingrain's typical restriction if an attempt is made to compel it to switch or flee. When a Pokémon possessing Suction Cups lands an attack on a target equipped with a Red Card, the item will still activate and be used up, yet it will be ineffective at forcing the target Pokémon to switch out or flee. Beginning with Pokémon Emerald, if a Pokémon with Suction Cups occupies the lead position in the player's roster (even if it has fainted), the frequency of bites during fishing endeavors will increase. Sticky Hold offers an identical effect. An oversight in Pokémon Diamond, Pearl, and Platinum caused this Ability to have no functional impact outside of combat within those titles. This characteristic was preserved in Pokémon Brilliant Diamond and Shining Pearl, where the Ability similarly lacks an out-of-battle effect. Exclusively in Pokémon HeartGold and SoulSilver, when a Pokémon featuring Suction Cups successfully breaks a rock, the probability of discovering an item is boosted by a fixed 5%. Across Red Rescue Team and Blue Rescue Team, Explorers of Time, Darkness and Sky, and the Mystery Dungeon (WiiWare) installments, Pokémon with this Ability gain immunity to being blown away (e.g., by Vital Throw or a Gust Trap), warped (e.g., by a Warp Trap), or compelled to move (e.g., through Baton Pass). In Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, however, while Pokémon with Suction Cups cannot be blown away (e.g., by Roar or a Whirlwind Wand) or warped, they can still be forced into movement (for instance, by Baton Pass or U-turn). Without exception, every Pokémon possessing this Ability falls into one of the designated Water Egg Groups.

Title: Intimidate (Ability)

Known in Japanese as いかく (Intimidate), this Ability made its debut in Generation III. Upon a Pokémon possessing Intimidate entering combat, the Attack stat of all directly opposing Pokémon decreases by a single stage. For Generations III and IV, the notification for Intimidate's activation displayed '<Pokémon>'s Intimidate cuts <opponent>'s attack!'. This Ability fails to impact Pokémon naturally protected against Attack stat debuffs, including those endowed with Clear Body, Hyper Cutter, or White Smoke. Furthermore, it bears no influence on a target protected by a substitute. Intimidate's effect also manifests when a Pokémon acquires this Ability during battle, for instance through Skill Swap or Mega Evolution. Beginning with Generation V, the on-screen message was altered to '<Opponent>'s Attack fell!'. Within the context of a Triple Battle, Intimidate's range does not extend to Pokémon that are not adjacent. Pokémon possessing the Full Metal Body Ability are also immune to Intimidate's influence. When encountering a Pokémon with Intimidate, wild Pokémon exhibit an increased propensity to summon allies for assistance. Pressure and Unnerve are Abilities that share this particular effect. Should a Pokémon equipped with an Adrenaline Orb be subjected to Intimidate's effect, the orb is consumed, subsequently boosting the holder's Speed stat by one stage. Currently, Intimidate's influence does not extend to Pokémon with Oblivious, Own Tempo, Inner Focus, or Scrappy. Furthermore, Intimidate's presence now triggers the Rattled Ability. Conversely, for Pokémon with the Guard Dog Ability, Intimidate causes their Attack stat to rise by one stage, rather than decreasing it. Since Pokémon Emerald, placing a Pokémon with Intimidate in the lead position of the party (even if knocked out) grants a 50% probability of averting a random wild encounter, provided the wild Pokémon's level is at least five levels below that of the Intimidate user. This particular effect is also shared by the Keen Eye Ability. Outside of direct combat, Intimidate seemingly has no discernible impact. Any numerical statistics presented herein represent exact figures, not base stats. Their values are custom-determined rather than being calculated from base stats, level, Nature, IVs, and EVs. If an adjacent opposing Pokémon targets a Pokémon with Intimidate, the strength of its physical moves experiences a 20% decrease. Within 'Pinsir Me, I Must Be Dreaming', Emerald's Pokédex confirmed that Noland's rental Mawile, deployed in his Battle Factory encounter against Emerald, possessed Intimidate as its Ability. In 'Salamence Evolves', Zinnia's Salamence was noted to have Intimidate as its Ability, though its activation was not depicted. Volume 40 disclosed that Pearl's Luxray, Rayler, had Intimidate as its Ability. In volume 42, Crystal's Arcanine, Archy, was confirmed to possess Intimidate as its Ability. Also in volume 42, Silver's Gyarados was shown to have Intimidate as its Ability. The PokéSPedia data-book documented Blue's Arcanine, Ruby's Mightyena (Nana), X's Mega Manectric (Élec), and Lysandre's Gyarados as all having Intimidate as their Ability. Additionally, Lance's Gyarados, Winona's Masquerain, Spenser's Salamence, Lenora's Stoutland, and the Staravia employed by Team Galactic Grunts were also identified with Intimidate as their Ability. Before its establishment as an Ability, Intimidate existed as an Attack within the Pokémon Trading Card Game.

Title: Shadow Tag (Ability)

The Ability known as Shadow Tag (in Japanese, かげふみ, translating to "Stepping on Shadows") first appeared in Generation III. Before the advent of Generation V, Wynaut and Wobbuffet uniquely possessed this Ability. When a Pokémon with Shadow Tag is active in battle, it stops all neighboring adversaries from escaping (even via Teleport) or swapping out. Nevertheless, should a Pokémon carrying Shadow Tag enter the battle, opposing Pokémon retain the ability to switch out during that specific turn. Moves such as Baton Pass, U-turn, Volt Switch, Flip Turn, or Parting Shot will still successfully allow the user to depart from the battle. A Pokémon equipped with the Run Away Ability or holding a Smoke Ball is able to flee or Teleport, yet cannot switch. Should a player opt to flee rather than replace a fainted Pokémon, a wild Pokémon possessing Shadow Tag will not impede their escape. Subsequently, Shadow Tag now grants its user immunity to the trapping effects of another Pokémon's Shadow Tag. A Pokémon holding a Shed Shell gains the freedom to switch out, though not to flee or Teleport. Currently, Ghost-type Pokémon are unaffected by Shadow Tag's trapping mechanism. Within Pokémon X and Y, Shadow Tag proves ineffective against roaming Pokémon, as they depart prior to the commencement of battle. For games in Generation V, Litwick, Lampent, and Chandelure were listed with Shadow Tag as their Hidden Ability; however, acquiring them with it legitimately was not possible. Their designated Hidden Ability transitioned to Infiltrator in Generation VI. Across Red Rescue Team and Blue Rescue Team, alongside Explorers of Time, Darkness and Sky, if a Pokémon bearing this Ability takes damage, there exists a 12% probability that the assailant will become immobilized. In Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, Shadow Tag renders all adjacent Pokémon immobile, though they retain the capacity to execute moves or utilize items. Its effects are nullified against Ghost-type Pokémon, as well as those possessing the Run Away or Shadow Tag Ability. Mirei's Wobbuffet is confirmed to possess Shadow Tag as its Ability.

Title: Rough Skin (Ability)

The Ability known as Rough Skin (さめはだ, or Rough Skin, in Japanese) made its debut in Generation III. Prior to Generation V, it served as the unique signature Ability for Carvanha and Sharpedo. By Generation IX, it became the exclusive signature Ability of the Gible evolutionary family. If a Pokémon possessing this Ability is struck by a contact-making move, the assailant Pokémon suffers damage equivalent to 1/16 of its total HP. In cases where a multistrike contact move (like Fury Swipes) connects with a Pokémon having Rough Skin, the Ability triggers for every individual hit. Should a contact move inflict zero damage upon the Rough Skin Pokémon (such as when the user employs Endure at 1 HP), the Ability will fail to activate. Subsequently, for a Pokémon with this Ability that is struck by a contact move, the attacking Pokémon will incur damage equal to 1/8 of its own maximum HP. Should Rough Skin result in both the Ability-bearer and its attacker being knocked out simultaneously, the attacking Pokémon is registered as fainting prior to the Rough Skin Pokémon. In contrast to earlier rules, the Ability will activate when a Pokémon possessing it is hit by a contact move, even if that move inflicts 0 damage.

Within the realm of Red Rescue Team and Blue Rescue Team, Explorers of Time, Darkness and Sky, and the Mystery Dungeon (WiiWare) titles, if an adjacent Pokémon attacks a Rough Skin user, the assailant might receive damage equal to half of the damage it inflicted. Commencing with Gates to Infinity, Rough Skin instead inflicts damage on the attacking Pokémon equal to 1/10 of its maximum HP. Should a Pokémon with this Ability be struck by a contact-based multistrike move (like Fury Swipes), the effect triggers on every single hit.

In Pokémon UNITE, Rough Skin functions as Garchomp's unique Ability. When Garchomp is subjected to a melee assault, a fraction of the incoming damage is reflected back to the aggressor. This specific activation is subject to a 2-second cooldown period, and factors in any defense reductions. The PokéSPedia data-book confirmed that Archie's Sharpedo possessed Rough Skin as its Ability. The Japanese name for Sharpedo, "サメハダー" (Samehader), shares its etymology directly with Rough Skin's Japanese name, "さめはだ" (Samehada), both signifying 'shark skin'.

Title: Wonder Guard (Ability)

Known in Japanese as ふしぎなまもり (Mysterious Protection), Wonder Guard is an Ability that made its debut in Generation III. It is exclusively possessed by Shedinja. Any Pokémon bearing Wonder Guard cannot be harmed by damaging attacks unless they are super effective against it, with Struggle being the sole exception. Furthermore, the ability nullifies damage from attacks whose power isn't scaled by type matchups, encompassing direct damage attacks, retaliatory moves, and one-hit knockout techniques. As an illustration, a Bug/Ghost-type Shedinja employing Wonder Guard would shrug off Metal Burst (Steel-type), Psywave (Psychic-type), and Fissure (Ground-type), yet would still be susceptible to Night Shade (Ghost-type). Wonder Guard offers no defense against non-damaging status moves, like Tail Whip or Poison Powder. It also fails to prevent passive damage sources, including burns, poison, confusion, weather effects, recoil, or entry hazards.

Certain moves and Abilities are capable of circumventing Wonder Guard's protection, specifically those designed to disregard other Abilities, such as Sunsteel Strike and Mold Breaker. Trace can replicate Wonder Guard, and a Pokémon can gain it through a transformation via Transform or Imposter. Apart from these methods, no other legitimate means exist for a Pokémon other than Shedinja to possess Wonder Guard. Role Play will fail when used on a target with Wonder Guard, and Skill Swap is ineffective if either participant has it. Similarly, Power of Alchemy and Receiver are unable to copy Wonder Guard, and Wandering Spirit will not facilitate an Ability swap with it.

In strong winds, a Flying-type Pokémon with Wonder Guard will suffer no damage if the attack is not super effective against its secondary type. A Pokémon that has become typeless (for example, a pure Fire-type after using Burn Up) will, with Wonder Guard, gain immunity to all damaging moves, irrespective of their type. Previously, damage without a specific type (typeless damage) was not blocked by Wonder Guard; thus, moves like Beat Up, Future Sight, and Doom Desire bypassed it in earlier games due to their typeless nature. Weather Ball, when unleashed in a shadowy aura, also inflicted typeless damage and therefore ignored Wonder Guard. Shadow moves similarly ignored Wonder Guard's defense, regardless of their type effectiveness. A glitch once allowed Fire Fang to strike through Wonder Guard, even if the target held no Fire-type weakness.

Within Pokémon Emerald, if an NPC trainer's active Pokémon possesses no moves capable of piercing the player's Wonder Guard, the AI will prompt a switch to another Pokémon within their party that does have at least one such move, provided one exists. Should the NPC's entire roster lack a Wonder Guard-bypassing attack, no switch will occur. Presently, typeless damage is now prevented by Wonder Guard, with Struggle remaining the only exception. Furthermore, Fire Fang no longer possesses the ability to bypass Wonder Guard.

In Pokémon Rumble World, Shedinja's Wonder Guard provides a random chance of complete invulnerability to attacks that aren't super effective. Here, Wonder Guard bestows immunity to typed damaging moves that aren't super effective. It does not mitigate passive damage like burns, weather, or recoil. Moreover, it offers no defense against other forms of typeless damage, including traps, thrown items, or wands. Damage from standard attacks is instead reduced to a mere 1 HP, unless the attacker is afflicted with the Set Damage status. Regarding Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, standard attacks have absolutely no impact on a Pokémon with Wonder Guard.

Greta's Shedinja was noted to possess Wonder Guard in "Sneaky Like Shedinja," while Crystal's Shedinja was also shown to have this Ability. Before the advent of the Fairy-type in Generation VI, the Dark/Ghost type pairing lacked any inherent weaknesses. When paired with Wonder Guard, this combination rendered a Pokémon impervious to all direct offensive actions. This unique trait was highlighted in-game by the Pokéstar Studios foe, Majin, whose defeat necessitated players to inflict indirect damage or alter its Ability or typing.

Multiple strategies exist to create a Wonder Guard Pokémon with no weaknesses: For instance, if a pure Electric-type or Poison/Dark-type Pokémon gains Wonder Guard (perhaps via Shedinja using Reflect Type or Entrainment through Mimic on such a Pokémon) and carries an Air Balloon, it achieves immunity to all eighteen Pokémon types. Nevertheless, Thousand Arrows can still strike it, and if the Pokémon loses its grounded status, it becomes vulnerable to any Ground-type move. Alternatively, if a pure Fire-type Pokémon uses Burn Up to shed its type and then acquires Wonder Guard (e.g., through Shedinja's Entrainment via Mimic), Wonder Guard will nullify all damaging moves, as typeless Pokémon inherently possess no weaknesses. Notably, Wonder Guard stands alone as the mechanism capable of rendering a Pokémon entirely immune to Flying, Rock, Bug, Steel, Ice, Dark, or Fairy-type moves.

Title: Levitate (Ability)

Introduced in Generation III, the Ability known as Levitate (Japanese: ふゆう, meaning Floating) grants the Pokémon possessing it immunity to damaging Ground-type moves, with the singular exception of Thousand Arrows. Furthermore, it renders the Pokémon "ungrounded," thereby neutralizing the effects of Arena Trap, Spikes, Toxic Spikes, Sticky Web, Rototiller, and various terrain conditions. An additional benefit is that Poison-type Pokémon with Levitate will not clear Toxic Spikes when switching into battle. These immunities, however, are lost under several circumstances: during the move Gravity, if the Pokémon holds an Iron Ball, is affected by Ingrain, or has been hit by either Smack Down or Thousand Arrows. Pokémon endowed with this Ability are generally eligible for Sky Battles; Gengar is the only exception, as its model touches the ground upon entering the fight. Before Generation VII, Gengar's Ability was Levitate instead of Cursed Body. In Pokémon UNITE, Levitate also functions as Gengar's Ability, increasing its movement speed by 10% for 8 seconds after it disengages from combat. Iono's Mismagius was noted to possess Levitate in PASV12. Numerous Pokémon have been observed to have Levitate as their Ability, including Koga's Weezing and Koffing, Agatha's Gengar, Morty's Misdreavus and Gastly, Wally's Flygon, Tate's Solrock, Liza's Lunatone, Fantina's Mismagius and Duskull, Palmer's Cresselia, Brycen's Cryogonal, Ghetsis's Hydreigon and Eelektross, Buck's Claydol, a collective of Unown controlled by Sird, Latios, Latias, Giratina in its Origin Forme, and Hugh's Eelektross. In PBK4, Yū's Heatran acquired Levitate after his Bronzong performed Skill Swap on it. Within the Pokémon Trading Card Game, Levitate first appeared as a Poké-BODY on Vibrava from the EX Dragon expansion. Any Pokémon with Levitate, whether as a Poké-BODY or an Ability, gains a zero Retreat Cost provided it has Energy attached. At its debut on EX Dragon Vibrava, this Poké-BODY specifically required Basic Energy cards, not just any Energy cards, to activate. A comprehensive listing of Pokémon cards featuring the Levitate Poké-BODY or Ability is available. Interestingly, many non-Flying-type Pokémon capable of floating or flying, such as Beedrill or Miraidon, cannot possess this Ability. Despite game descriptions from Generation IV onward stating that Levitate provides immunity to *all* Ground-type moves, Pokémon with Levitate are still affected by the status move Sand Attack. Fan Rotom is unique among Levitate users, being the only one naturally immune to Ground-type moves due to its Flying-type designation from Generation V onwards. This Ability is the most frequently encountered among winning Video Game World Championships teams. It was the most prevalent Ability in Generation VII, appearing in 40 different instances, which accounts for Mega Evolutions and alternate forms. It is also the most common Ability to be a Pokémon's exclusive option. Levitate stands alone as the only Ability that grants immunity to an attack type without restoring HP or altering the Pokémon's stats or damage output when hit by that attack.

Title: Effect Spore (Ability)

Known in Japanese as ほうし (Spore), Effect Spore is an Ability that made its debut in Generation III. Should a Pokémon possessing this Ability be struck by a move that makes contact, the attacking foe has a 10% likelihood of experiencing one of three status conditions: poison, paralysis, or sleep, each with an individual 3.3% chance. In situations where a Pokémon carrying this Ability sustains damage from a contact-based multistrike attack (such as Fury Swipes), every individual strike carries its own distinct probability of triggering Effect Spore. Should Effect Spore induce sleep in the user of such a multi-hit technique, the move's sequence is instantly terminated. If an opponent is already resistant to certain status effects, the probability of applying each of the remaining possible conditions remains unchanged, leading to an overall reduction in the total success rate for inflicting any condition. When a Pokémon with this Ability is struck by a contact move, there is a 30% chance that the attacking Pokémon will become either poisoned, paralyzed, or asleep, with a 10% probability each. Furthermore, some documentation details specific probabilities for these status ailments when hit by a contact move: a 9% chance of poison, a 10% chance of paralysis, and an 11% chance of sleep for the attacking Pokémon. Currently, Grass-type creatures, those possessing the Overcoat Ability, and Pokémon equipped with Safety Goggles are immune to the effects of Effect Spore. The Ability will not trigger if the Pokémon endowed with it is rendered unconscious by the incoming attack.

Effect Spore featured prominently within the narrative of The Power of Us film. In that story, Team Rocket sought to plunder Fula City's Pokémon Research Pavilion, succeeding only in absconding with a solution of Effect Spore concocted by Toren. Subsequently, their accidental dropping of the solution liberated a noxious gas, which then ignited, causing a catastrophic blaze. These two calamities posed a grave threat to Fula City, yet Ash, alongside the city's inhabitants, united to overcome them. According to the PokéSPedia data-book, Whitley's Foongus, named Foongy, possesses Effect Spore as his designated Ability. Notably, Effect Spore stands alone as the sole Ability capable of inducing the sleep status condition.

Title: Synchronize (Ability)

Known in Japanese as シンクロ (Synchro), Synchronize is an Ability first appearing in Generation III. Should a Pokémon possessing Synchronize become burned, paralyzed, or poisoned by an opposing Pokémon, the instigator of that status condition will, in turn, suffer from the identical ailment. However, Synchronize cannot impose confusion, sleep, or freeze. It will still trigger (thereby revealing the Pokémon's Ability), even if it ultimately has no impact because the target already has a status condition, or due to its type, Ability, or a team effect (such as Safeguard). This Ability is capable of affecting a Pokémon even when protected by a substitute. While certain Abilities of other Pokémon, like Static, can cause Synchronize to activate, items such as the Toxic Orb or Flame Orb cannot. If a Synchronize-user holds a Berry that remedies the status condition just applied, Synchronize will take effect prior to the Berry's consumption. In instances where a Pokémon with Synchronize is afflicted with bad poison by an adversary, that adversary will instead receive the standard poison condition.

When Psycho Shift is employed by a burned, paralyzed, or poisoned Pokémon against a Synchronize-bearer, Synchronize will activate after Psycho Shift has removed the status from the user. Consequently, Synchronize will re-inflict the status condition in this scenario. If a single move, like Discharge, afflicts multiple Pokémon with a status ailment (including via Synchronize itself), Pokémon receiving the status in even-numbered positions (second, potentially fourth) cannot activate their own Synchronize, even if they possess it. Toxic Spikes generally do not trigger this Ability, unless a Pokémon on the field directly forces the Synchronize-user to switch in, through moves like Roar or Whirlwind.

As of a later change, if a Pokémon with Synchronize is badly poisoned by an opponent, that opponent will now similarly suffer from bad poison, instead of regular poison. Moreover, if a burned, paralyzed, poisoned, or badly poisoned Pokémon uses Psycho Shift against a Synchronize-user, Synchronize now triggers before Psycho Shift removes the ailment from the attacker. This means Synchronize will consequently fail to re-inflict the status condition in such circumstances. In situations where multiple Pokémon receive a status from a single move, Synchronize now has the chance to activate for any Pokémon possessing it, irrespective of the order in which they were afflicted. Synchronize can also now trigger another Pokémon's Synchronize; however, if this occurs, the initial Pokémon is already statused, so it will only result in an Ability message with no further consequence. Furthermore, Toxic Spikes no longer activate this Ability, even if another Pokémon compelled the Synchronize-bearer to switch in using a move such as Roar or Dragon Tail.

Within SOS Battles, should a Synchronize Pokémon occupy the lead party slot when a wild Pokémon summons an ally, there is a 50% probability that the summoned ally will be compelled to share the Nature of the Pokémon originally in the first slot at the start of the battle (this lead Pokémon is not necessarily the Synchronize user itself). Starting with Pokémon Emerald, if a Synchronize-wielder is placed in the party's first position (even if it has fainted) during a wild Pokémon encounter, there's a 50% likelihood the game will ensure the wild Pokémon possesses the same Nature as that lead Pokémon. (Otherwise, Nature is random, retaining a 1-in-25 chance of matching the lead.) This specific effect does not extend to roaming Pokémon, those found in the Pokéwalker, or creatures in the Entree Forest. In Emerald exclusively, it also excludes stationary Pokémon like Regirock or Rayquaza.

Commencing with Pokémon Omega Ruby and Alpha Sapphire, when a Synchronize Pokémon is at the head of the party, gift Pokémon (excluding those from Mystery Gift and Eggs) belonging to the No Eggs Discovered Egg Group will invariably match the Nature of the Ability-holder. Exclusively in Pokémon Ultra Sun and Ultra Moon, if Synchronize leads the party, gift Pokémon (excluding Mystery Gift acquisitions and Eggs) will always exhibit the same Nature as the Pokémon with this Ability, irrespective of their Egg Group. Within Pokémon Sword and Shield, having a Synchronize Pokémon at the forefront of the party guarantees that wild Pokémon will possess the same Nature as the lead (disregarding Mints). This rule doesn't cover static encounters (like Zacian, Calyrex, or the legendary giants) but is effective for wandering and roaming Pokémon (such as the Swords of Justice and Galarian legendary birds). However, Pokémon found in Max Raid Battles or received as gifts remain unaffected by Synchronize. In Pokémon Brilliant Diamond and Shining Pearl, bringing a Synchronize Pokémon as the leader into a Pokémon Hideaway ensures all wild Pokémon encountered within will share the leader's Nature. However, in Pokémon Scarlet and Violet, Synchronize ceases to influence the Nature of wild Pokémon.

For the Mystery Dungeon series, specifically Red Rescue Team, Blue Rescue Team, and the Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky titles: if the user becomes paralyzed, burned, poisoned, or badly poisoned, it will subsequently inflict all adjacent foes with that same status condition (if permissible). Notably, bad poison transfers as bad poison, a distinction from core series games before Generation V.

In Pokémon UNITE, Synchronize functions as Gardevoir's Ability. When an opposing combatant applies a movement speed reduction or a damage-over-time effect to Gardevoir, Synchronize retaliates by inflicting the identical effect upon that opponent. This effect initiates a 9-second cooldown upon activation. Synchronize is additionally Mew's Ability, though its effect differs. Mew's Synchronize boosts the movement speed of Mew and proximate allied Pokémon by 15% each time Mew utilizes a move. This speed enhancement persists for two seconds.

According to the PokéSPedia data-book, Red's Espeon, Vee, and Green's Abra were confirmed to possess Synchronize as their Ability. Sabrina's Alakazam, Greta's Umbreon, and Mew were also shown to have Synchronize as their Ability.

Title: Clear Body (Ability)

This power, known in Japan as クリアボディ Clear Body, made its debut in Generation III. It serves to block decreases in stats originating from an opposing Pokémon's maneuvers or innate powers, such as the effects of Scary Face or Intimidate, and notably includes the impact of Sticky Web. However, it offers no protection against self-imposed stat declines, typical of moves like Superpower or Shell Smash. Its protective scope is limited solely to preventing reductions in stat *stages*; consequently, it cannot negate diminished stats resulting from held items such as the Iron Ball, status ailments like paralysis or burn, nor the effects of Guard Split or Power Split. Furthermore, this Ability provides no defense against direct alterations to stat stages enacted by Heart Swap, Guard Swap, Power Swap, Baton Pass, or Topsy-Turvy. Nor does it impede Spectral Thief's capacity to seize (effectively resetting) beneficial stat enhancements. Actions and special powers designed to bypass Abilities, such as Mold Breaker, will disregard Clear Body entirely. Several Pokémon have been shown possessing Clear Body: Crystal's Metagross, Steven's Metagross, Beldum, Metang, Brandon's Regirock, Registeel, and Regice, along with a Diancie under Ruby's command. During the events of A Novel Test!!, Candice's Regice and Registeel were both noted to possess Clear Body, though no actual activation of the Ability was depicted.

Title: Natural Cure (Ability)

Referred to in Japanese as しぜんかいふく (Natural Recovery), this Ability made its debut in Generation III. It effectively remedies any status conditions afflicting the Pokémon when it is switched out. This curative effect takes place only if Natural Cure is the active, unsuppressed Ability during the battle; for example, a Pokémon that acquired Natural Cure via Trace will have its status condition healed upon switching out, unlike a Pokémon possessing Natural Cure that has been impacted by Gastro Acid. Adversary trainers will deliberately withdraw a Natural Cure user to cure it only when the Pokémon is asleep. Beyond in-battle swaps, Natural Cure previously cleared status conditions upon the conclusion of a battle (if the Pokémon was active at the end), though this particular effect is no longer active. Nonetheless, from Generation IV through Generation VII, Natural Cure consistently provided automatic status healing once a battle ended.

Within Pokémon UNITE, Natural Cure serves as the unique Ability for both Blissey and Trevenant. Blissey's variant causes the user to shed all debuffs every 6 seconds, a process that initiates even if Blissey currently has no active debuffs. Trevenant's interpretation of Natural Cure, conversely, restores the user's HP every 0.5 seconds for a duration of 4 seconds once its health drops below 35%; concurrently, it decreases the cooldowns of the user's moves by 1 second per second. Following the conclusion of this HP restoration, Trevenant's Ability enters a 100-second cooldown period, which can be shortened by 1 second each time the user lands a hit on an opponent with any of its moves, though this reduction cannot occur more frequently than every 0.5 seconds.

The animated film "The Power of Us" depicted an effort by Toren and his scientific colleagues at the Pokémon Research Pavilion to synthesize an elixir from Natural Cure, amidst two concurrent catastrophes in Fula City. This endeavor was interrupted by a power outage before the formula's completion, compelling Toren to adapt by utilizing Team Rocket's Lum Berries instead. In the manga chapter "Moving Past Milotic," Crystal's Starmie was shown to possess Natural Cure as its Ability. Additional Pokémon confirmed to have this Ability include Winona's Altaria and Swablu, Marley's Land Forme Shaymin, and Celebi.

In Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, the Ability is presented as Natural Recovery, directly reflecting its Japanese namesake. Despite Natural Cure lacking an explicit activation message, its presence can be inferred by observing the user interface, which indicates the number of status conditions afflicting the opponent's Pokémon. This deduction is possible the subsequent moment the Poké Balls symbolizing the opponent's active party are visually updated. Specifically, during Generation III, this occurs when the opponent switches Pokémon. For Generation IV, visibility is granted both upon an opponent's Pokémon switch and on the move selection screen. However, from Generation V onwards, this information is exclusively discernible on the move selection screen.

Title: Lightning Rod (Ability)

Lightning Rod, known in Japanese as ひらいしん (Lightning Rod), made its debut in Generation III. Before Pokémon X and Y, its name was styled as Lightningrod. This Ability's primary function is to compel all single-target Electric-type moves to target the Pokémon that possesses it. However, if another Pokémon becomes the focus of attention, Electric-type attacks will be redirected to that Pokémon instead. In Triple Battles, Lightning Rod is unable to divert Electric-type moves if the Pokémon is positioned outside of the attack's range. Initially, Electric-type Hidden Power, Judgment, and Natural Gift could not be redirected by Lightning Rod. Electric-type moves, whether from foes or allies, will invariably hit the Pokémon with this Ability, unless the Pokémon is undergoing the semi-invulnerable phase of moves like Dig or Fly. An exception exists for Thunder, which will always connect with a Pokémon in the semi-invulnerable turns of Fly or Bounce.

From Generation V onwards, Lightning Rod gained expanded capabilities. It now confers complete immunity to all Electric-type moves and, each time the Pokémon is struck by an Electric-type attack, its Special Attack stat is boosted by one stage. Electric-type multi-strike moves, such as those influenced by Electrify or affected by Parental Bond, will only trigger this Special Attack increase once. Should the Pokémon be protected from the move or inherently immune due to being a Ground-type, its Special Attack will not rise. If a Pokémon with Lightning Rod holds a Cell Battery and an Electric-type move targets it, Lightning Rod absorbs the attack, thus preventing the Cell Battery's effect from activating.

Subsequently, Lightning Rod acquired the capacity to redirect Electric-type Hidden Power, Judgment, and Natural Gift. Nevertheless, it will not divert Sky Drop if that move’s type has been altered to Electric by Electrify. A Ground-type Pokémon equipped with Lightning Rod will now both activate the Ability's effects and receive the Special Attack increment. The Abilities Propeller Tail and Stalwart are capable of bypassing Lightning Rod's targeting mechanism, but they do not negate its Electric-type immunity. In the context of Dynamax Raids, Lightning Rod effectively draws Electric attacks away from allied Pokémon.

Beyond battle, in Pokémon Emerald, Trainers registered with the PokéNav's Match Call feature will contact the player twice as often if a Pokémon with Lightning Rod is placed first in the party, even if it has fainted. In Pokémon Sword and Shield, positioning a Pokémon with Lightning Rod at the party's lead (regardless of its fainted status) results in a 51% chance of forcing an encounter with an Electric-type Pokémon, assuming one is available. This probability stands at 50% in Pokémon Brilliant Diamond and Shining Pearl. The Ability Static shares this specific out-of-battle function. However, in Pokémon Scarlet and Violet, Lightning Rod offers no benefit outside of battle, with Meal Powers having superseded its former field utility.

In Generation V, Zapdos was designated to have Lightning Rod as its Hidden Ability, though it remained unobtainable through legitimate gameplay. In Generation VI, Static replaced Lightning Rod as Zapdos's Hidden Ability.

Within the Mystery Dungeon series, the Ability was titled "Lightningrod" in Red Rescue Team and Blue Rescue Team, as well as Gates to Infinity. In Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky, it was referenced as "LightningRod." Starting with Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, the Ability adopts its current name, "Lightning Rod." In Red Rescue Team and Blue Rescue Team, along with Explorers of Time and Darkness, a Pokémon possessing Lightning Rod would attract and completely nullify all enemy Electric-type attacks across the entire floor.
