Notably, unlike the main series, it could draw in moves targeting multiple Pokémon. In Explorers of Sky, this effect was confined to the current room. From Gates to Infinity onwards, Lightning Rod also began to increase the Pokémon's Special Attack.

In the Pokémon Adventures manga, Crystal's Cubone, Bonee, and Silver's Rhyperior were both revealed to possess Lightning Rod in volume 42. In the anime episode "Riddle Me This," Pikachu managed to defeat Blaine's Rhydon by aiming an Electric attack at its horn, which was depicted as functioning like a lightning rod, seemingly enabling Pikachu to overcome Rhydon's natural immunity to Electric attacks. This contrasts with the main series games, where Lightning Rod did not affect type immunity in Generations III and IV, only nullifying damage from Electric-type moves from Generation V onward. "Tag! We're It...!" accurately portrayed Lightning Rod's effects and the type-effectiveness of Electric-type moves on Ground-types. Similarly, Pikachu's feat in "Electric Shock Showdown!" of insulating itself from Lt. Surge's Raichu's Thunderbolt with its tail mirrors Lightning Rod's Electric attack nullifying effect as of Generation V, the generation where it became Pikachu's evolutionary line's Hidden Ability. Despite these depictions, Pikachu's canonical Ability was later revealed to be Static in "Balance of Power" and has remained consistent thereafter. Prior to its establishment as an Ability, Lightning Rod appeared as an attack in the Pokémon Trading Card Game.

Title: Serene Grace (Ability)

Known in Japanese as てんのめぐみ (Heavenly Blessing), Serene Grace made its debut as an Ability in Generation III. The primary function of Serene Grace is to double the likelihood of a move's additional effects occurring. For instance, Psychic's probability of decreasing the target's Special Defense stat becomes 20%, up from its standard 10%. Similarly, Rock Smash, which normally has a 50% chance of reducing an adversary's Defense by one stage, will now unfailingly do so. The 10% flinch likelihood attributed to holding a King's Rock or Razor Fang is not impacted by Serene Grace. Nonetheless, Serene Grace elevates the 10% flinch probability that King's Rock or Razor Fang confer to 20%.

When combined with the rainbow effect generated by simultaneous use of Fire Pledge and Water Pledge, Serene Grace exhibits a cumulative interaction, resulting in secondary effect probabilities being quadrupled when both enhancements are active. An exception applies to moves with a flinch chance, where this probability is merely doubled a single time. In games ranging from Generation V up to Pokémon Sword and Shield (excluding Pokémon: Let's Go, Pikachu! and Let's Go, Eevee!, where Abilities are absent), a user possessing Serene Grace who employs Charge Beam while their side benefits from the Fire Pledge/Water Pledge rainbow will find Charge Beam's additional effect chance to "overflow" and settle at 24%. It is important to note that Serene Grace does not augment the chances for G-Max Replenish's or G-Max Snooze's secondary effects.

A comprehensive list is provided below, detailing moves influenced by Serene Grace, alongside the Pokémon capable of possessing this Ability and learning those specific moves. Even though the subsequent moves are impacted by Serene Grace, no Pokémon with the Ability can currently learn them natively. Nonetheless, these moves can be accessed through techniques like Metronome, Mirror Move, Copycat, or Mimic, or executed by a Pokémon that acquired Serene Grace via Trace or Abilities/moves like Skill Swap or Role Play. Exclusively within Pokémon HeartGold and SoulSilver, when a Serene Grace Pokémon shatters a rock and yields an item, the game will select the item positioned immediately below it on the internal item list, unless the 5% rarity item was initially chosen. This mechanism ensures that the most frequently occurring item is never discovered. It was disclosed that Marley's Sky Forme Shaymin possesses Serene Grace as its designated Ability.

Title: Swift Swim (Ability)

The Ability known as Swift Swim (Japanese: すいすい, Smooth Swim) made its debut in Generation III. While rain is active, the Speed stat of any Pokémon equipped with this Ability becomes doubled. Within Pokémon Legends: Arceus's internal code, Basculegion's designated Ability is Rattled, rather than Swift Swim. If the Pokémon employs a special move during rainy weather, that move will be executed twice. The subsequent performance does not consume any additional PP. Should the Pokémon possess a speed bonus, only the initial usage of the move will gain the benefit of Swift Swim. In instances where the special move forms part of a linked move, all moves in the link are performed once before the entire sequence is repeated a second time.

Volume 25 disclosed that Yellow's Omastar, Omny, possessed Swift Swim as his Ability. Later, in volume 40, Pearl's Buizel, Zeller, was similarly shown to have Swift Swim as his Ability. Volume 42 then revealed Silver's Kingdra to also feature Swift Swim as her Ability. According to the PokéSPedia data-book, both Emerald's Mantine and N's Tympole were confirmed to possess Swift Swim as their Ability. Additional Pokémon identified with Swift Swim as their Ability include Ruby's Feebas (Feefee), Wallace's Luvdisc (Elizabeth), Crasher Wake's Floatzel, Clay's Palpitoad, Ghetsis's Seismitoad, and Mr. Briney's Huntail. Uniquely, Anorith and Armaldo are the sole Pokémon possessing the Swift Swim Ability who also bear a weakness to Water.

Title: Chlorophyll (Ability)

Known in Japanese as ようりょくそ (Chlorophyll), this Ability made its debut in Generation III. When exposed to harsh sunlight, any Pokémon possessing Chlorophyll will see their Speed stat increase twofold. Furthermore, under Sunny weather conditions, a Pokémon is capable of deploying an attack twice within a single turn, consuming only one PP. This dual attack also extends to any linked moves. Solar Beam, which becomes a single-turn attack in harsh sunlight, will similarly be performed twice. Confirmed instances of Chlorophyll include Gold's Sunflora, Sunbo, as documented in volume 42. The PokéSPedia data-book further attributes this Ability to Blue's Exeggutor, Sapphire's Tropius (Troppy), and White's Deerling (Darlene). Additional Pokémon confirmed with Chlorophyll are Red's Victreebel and Weepinbell, Erika's Vileplume and Bellossom, Gardenia's Cherubi, a Team Plasma Grunt's Maractus, and the Bellsprout owned by the Sages. Excluding Scovillain, every Pokémon possessing this Ability holds a weakness to Fire-type attacks, whose potency is amplified during harsh sunlight. Within Pokémon the Series, all instances of this Ability being used have been by Pokémon owned by a Grass-type Gym Leader.

Title: Illuminate (Ability)

Known in Japanese as はっこう (Luminescence), Illuminate is an Ability that first appeared in Generation III. As of Pokémon Scarlet and Violet's 2.0.1 update, Illuminate bestows a significant combat advantage: it protects the user from accuracy stat stage reductions inflicted by opposing Pokémon and simultaneously disregards the target's evasion stat stages. However, effects that reduce accuracy without involving stat stages, like Wonder Skin and Sand Veil, are not nullified by Illuminate. Abilities such as Mold Breaker, Teravolt, and Turboblaze can bypass Illuminate's effects. Before Scarlet and Violet's 2.0.1 patch, Illuminate lacked any battle functionality, despite its descriptive text being updated in version 1.3.2. This discrepancy went unnoticed in gameplay, though, as no Pokémon possessing Illuminate were obtainable in Scarlet and Violet during that period.

When a Pokémon with Illuminate occupies the lead position in the party (even if unconscious), the frequency of wild Pokémon encounters doubles (a 100% increase). This property is shared with the Abilities Arena Trap and No Guard. The encounter rate influenced by Rock Smash is not impacted by Illuminate. Notably, in Pokémon HeartGold and SoulSilver, Illuminate *does* boost the Rock Smash encounter rate. Within Pokémon Sword and Shield, Illuminate exclusively influences the occurrence frequency of random encounters. Curiously, Illuminate seemingly has no discernible effect on the general encounter rate of wild Pokémon.

In games predating Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, an Illuminate-wielding Pokémon would visibly "blink" following any turn where it took damage from an offensive move. This "blink" action results in a hostile Pokémon appearing on that floor. Should the floor's Pokémon capacity be met (15 Pokémon, encompassing ungenerated Monster Houses, but excluding mission objectives or Kecleon), Illuminate will fail to activate. Specifically in Gates to Infinity, Illuminate remains dormant if an attack inflicts zero damage owing to inadequate attack power; however, the Pokémon will still perform the visible blink animation even at floor capacity, unless a Monster House is already active. Within Super Mystery Dungeon, an illuminating move bestows the Decoy status upon the Pokémon that uses it. The Decoy status compels enemies to prioritize attacking that Pokémon over its allies.

A subsequent table enumerates both the moves influenced by Illuminate and the Pokémon capable of possessing Illuminate while also learning those specific moves. Certain Pokémon, such as Misty's Starmie and Chinchou, and a Team Plasma Grunt's Watchog, were explicitly shown to have Illuminate as their designated Ability. Universally, Pokémon that can manifest this Ability are also capable of mastering Flash and Confuse Ray.

Title: Trace (Ability)

Known in Japanese as トレース Trace, this Pokémon Ability was first introduced in Generation III. Its function is to duplicate the Ability of a randomly chosen adjacent opposing Pokémon, thereby replacing Trace itself. This activation occurs when the Pokémon possessing Trace enters combat or when it newly acquires Trace. Within Wonder Launcher battles, an ally's Ability Urge can prompt Trace's activation. Skill Swap is capable of exchanging Trace. In Generations III and IV, Role Play could replicate Trace, yet in later generations, Trace cannot be copied by Role Play, Power of Alchemy, or Receiver. Entrainment, if utilized by a Pokémon with Trace, will invariably fail. Trace is capable of copying any Ability, but if it happens to duplicate Trace, it will not re-activate. A number of Abilities now exist that Trace is unable to copy. Should a Pokémon with Trace be engaged in battle, if at any point an opponent possesses an Ability amenable to copying by Trace, Trace will activate immediately, taking on that Ability. Trace can only be activated once per instance of a Pokémon being on the field; even if the Pokémon obtains Trace anew (for example, through Skill Swap) after its initial activation, Trace will not re-activate until the Pokémon has been withdrawn and subsequently re-entered. In Double Battles featuring only a single remaining opponent, if a Pokémon with Trace is deployed to replace a fainted team member, there exists a 50% chance for Trace to target the non-present opponent, thus failing to activate. However, once the Pokémon completes its turn (such as using a move or switching out), Trace will then activate and replicate the Ability of the opponent currently present. Upon a Pokémon with Trace entering combat, it duplicates an adjacent adversary's Ability at random (superseding its own). If no opponents possess Abilities that Trace can copy when the Pokémon with Trace enters battle, Trace cannot be activated until it next enters battle. Throughout the duration a Trace-equipped Pokémon is engaged, if an adjacent adversary ever possesses an Ability that Trace can replicate, Trace will activate to assume that Ability. Notwithstanding a Pokémon having previously activated Trace, if it subsequently re-acquires Trace (e.g., via Skill Swap), the Ability will trigger at the earliest possible moment. Trace is incapable of duplicating any of the following Abilities in generations marked with a ✔. If a Pokémon possessing this Ability is struck by an offensive move or a standard attack, Trace will substitute itself with the Ability of the attacking Pokémon. Trace exhibits no distinction between adversarial and allied Pokémon, and can even be triggered by advantageous actions like Safeguard. In games predating Gates to Infinity, Pokémon were capable of possessing multiple Abilities; in such instances, Trace would randomly select one to duplicate. The copied Ability persists until the Pokémon transitions to a subsequent floor. Diantha disclosed in 'Connecting with Gardevoir' that her Gardevoir's Ability is Trace. Volume 23 unveiled Blue's Porygon2 as having the Trace Ability.

Title: Huge Power (Ability)

The ability known as Huge Power (in Japanese: ちからもち, or Muscleman) first appeared in Generation III. Before Generation VI, it exclusively belonged to the evolutionary lineage of Marill. Any Pokémon possessing Huge Power will see its Attack stat multiplied by two. As this effect is an Attack modifier rather than a stat stage alteration, it effectively doubles the user's Attack when moves like Foul Play or Body Press are employed. Furthermore, damage caused by self-inflicted confusion is also influenced by Huge Power. There is a one-third probability that Huge Power will enhance a physical attack by 50% upon its execution. Its activation rate seems diminished within Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon.

Within Pokémon UNITE, Huge Power serves as Azumarill's unique Ability. If the user strikes just one opposing Pokémon using a basic attack, a standard move, or its Unite Move, that strike is assured to be a critical hit. Conversely, if the user's basic attack, move, or Unite Move connects with two or more foes simultaneously, none of those hits will register as critical. The Japanese name, ちからもち (chikaramochi), appears to be a dual meaning, interpretable as both "muscleman" and "strengthening mochi." This linguistic play might explain its presence on rabbit-esque Pokémon like Azumarill and Diggersby, referencing the moon rabbit from Japanese folklore, often shown pounding mochi. It could also provide a rationale for Mega Mawile's possession of this ability, given that its likely inspiration, the futakuchi-onna, is frequently depicted consuming mochi.

Title: Poison Point (Ability)

Originating in Generation III, the Ability known as Poison Point (どくのトゲ Poison Thorns in Japanese) was introduced. When a Pokémon bearing this Ability is struck by a contact-making move, there is a one-third probability of the attacking Pokémon becoming poisoned. This Ability is incapable of poisoning Pokémon categorized as Poison-type or Steel-type. Should the Ability-holder be hit by a multistrike move involving contact (such as Fury Swipes), each individual hit retains an independent chance to trigger this effect. The probability for Poison Point to inflict poison is lowered to 30%. Volume 24 revealed Green's Nidorina, Nido, to possess Poison Point as her Ability. According to the PokéSPedia data-book, Red's Nidorino, along with Giovanni's Nidoqueen and Nidoking, also featured Poison Point as their respective Abilities. Violet's Clodsire, Gardenia's Roserade, and Burgh's Whirlipede have additionally been identified as having Poison Point. The Ability Poison Touch, introduced in Generation V, can be understood as having an inverse effect to Poison Point. Prior to functioning as an Ability, Poison Point appeared as an Attack in the Pokémon Trading Card Game. Seadra is unique as the only Pokémon capable of having the Poison Point Ability without being a Poison-type itself.

Title: Inner Focus (Ability)

Inner Focus, known in Japanese as せいしんりょく (Force of Will), debuted as an Ability in Generation III. Its primary effect is to safeguard the Pokémon from flinching. This protection is not absolute, however; creatures possessing Mold Breaker, Teravolt, or Turboblaze can still cause a Pokémon with Inner Focus to flinch. It's important to note that while some descriptions might imply otherwise, the failure of Focus Punch when its user takes damage is distinct from flinching, meaning Inner Focus has no bearing on its operation. When a Pokémon with Inner Focus is targeted by a move that invariably causes flinching, distinct on-screen notifications appear: in Generation III, it states, "<Pokémon>'s Inner Focus prevents flinching!", while Generation IV displays, "<Pokémon> won't flinch because of its Inner Focus!". Starting from subsequent generations, no such explanatory message is shown. For Generations V and VI, the Legendary beasts Raikou, Entei, and Suicune possess Volt Absorb, Flash Fire, and Water Absorb as their respective Hidden Abilities, rather than Inner Focus. Despite this designation, acquiring these specific Pokémon with their Hidden Abilities was not possible in Generations V and VI. Within the events of Gotcha Where I Wantcha, Emerald's Pokédex confirmed that the rental Glalie utilized by Noland during his Battle Factory confrontation with Emerald possessed Inner Focus. The PokéSPedia data-book detailed that Blue's Alakazam, Giovanni's Crobat, and Lysandre's Mienfoo each had Inner Focus as their respective Abilities. Additionally, Koga's Golbat, Janine's Crobat, Lance's Dragonite, Glacia's Glalie, Maylene's Riolu, and Marshal's Mienshao were also shown to possess Inner Focus.

Title: Magma Armor (Ability)

Magma Armor (Japanese: マグマのよろい Magma Armor) is an Ability that made its debut in Generation III. This characteristic inherently shields the Pokémon from ever succumbing to the frozen status condition. Should a Pokémon possessing this Ability become frozen while engaged in battle—for instance, if it obtained the Ability prior to combat, through an effect like Skill Swap, or was frozen by an opponent with an Ability such as Mold Breaker—it will promptly thaw. In the event a frozen Pokémon also holds an Aspear, Lum, or Pumkin Berry, its Magma Armor will activate to remove the freeze condition, preempting the Berry's consumption.

However, if a frozen Pokémon with Magma Armor enters the fray as a starter, replaces a fallen ally, or acquires Magma Armor through Trace, its frozen state will persist until any Pokémon completes its action (e.g., executing a move or switching out). Conversely, thawing occurs immediately if it is brought into battle to substitute a Pokémon that has switched out, even if introduced via Baton Pass. A frozen Pokémon retaining Magma Armor as its inherent Ability, but not its active one, will stay frozen upon being withdrawn from battle.

Upon entering combat, a frozen Pokémon with Magma Armor will invariably be thawed. If a frozen Pokémon possesses Magma Armor as its innate Ability but not its currently active one, and is either active at the battle's conclusion or switched out, it will be thawed silently, without any onscreen notification. Furthermore, if a frozen Pokémon possesses Magma Armor as its base Ability yet not its currently active one, it will remain frozen when switched out. Should a frozen Pokémon acquire Magma Armor through Evolution or the use of an Ability Capsule, it will not thaw until it is explicitly deployed into battle.

Moreover, Pokémon endowed with Magma Armor are immune to being frozen by the Gentleman's Dusclops encountered in the Battle Pike, as well as on the Battle Arcade's game board. Beginning with Pokémon Emerald, if a Pokémon holding Magma Armor is present in the party (even if unconscious), the required number of cycles for all Pokémon Eggs within the party to hatch is reduced by half. This particular effect does not accumulate with other instances of Magma Armor, Flame Body, or Steam Engine; nevertheless, it can be combined with Hatching Power Pass Power, O-Power, or Roto Hatch. To reiterate, Magma Armor actively prevents the Pokémon from ever entering a frozen state.

Within the realms of Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon and Pokémon Mystery Dungeon: Rescue Team DX, Magma Armor furthermore causes projectiles launched at the Pokémon to incinerate, nullifying any potential damage or effects from such thrown items. However, items that fail to hit the Pokémon will not be subject to incineration.

Title: Water Veil (Ability)

Known in Japanese as みずのベール (Water Veil), this Ability first appeared in Generation III. Its primary function is to prevent the Pokémon from suffering the Burn status condition. If a Pokémon with Water Veil somehow becomes burned while in battle—for instance, by acquiring the Ability already burned (e.g., through Skill Swap or if gained outside of battle) or by being burned by a Pokémon with an Ability like Mold Breaker—it will be immediately cured of that burn.

Conversely, should a burned Pokémon possess Water Veil as its original Ability but not its current active Ability, switching it out will not remove the burn. When a burned Pokémon holding Water Veil enters the fray as a lead or to replace a fainted ally, or if it gains Water Veil through Trace, its burn will persist until after another Pokémon takes its turn (e.g., uses a move or switches out). Nevertheless, if such a Pokémon is switched in to replace a switched-out Pokémon (even via Baton Pass), the burn will be nullified.

Should a Pokémon with this Ability be burned while simultaneously holding a Rawst, Lum, or Yago Berry, Water Veil will act first to heal the burn before the Berry has a chance to be consumed. If a burned Pokémon has Water Veil as its inherent Ability but not its current one, and it is active at the battle's conclusion or is switched out, the burn will be silently cured. This implies that a burned Pokémon with Water Veil cannot enter battle in a burned state. A Pokémon entering battle with Trace and acquiring Water Veil will now instantly recover from any existing burn. However, if a burned Pokémon holds Water Veil as its original, non-active Ability, it will still remain burned when withdrawn or when the battle ends. Consequently, a burned Pokémon entering battle with Water Veil will now find itself immediately healed. If a Pokémon with this Ability is burned and holds a Rawst or Lum Berry, the Berry will be consumed to cure the burn before Water Veil has an opportunity to activate. A burned Pokémon that obtains Water Veil via Evolution or an Ability Capsule will not be cured of its burn until it is sent into battle.

Notably, Pokémon with Water Veil cannot be burned by the Gentleman's Kirlia or Dusclops in the Battle Pike, nor by hazards on the Battle Arcade's game board. Wallace's Seaking, named Charles, is confirmed to possess Water Veil as its Ability.

Title: Magnet Pull (Ability)

Known in Japanese as じりょく (Magnetism), Magnet Pull made its debut as an Ability in the third generation of games. While the user remains engaged in battle, Magnet Pull imposes a restriction on neighboring Steel-type adversaries, stopping them from escaping (even via Teleport) or swapping out. Nevertheless, when a Pokémon possessing Magnet Pull enters the battlefield, opposing Pokémon retain the freedom to switch out during that specific turn. The Pokémon is still able to switch out by employing moves such as Baton Pass, U-turn, Volt Switch, Flip Turn, or Parting Shot. Equipping a Shed Shell grants the Pokémon the ability to switch out, though it does not enable fleeing or the use of Teleport. Conversely, possessing the Run Away Ability or carrying a Smoke Ball permits the Pokémon to flee or utilize Teleport, but not to switch out. Should a player opt to flee rather than substituting a fainted Pokémon, they will bypass the trapping effect of a wild Pokémon with Shadow Tag. Its trapping effect extended to allies exclusively within Generation III. Presently, Steel-type Pokémon become immune to Magnet Pull if their typing also includes Ghost. Starting with Pokémon Emerald and continuing in subsequent titles, positioning a Pokémon with Magnet Pull at the head of the party (even if it has fainted) creates a 50% probability for the game to trigger a mandatory encounter with a Steel-type Pokémon, assuming such an encounter is feasible. Exclusively in Pokémon HeartGold and SoulSilver, when a Pokémon endowed with Magnet Pull shatters a rock, there's a fixed 5% boost to the likelihood of discovering an item. However, in Pokémon Sword and Shield alone, this specific chance is raised to 51%. For Pokémon Scarlet and Violet, this Ability holds no utility outside of combat, as its previous overworld functionality has been superseded by Meal Powers. Across Red Rescue Team and Blue Rescue Team, alongside Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky, a Pokémon bearing Magnet Pull possesses a 12% chance to incapacitate Steel-type attackers upon direct hit, thereby restricting their movement for several turns. Within Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, Magnet Pull renders adjacent Steel-type Pokémon incapable of motion. Nevertheless, these Pokémon can still employ moves or items. The PokéSPedia data-book confirmed that Cyrus's Probopass possessed Magnet Pull as its Ability. On My Pokémon Ranch, observing Probopass with the camera will occasionally cause it to descend and emit a pulse, attracting all proximate Steel-type Pokémon and Miis. With the sole exception of Meltan, every Pokémon species capable of learning Magnet Pull is also able to possess the Ability Sturdy.

Title: Soundproof (Ability)

Designated as ぼうおん (Soundproof) in Japanese, this Ability was first introduced in Generation III. Soundproof grants its possessor complete immunity to all sound-based moves. A sleeping Pokémon with this Ability cannot be roused by a Poké Flute; however, the Blue Flute will still awaken it as usual. Historically, Pokémon with Soundproof were immune to all sound-based moves, including those they themselves used. At one point, Pokémon exhibiting Soundproof, whether active or inactive, were not affected by Heal Bell. Furthermore, a Pokémon with Soundproof could still fall asleep during an Uproar.

In subsequent game iterations, certain mechanics underwent revision. Pokémon with Soundproof can now be affected by Heal Bell, though a distinction exists: active Pokémon with Soundproof still cannot be affected by Heal Bell, while inactive Pokémon with Soundproof *can* be. Additionally, a Pokémon equipped with this Ability will no longer fall asleep during an Uproar. Moreover, Pokémon with Soundproof are no longer immune to their own sound-based moves.

Within the Mystery Dungeon series titles – specifically Red Rescue Team, Blue Rescue Team, and Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky – Soundproof blocks the identical set of moves as in the core series, with the exception of Uproar. The PokéSPedia data-book confirms that Crystal's Mr. Mime, known as Mymee, has Soundproof as her designated Ability.

Title: Sand Stream (Ability)

This combat skill, known in Japan as すなおこし (Sand Raising), made its debut as an Ability in Generation III. Until the fourth generation, Tyranitar was uniquely associated with this particular power, functioning as its signature Ability. When a Pokémon possessing Sand Stream enters combat, it immediately conjures a sandstorm across the battlefield. The resulting weather effect largely mirrors the move Sandstorm; however, unlike the move's five-turn duration, Sand Stream's sandstorm persists for the entire battle unless superseded by another weather phenomenon. Should a Pokémon with Sand Stream be deployed concurrently with another whose Ability triggers a distinct weather type, the Ability of the slower Pokémon takes precedence over the faster one's, in which case the sandstorm's duration is limited to five turns. This five-turn limit can be prolonged to eight turns if the Pokémon is equipped with a Smooth Rock. Activation of Sand Stream is prevented if conditions like heavy rain, extremely harsh sunlight, or strong winds are already active.

Within the games Red Rescue Team, Blue Rescue Team, and Explorers of Time, Darkness, this Ability transforms the weather into a Sandstorm upon entering a new floor, and subsequently re-activates every 36 turns thereafter. In Super Mystery Dungeon, Sand Stream similarly initiates a sandstorm upon floor entry. This sandstorm persists for a specific duration, always a multiple of 66 turns, before dissipating. Once the weather has cleared, Sand Stream will re-trigger after another waiting period, also a multiple of 66 turns. Notably, a Gentleman's Hippopotas is confirmed to possess Sand Stream as its inherent Ability.

Title: Pressure (Ability)

Known in Japanese as プレッシャー (Pressure), this Ability made its debut in Generation III. Should any Pokémon (even an ally) direct a move at a target possessing Pressure, the PP cost for that move increases by one beyond its standard deduction. When multiple Pokémon targeted by a single attack are all endowed with Pressure, each instance causes an extra PP reduction. The supplementary PP drain imposed by Pressure occurs whenever a PP deduction would ordinarily take place. Consequently, its effect persists even if the move fails to connect, strikes multiple foes, or is negated by a target's elemental type or by defensive actions like Protect or Detect. Moves that influence the entire battlefield, for example, Rain Dance, are also subject to Pressure's effect. For non-Ghost-type Pokémon, Pressure affects Curse's PP, but exclusively when a wild Pokémon or an in-game Trainer employs it.

Upon a Pokémon with Pressure entering combat, the message "<name> is exerting its Pressure!" appears. Despite being self-targeting, an adversary's Imprison and Snatch moves incur increased PP expenditure due to Pressure. For Snatch, the extra PP is expended even if the move misses or intercepts a move from a Pokémon other than the one with Pressure. This ability also escalates the PP drain for Spikes, Stealth Rock, and Toxic Spikes, but notably not for Sticky Web. Should a Pokémon unleash Tera Blast against opponents, and one of those opponents possesses Pressure, the extra PP will be consumed even if the Pressure-wielding Pokémon isn't the direct target of the attack. Wild Pokémon are more prone to receiving assistance via calls for help when confronting a Pokémon endowed with Pressure. This particular effect is also shared by the Abilities Intimidate and Unnerve.

Beginning with Pokémon Emerald, if a Pressure-bearing Pokémon occupies the lead position in the party (even if incapacitated), there exists a 50% probability that encountered Pokémon will be at the pinnacle of their possible level range. Hustle and Vital Spirit possess this same field effect. However, in Pokémon Sword and Shield, Pressure seemingly has no bearing on the level spectrum of wild Pokémon. Similarly, in Pokémon Scarlet and Violet, Pressure also does not influence the level ranges of wild Pokémon. The targeting of a Pressure-equipped Pokémon by an opposing move results in the deduction of one additional PP immediately upon the move's execution.

Within Pokémon Mystery Dungeon: Rescue Team DX, the effects of multiple Pressure instances now accumulate; for example, striking two enemies concurrently, both possessing this Ability, will cause the attacker to forfeit 3 PP. The Mewtwo featured in Genesect and the Legend Awakened was officially verified on the Pokémon website to possess Pressure as its Ability, despite it never being explicitly shown or stated within the film itself. During the events of Storming the Forretress, Red's Poliwrath, named Poli, acquired the Pressure Ability after Organism No. 2 employed Skill Swap against him. Volume 42 disclosed that Silver's Weavile possessed Pressure as its Ability. The PokéSPedia data-book confirmed Emerald's Dusclops to have Pressure. Several other notable Pokémon, including Green's Articuno, Zapdos, and Moltres; Blaine's Mewtwo; a Roughneck's Vespiquen; the Tower duo; the Legendary beasts; the Pokémon of myth; and Zinzolin's Kyurem, were also revealed to bear the Pressure Ability. Amongst Legendary Pokémon, Pressure stands out as the most frequently observed Ability.

Title: Thick Fat (Ability)

Named あついしぼう (Atsui Shibō) in Japanese, Thick Fat is an Ability first seen in Generation III. When a Pokémon possessing Thick Fat is targeted by a move of either the Ice or Fire type, the attacking Pokémon's Attack or Special Attack stat is halved for the purpose of damage calculation, leading to a 50% reduction in the damage received by the Thick Fat Pokémon. Across the Red Rescue Team, Blue Rescue Team, Explorers of Time, Darkness, Sky, and Mystery Dungeon (WiiWare) titles, Thick Fat consistently cuts incoming damage from Fire- and Ice-type moves by half. However, in Gates to Infinity and Super Mystery Dungeon, the damage reduction from Fire- and Ice-type attacks provided by Thick Fat is 30%.

Within Pokémon UNITE, Thick Fat functions as Mamoswine's unique Ability. Upon dealing damage, Mamoswine experiences a temporary boost to its Defense and Special Defense stats, stackable up to three times. Furthermore, utilizing Ice Shard, Icicle Crash, or Ice Fang causes the Pokémon's subsequent basic attack to transform into a boosted attack. Moreover, should any of these attacks generate a freezing area of effect within an allied goal zone, it impairs the goal-scoring velocity of enemy Pokémon located within that zone.

Volume 40 confirmed that Diamond's Mamoswine, nicknamed Moo, possesses the Thick Fat Ability. The PokéSPedia data-book indicates that Emerald's Snorlax also carries Thick Fat as her Ability. Its introduction into the Pokémon Trading Card Game occurred with Hariyama from the Furious Fists expansion, where Thick Fat appeared as an Ability. A Pokémon featuring this Ability in the TCG suffers 30 fewer damage points from assaults originating from opposing Fire-type or Water-type Pokémon, with this reduction applied after Weakness and Resistance modifiers. Below is enumerated a compilation of Pokémon cards that feature the Thick Fat Ability.

Title: Early Bird (Ability)

Known as はやおき (Early Rising) in Japanese, Early Bird made its debut as an Ability in Generation III. Its primary function is to halve the duration a Pokémon remains asleep. Upon falling asleep, a predetermined number of turns for its slumber is established (the specific range varies by generation). For a Pokémon possessing Early Bird, this turn count is reduced by half, with any odd resulting value being rounded downwards. Consequently, if only a single turn is initially preset, this becomes 0 turns, leading to an immediate awakening upon its next move. The ability's effect similarly extends to the self-induced sleep caused by the move Rest, shortening its duration from a standard 2 turns to just 1 turn of unconsciousness (excluding the turn Rest is performed). Before Generation IX, the Pokémon Shiftry was endowed with Early Bird, rather than Wind Rider. Volume 25 revealed that Dody, Yellow's Dodrio, possessed Early Bird as his Ability. Later, in volume 42, Crystal's Xatu, Natee, was also shown to have Early Bird as her Ability. Furthermore, Violet's Dolliv has been identified with Early Bird as her Ability.

Title: Flame Body (Ability)

Known as ほのおのからだ (Flame Body) in Japanese, this Ability first appeared in Generation III. When a Pokémon with Flame Body is struck by a move that makes contact during battle, there is a 1/3 chance that the attacking Pokémon will be afflicted with a burn. For multistrike contact attacks (such as Fury Swipes), each individual hit possesses its own independent probability of triggering Flame Body's effect. The chance for Flame Body to inflict a burn was subsequently reduced to 30%.

Beyond combat, beginning with Pokémon Emerald, Flame Body offers a beneficial utility: if a Pokémon possessing this Ability is in the party (even if fainted), the total number of cycles required for all Pokémon Eggs within the party to hatch is halved. This particular egg-hatching bonus does not stack with itself, Magma Armor, or Steam Engine. However, it can be combined with Hatching Power, Pass Power, O-Power, or Roto Hatch.

In the spin-off titles Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon and Pokémon Mystery Dungeon: Rescue Team DX, Flame Body introduces an additional property: any items thrown at the Pokémon are incinerated, thereby nullifying their intended effects. Nevertheless, thrown items that fail to make contact with the Pokémon will not be burned.

In the manga's Japan Mini-volume 6 for the X & Y arc, Y's Fletchinder, nicknamed Fletchy, was confirmed to have Flame Body as her Ability. Other Pokémon revealed to possess Flame Body include Flint's Magmortar, Ghetsis's Volcarona, and various Magby utilized by Team Galactic Grunts.

Title: Run Away (Ability)

The Ability known as Run Away (Japanese: にげあし Run Away) made its debut in Generation III. A Pokémon equipped with this power can consistently escape from battle and successfully employ the move Teleport, bypassing the effects of all trapping maneuvers and Abilities, including Ingrain and various binding moves. It's important to note that this Ability has no bearing on the act of switching out a Pokémon. Run Away does not ensure a guaranteed flight in the Battle Pyramid; however, if a Pokémon with this Ability manages to flee, that successful escape will be credited to Run Away. In both Generations III and IV, if a player's Pokémon possessing Run Away faints during a wild encounter, and the player attempts to flee instead of sending out another Pokémon, escape is certain. This guaranteed escape mechanism was removed from Generation V onwards, making it no longer assured in such scenarios. Until Generation V, upon successful activation, the text "<Pokémon name> escaped using Run Away!" would appear. From Generation V onward, a banner simply displaying "Run Away" is shown instead. Before the release of Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, a Pokémon with Run Away would become Terrified and flee from all Pokémon when its HP dropped below 50%. Leaders were exempt from this effect. In the Explorers series and Gates to Infinity, Run Away additionally conferred protection against becoming Stuck by moves like Mean Look or Abilities such as Shadow Tag, specifically when the Pokémon's HP fell below the 50% mark. In Super Mystery Dungeon, however, Run Away's function simplified to merely preventing the Pokémon from becoming stuck, entirely irrespective of its current HP. In the Pokémon Adventures chapter "Just My Luck...Shuckle," Crystal's Rapidash was identified as possessing Run Away as his Ability by Emerald's Pokédex. Volume 24 disclosed that Green's Snubbull also had Run Away as his characteristic Ability. Later, in volume 40, Platinum's Pachirisu was shown to possess Run Away as her Ability. Furthermore, Ruby's Poochyena, Nana, and Professor Elm's Sentret were all confirmed to have Run Away as their respective Abilities. Given that Ghost-type Pokémon are inherently assured to escape any wild encounter regardless of their Speed stat, Gimmighoul's Run Away Ability serves no practical purpose unless its type is altered through a move like Soak. Should the Nihilego encountered within Aether Paradise have its Ability swapped to Run Away, it gains the capability to flee on the fifth turn of combat, even if it is currently trapped. In such an instance, the message "You got away safely!" will still be presented, even though it is Nihilego, not the player, that is making the escape.

Title: Keen Eye (Ability)

Originating in Generation III, Keen Eye (Japanese: するどいめ Keen Eye) is an Ability that prevents other Pokémon from lowering the accuracy stat stage of the Pokémon possessing it. This protection does not, however, extend to preventing Spectral Thief from stealing (i.e., resetting) beneficial stat changes. Effects that decrease accuracy through means other than stat stages, such as Wonder Skin and Sand Veil, remain unaffected by Keen Eye. Furthermore, the Abilities Mold Breaker, Teravolt, and Turboblaze can ignore Keen Eye. Notably, Keen Eye also now causes the user to disregard the target's evasion stages.

From Pokémon Emerald onward, Keen Eye grants a 50% chance to prevent a random wild encounter when the Pokémon holding this Ability is in the lead party slot (even if fainted), provided the wild Pokémon is at least 5 levels lower than the lead. This particular field effect is shared with Intimidate. Exclusively in Pokémon HeartGold and SoulSilver, a Pokémon with Keen Eye that breaks a rock receives a flat 5% increase in the odds of finding an item. Apart from these specific instances, Keen Eye appears to hold no additional effects outside of battle.

In general, Keen Eye serves to avert accuracy reduction, though it offers no defense against the Whiffer status condition. Biographical data, specifically the PokéSPedia data-book, confirmed Blue's Pidgeot to have Keen Eye. Other Pokémon revealed to possess Keen Eye include Falkner's Skarmory, Skyla's Swanna, three Patrat from the BW Agency, and Professor Oak's Fearow. A relevant media reference is Pocket Monsters BW: The Heroes of Fire and Thunder.

Title: Hyper Cutter (Ability)

An Ability originating from Generation III, Hyper Cutter (known in Japanese as かいりきバサミ, or Superpowerful Claws) made its debut then. It prevents external forces from reducing the Attack stat stage of the Pokémon possessing it. However, it offers no protection against the Pokémon itself decreasing its own Attack, for instance, through moves like Superpower. Furthermore, Spectral Thief's ability to pilfer (which also resets) beneficial stat modifiers remains unchecked by Hyper Cutter. The Ability has no impact on mechanics that reduce Attack through means other than stat stages, including Power Swap, Heart Swap, and Power Split. Abilities like Mold Breaker, Teravolt, and Turboblaze can bypass Hyper Cutter's effect. A unique overworld effect in Pokémon Emerald allowed a Pokémon with Hyper Cutter, when using Cut, to clear all tall grass within a two-square radius around the player, a broader area compared to the standard one-square radius without the Ability. With the removal of tall grass cutting functionality, Hyper Cutter no longer provides any benefit outside of combat. Within the narrative "Pinsir Me, I Must Be Dreaming," Emerald's Pokédex confirmed that the rental Pinsir he utilized in his Battle Factory bout against Noland possessed Hyper Cutter as its Ability. Later, X's Pinsir, Rute, was shown to have Hyper Cutter in the Japan Mini-volume 6 of the X & Y arc. The PokéSPedia data-book also disclosed that Blake's Gliscor, named Glisott, has Hyper Cutter.

Title: Pickup (Ability)

Introduced in Generation III, Pickup (Japanese: ものひろい Pickup) is an Ability. Should another Pokémon consume a single-use item during a turn (such as by launching it with Fling or activating Natural Gift), a Pokémon possessing Pickup will retrieve that item at the turn's conclusion, provided it isn't currently holding an item. When multiple Pokémon benefit from either Pickup or Harvest, the one with superior speed takes precedence in item restoration. In scenarios where several items are available for collection, the Ability user will acquire the item that was most recently utilized. A given Pokémon's Pickup effect is limited to a single activation per turn.

Pickup is incapable of retrieving an item if the consuming Pokémon has departed the battlefield by the turn's end, or if the item has already been restored through other means (e.g., Recycle, another Pokémon's Pickup, or Harvest). An item consumed by a Pokémon prior to switching out cannot be picked up, even if that Pokémon returns to the field within the same turn. Items obliterated by Incinerate, devoured by Bug Bite or Pluck, or removed by Knock Off are beyond Pickup's retrieval capabilities. A deflated Air Balloon is also uncollectible. Only one item consumption is tracked per Pokémon at any given moment; should a Pokémon consume an item, then acquire and consume a second, the initial item becomes uncollectible, regardless of whether the second item is retrieved or restored and then exchanged. However, if a Pokémon gains a new item *after* consuming one, Pickup can still retrieve the original item, provided the new item is not subsequently consumed. During encounters with wild Pokémon, a Pickup user will recover its own consumed held item at the close of the turn.

Upon victorious completion of a battle, any party Pokémon with this Ability that isn't currently holding an item has a 10% probability of spontaneously acquiring a new held item, even if it remained inactive or is otherwise incapable of battling. Generation VIII introduced the functionality for Pickup Pokémon to locate items even within the Battle Tower. In Pokémon FireRed and LeafGreen, Pickup's focus was primarily on Berries, a resource less abundant in Kanto and the Sevii Islands compared to Hoenn. Pokémon Emerald brought a modification, linking the Ability's outcome to the user's level. Furthermore, the Battle Pyramid features a distinct item list for Pickup, with its contents determined by the number of floors or rounds the player has finished within the Pyramid. This specific pool of items is shared between the Pickup table and the visible item balls on the ground. Pokémon XD: Gale of Darkness employs the identical item table from Pokémon Emerald Version, save for the replacement of Escape Rope and Repel with Burn Heal and Paralyze Heal, respectively.

When deployed from a Poké Ball, Munchlax roams the stage, utilizing Pickup to consume items present. Nevertheless, it refrains from consuming sentient items (like a Mr. Saturn), Assist Trophies, Poké Balls, Smash Balls, or various collectibles (including CDs, Trophies, or Stickers). Upon entering each new floor, a Pokémon with Pickup possesses a slight chance of discovering an item native to that floor (excluding Poké), provided it is not currently holding anything. In the Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky series, Pickup's functionality remains inactive until the player acquires the Treasure Bag. Rescue Team DX saw a revision to Pickup, allowing it to locate items even while holding one, as found items are now deposited into the Toolbox. Should the Toolbox be at capacity, the discovered item is instead placed on the dungeon floor. Pickup remains effective within Makuhita Dojo, despite the absence of naturally spawning items on the ground. In the story 'Pinsir Me, I Must Be Dreaming,' Emerald's Pokédex identified Pickup as the Ability of the rental Linoone he employed in his Battle Factory bout against Noland. Within the Unova Pokédex, Lillipup stands as the sole Pokémon capable of possessing the Pickup Ability. Most Pokémon that can manifest Pickup will relinquish this Ability upon undergoing evolution.

Title: Truant (Ability)

The Ability Truant (known in Japanese as なまけ, meaning "Lazy") made its debut in Generation III. Before Generation V and again in Generation IX, this Ability was exclusive to Slakoth and Slaking. A Pokémon possessing Truant is prevented from executing a move every other turn because it loafs around; consequently, it can act on its initial turn but not its subsequent turn. The turn counter for Truant resets upon switching the Pokémon out or if the Pokémon acquires the Truant Ability. Utilizing an item is not considered a turn for Truant's effect. Generation V introduced a mechanic where sleep would reset Truant, ensuring a Truant Pokémon could always attack on the turn it awakened. During Generation III, employing a healing item was counted as a turn for Truant. Moves requiring a charging turn, like Fly, are ineffective (unless the user carries a Power Herb). This is because the turn designated for move execution coincides with the turn the Pokémon is truant. Should a Truant Pokémon employ a move that mandates a recharge on the following turn (for example, Hyper Beam), it will both loaf around and recharge within that same turn. Due to this recharge, the Pokémon is unable to utilize items or withdraw from battle during that specific turn. If a Pokémon acquires Truant after initiating Mega Evolution, Ultra Burst, or Dynamax at the turn's start, yet prior to making its move for that turn, Truant will manifest on its initial attempt to act.

Truant resists being overwritten by Simple Beam, Worry Seed, or Entrainment; however, it is susceptible to being overwritten by Skill Swap, Role Play, Doodle, or Mummy, or can be nullified by Gastro Acid or Neutralizing Gas. When a Truant Pokémon executes a move and subsequently receives an Instruction to repeat that move, the Pokémon will merely loaf around instead of performing the action. On the following turn, the Truant-afflicted Pokémon will regain its normal ability to use a move. Should a Pokémon with Truant enter the battle after an ally faints due to end-of-turn damage, Truant will become active during its first move attempt. Encore compels a Truant Pokémon to repeatedly use the last move it executed. Within Red Rescue Team and Blue Rescue Team, Explorers of Time, Darkness and Sky, and the Mystery Dungeon (WiiWare) titles, if a Pokémon bearing this Ability utilizes a move or a Wonder Orb, it acquires the Paused status, which hinders it from acting on the subsequent turn. In Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, Truant instead inflicts the "truant" status condition for a single turn. Diverging from the earlier Paused status, the "truant" status solely inhibits move usage but permits item consumption. Furthermore, Truant possesses the capacity to impose the Yawning status condition on all nearby adversaries if the Truant-enabled Pokémon is asleep. The Truant Wand grants its wielder the power to alter any Pokémon's Ability to Truant, even that of bosses. During Rayquaza Redemption I, Norman's Spinda obtained Truant by employing Skill Swap on his own Slaking. His pair of Slakoth also possess this Ability. The rental Slaking utilized by Emerald during his Battle Factory challenge was identified as having Truant as its Ability.

Title: Hustle (Ability)

An Ability making its debut in Generation III, Hustle is known in Japan as はりきり, meaning "Enthusiasm." This Ability boosts the user's Attack stat by a substantial 50%; however, it concurrently reduces the accuracy of the user's physical moves by 20% (which, starting from Generation V, is roughly represented by a 3277/4096 multiplier). OHKO moves and those categorized as special are immune to Hustle's accuracy reduction. Furthermore, beyond diminishing the precision of physical attacks, Hustle also impairs the accuracy of status moves whose types align with those typically designated as physical. Whether Hidden Power's accuracy is influenced by Hustle depends on the specific damage type it delivers. However, status moves are no longer subject to Hustle's accuracy penalty, irrespective of their type. Commencing with Pokémon Emerald, if a Pokémon possessing Hustle occupies the first position in the party (even if it has fainted), a 50% likelihood exists that encountered wild Pokémon will have their level capped at the upper limit of their potential encounter range. This particular field effect is shared with the Abilities Pressure and Vital Spirit. Within Pokémon Sword and Shield, Hustle seemingly exerts no influence on the level spectrum of wild Pokémon. Similarly, in Pokémon Scarlet and Violet, Hustle also appears to have no impact on the level spread of wild Pokémon. Across the Red Rescue Team and Blue Rescue Team titles, as well as Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky, Hustle provides a 50% power increase to physical moves, yet simultaneously diminishes the user's accuracy by two stages. Volume 42 confirmed that Gold's Togepi, named Togebo, possesses Hustle as its Ability.

Title: Cute Charm (Ability)

Cute Charm (Japanese: メロメロボディ Mad Love Body) is an Ability that made its debut in Generation III. During a battle, should a Pokémon possessing this Ability be struck by a contact-inducing move from an opposing Pokémon of the contrary sex, there is a 30% likelihood that the attacking Pokémon will succumb to infatuation. This effect will not activate if either combatant is a sexless Pokémon (for instance, Magnemite). When a multi-hit attack that makes contact (such as Fury Swipes) strikes a Pokémon with Cute Charm, each individual hit carries its own separate opportunity to trigger the Ability.

While opponents in Tera Raid Battles are entirely unaffected by infatuation, rendering Cute Charm inert against them regardless of their sex, the Ability does influence foes in Max Raid Battles from Generation VIII.

Commencing with Pokémon Emerald, if a Pokémon with Cute Charm is positioned in the foremost slot of the party (even if it has been incapacitated), the game is given a 66.7% probability of encountering a wild Pokémon of the alternate sex to the Cute Charm user, provided that sex is possible for the species. This specific effect does not extend to swarming Pokémon species, with Generation IV being an exception, nor does it influence Pokémon encountered within Hidden Grottoes. In Pokémon Sword and Shield, this overworld gender-forcing chance is adjusted to 66% instead. However, in Pokémon Scarlet and Violet, this Ability has no utility outside of battle.

Exclusive to Generation IV, when Cute Charm dictates a Pokémon's gender, it achieves this by assigning its personality value from a selection of 25 possibilities, each uniquely tied to a nature that aligns with the desired sex. The precise array of these potential values is contingent upon the Cute Charm Pokémon's own sex and, if the Cute Charm Pokémon is female, also on the wild species' inherent gender ratio. Such manipulation of the personality value carries substantial implications for other traits that are derived from it. Consequently, for species that Cute Charm can influence (i.e., those not exclusively male, female, or sexless), the odds of encountering a Shiny Pokémon diverge from the standard 1/8192. These probabilities hinge on how many of the 25 forced personality values, when combined with the Trainer ID and Secret ID, result in a Shiny. The chances can plunge to a minimum of 1/24576 (occurring if none of the 25 forced values produce a Shiny, meaning a Shiny can only manifest if Cute Charm's gender-forcing fails), or they can surge to an impressive 21.34% (if 8 of the 25 values yield a Shiny). Furthermore, a smaller numerical difference between the Trainer ID and Secret ID correlates with higher Shiny encounter probabilities. This 1/24576 figure represents the lowest non-zero Shiny chance for wild Pokémon in any core series game when Cute Charm is active and fails to force a Shiny-compatible personality value. Also in Generation IV, Spinda whose sex has been influenced by Cute Charm possess only one of 50 possible patterns (25 for males, 25 for females).

Internally, Pokémon Legends: Arceus lists Incarnate Forme Enamorus's Ability as Healer, not Cute Charm. In the multiplayer online battle arena game Pokémon UNITE, Cute Charm is Wigglytuff's unique Ability. Upon taking damage from a close-range enemy attack, it infatuates that foe for a brief duration, causing them to involuntarily move towards Wigglytuff. This effect has a 9-second cooldown after activation.

Several manga entries feature Pokémon with Cute Charm: Green's Wigglytuff, Jiggly, and Clefable, Clefy, were noted to have it in volume 24. Y's Sylveon, Veevee, was revealed with this Ability in the Japan Mini-volume 6 for the X & Y arc. The PokéSPedia data-book confirmed Green's Jigglypuff, Ririri, possessed Cute Charm, as did Whitney's Igglybuff, Buff Buff, and Cleffa, Fafa.

Title: Plus (Ability)

An Ability first appearing in Generation III, Plus (Japanese: プラス Plus) was, until Generation V, the signature skill of Plusle. This Ability fundamentally powers up when combined with Minus. In a Double Battle scenario, if a Pokémon with Plus begins combat alongside an ally possessing the Minus Ability, its own Special Attack stat immediately increases by 50%. More broadly, when a Pokémon with Plus enters any battle with an ally also holding Plus or Minus, that Pokémon's Special Attack stat is similarly boosted by 50%. Furthermore, this Ability influences certain moves: Magnetic Flux elevates the Defense and Special Defense of allied Pokémon with Plus or Minus by one stage, while Gear Up increases the Attack and Special Attack of such allies by one stage. Additionally, a Pokémon with Plus will gain a 50% bonus to its Special Attack when it uses a special move, provided an allied Pokémon with the Minus Ability is nearby. In the Pokémon Mystery Dungeon series, starting from Gates to Infinity, the Ability also becomes active if a Plus-carrying ally is present in the same room. As a notable example, Ingo's Klinklang has been revealed to possess Plus.

Title: Minus (Ability)

Minus (Japanese: マイナス Minus) is a distinct Ability first introduced in Generation III. Before Generation V, it was Minun's exclusive characteristic. This Ability is designed to function synergistically with Plus.

Upon entering any battle, a Pokémon with Minus will receive a 50% boost to its Special Attack stat, provided an ally possessing either the Plus or Minus Ability is present on the field.

Furthermore, when a Pokémon exhibiting Minus executes a special move, it benefits from a 50% bonus to its Special Attack power for that specific move, given that an allied Pokémon with the Plus Ability is nearby.

Specific moves also interact beneficially with this Ability. The move Magnetic Flux elevates the Defense and Special Defense of all allied Pokémon with Plus or Minus by one stage. Similarly, Gear Up increases the Attack and Special Attack of allied Pokémon possessing the Plus or Minus Ability by a single stage.

In the Pokémon Mystery Dungeon series, starting with Gates to Infinity, this Ability additionally activates if an allied Pokémon with Minus is located in the same room.

Title: Forecast (Ability)

Introduced in Generation III, Forecast (Japanese: てんきや Temperamental) is an Ability exclusively held by Castform. This unique capability causes Castform to alter its physical appearance and subsequently its elemental type, contingent on prevailing weather conditions. Specifically, during harsh sunlight or extremely harsh sunlight, Castform transforms into its Sunny Form, acquiring the Fire-type. When hail is active, it takes on the Ice-type Snowy Form. In the presence of rain or heavy rain, it transitions into its Water-type Rainy Form. Conversely, if a sandstorm, fog, strong winds, a shadowy aura, or clear weather persists, Castform reverts to its standard, Normal-type form. This reversion to its normal form also occurs if another Pokémon on the battlefield possesses the Cloud Nine or Air Lock Abilities. Forecast's effects are exclusively observed on Castform; it does not impact any other Pokémon. Notably, Forecast's form-changing mechanism is delayed until after damage from hazards like Stealth Rock has been applied.

Should a Pokémon successfully transform into Castform while retaining Forecast, it will mimic Castform's weather-dependent form changes. If Castform, or a Pokémon that has transformed into it, loses the Forecast Ability, or if Forecast is suppressed via Gastro Acid, the Pokémon will become fixed in its current form. Upon regaining Forecast, the Pokémon immediately shifts to the form corresponding to the prevailing weather, even if it was already in that form (an action that will reset its type if it had been altered by effects such as Color Change).

However, should a Pokémon transform into Castform without also acquiring the Forecast Ability, it will retain the precise form of the target Castform, irrespective of any active weather conditions. Conversely, if Forecast is lost or suppressed by Gastro Acid specifically for Castform itself, it will promptly revert to its normal form and lose the capability to shift forms thereafter. Furthermore, when Castform regains Forecast, it will adopt the form dictated by the current weather, unless the weather would prompt its normal form.

The move Role Play will fail if its target possesses the Forecast Ability. Similarly, Entrainment cannot be successfully used if the acting Pokémon has Forecast. Forecast resists being copied by Abilities like Power of Alchemy or Receiver, and it cannot be swapped via Wandering Spirit. In environments experiencing snow, Forecast causes Castform to become Ice-type.

A unique exception in Pokémon Mystery Dungeon: Red Rescue Team and Blue Rescue Team is that foggy weather will cause Castform to become Water-type. In the titles Red and Blue Rescue Team, alongside Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky, Forecast's type-altering aspect can affect Pokémon beyond Castform (e.g., if acquired through Role Play or Trace), although its form-changing capability remains exclusive. A distinctive detail in Pokémon XD: Gale of Darkness is that when Castform undergoes a transformation, the Trainer's animation will be the same as if one of their Pokémon had fainted.

Title: Sticky Hold (Ability)

Known in Japanese as ねんちゃく (Adhesion), Sticky Hold is an Ability that made its debut in Generation III. This Ability serves to protect the held item of the Pokémon possessing it. The item becomes impervious to being taken by moves such as Covet, Thief, Pickpocket, or the Ability Magician; to being eaten by Bug Bite or Pluck; to being destroyed by Incinerate or Corrosive Gas; or to being removed by Knock Off. Furthermore, Sticky Hold prevents the Pokémon from being affected by another Pokémon's Trick or Switcheroo, a protection that applies even if the Pokémon holds no item, though it does not prevent the Pokémon from using these moves itself. The inherent transfer effect of a Sticky Barb is an exception, as Sticky Hold offers no safeguard against it. Certain Abilities, namely Mold Breaker, Turboblaze, and Teravolt, are capable of ignoring Sticky Hold's effects. The held item remains protected by Sticky Hold even when the Pokémon is knocked out by an attack. However, should the Pokémon be knocked out by the attack, Sticky Hold does not shield the held item.

From Pokémon Emerald onwards, placing a Pokémon with Sticky Hold at the head of the party, even if it has fainted, causes bites to occur more frequently while fishing. Suction Cups provides this same effect. Due to an error in Pokémon Diamond, Pearl, and Platinum, Sticky Hold had no functional effect outside of battle. This specific behavior was carried over into Pokémon Brilliant Diamond and Shining Pearl, where the Ability also remains ineffective outside of combat. It was revealed that Alder's Accelgor possesses Sticky Hold as its Ability.

Title: Shed Skin (Ability)

Shed Skin (だっぴ Shed Skin in Japanese) is a Pokémon Ability that was introduced in Generation III. At the conclusion of each turn, a Pokémon with Shed Skin has a probability of being cured of any non-volatile status condition. If a status condition is successfully cleared by this Ability, the recovery takes place before any damage from burn or poison is inflicted during that turn. Solely in Pokémon Ruby and Sapphire, due to an oversight, a Pokémon that awakens from sleep via Shed Skin will not have the Nightmare status removed, even after waking up. Several Pokémon have been depicted with Shed Skin as their Ability, including Yellow's Metapod (Kitty), Bugsy's Kakuna, Lance's Dragonair and Pupitar, and Paka's Burmy. Prior to its establishment as an Ability, Shed Skin appeared as an Attack in the Pokémon Trading Card Game.

Title: Guts (Ability)

Known in Japanese as こんじょう Guts, this Ability was first implemented in Generation III. Should a Pokémon possessing this Ability be afflicted by a status condition (specifically, a non-volatile one), its Attack stat receives a 50% boost. Furthermore, the Guts-bearing Pokémon becomes immune to the Attack reduction typically imposed by a burn. It's important to note that the Attack enhancement provided by Guts fails to trigger if the Pokémon is frozen and concurrently employs a move capable of thawing itself, for instance, Flame Wheel or Flare Blitz. Upon suffering from a detrimental status affliction, the Pokémon's Attack becomes either 2 times or 1.5 times its original value. In the context of Pokémon UNITE, Guts functions as Machamp's unique Ability, causing its Attack stat to rise by 10% when it is under the effect of any status condition. The Ability was identified as belonging to Blue's Machamp in volume 23. Subsequently, in volume 25, Yellow's Raticate, known as Ratty, was also shown to possess Guts. Additionally, Silver's Ursaring and Marshal's Throh have both been depicted with Guts as their Ability.

Title: Marvel Scale (Ability)

Introduced in Generation III, the Ability known as Marvel Scale (Japanese: ふしぎなうろこ, or Marvelous Scale) functioned as Milotic's signature Ability exclusively until Generation V. Should a Pokémon possessing this Ability be affected by a non-volatile status condition, its Defense stat receives a 50% increment. Consequently, when such a Pokémon is struck by a physical attack while under a status ailment, it benefits from a 50% defense bonus. In the multiplayer online battle arena game Pokémon UNITE, Marvel Scale is the designated Ability for both Dratini and Dragonair. Here, it provides a flat increase of 100 to the user's Defense stat if they are afflicted by any status condition. However, upon evolving into Dragonite, this Ability transforms into Multiscale. In the manga chapter 'Just My Luck...Shuckle', Lucy's Milotic was confirmed to possess Marvel Scale as its Ability by Emerald's Pokédex.

Title: Liquid Ooze (Ability)

Originating in Generation III, Liquid Ooze (Japanese: ヘドロえき, meaning 'Oozing Liquid') is an Ability with a unique effect on HP-draining maneuvers. When a Pokémon possessing Liquid Ooze is targeted by such a move, the typical healing outcome for the attacker is reversed: the move instead inflicts damage upon its user.

Specifically, should a Pokémon with this Ability be struck by a draining technique like Giga Drain or Leech Life—including the status move Strength Sap—or if it sustains damage from the debilitating Leech Seed effect, the health points that would have been restored to the opposing Pokémon are instead dealt back as damage. This calculation occurs after any Big Root modifications. Importantly, the primary damage inflicted by the draining move upon the Liquid Ooze Pokémon remains entirely unaffected by this ability's mechanism.

A notable historical exception is Dream Eater, which was not affected by Liquid Ooze in generations prior to Generation V. Regardless of whether the HP-draining move's user is unable to recover HP—for instance, if they are at maximum health or under the influence of Heal Block—they will still incur damage from Liquid Ooze.

In a scenario where the Pokémon using an HP-draining move would faint from Liquid Ooze's backlash on the same turn that the target also faints from the move itself, the attacker's faint takes precedence over the victim's. Consequently, if these are the final Pokémon for each trainer, the team of the attacking Pokémon is defeated.

However, a distinct priority applies to Leech Seed: if the Pokémon affected by Leech Seed's HP drain (the Liquid Ooze holder) faints on the same turn that the Leech Seed user (who is now damaged by Liquid Ooze) also faints, the Pokémon initially affected by Leech Seed is declared fainted first. This implies that if both trainers have only one Pokémon remaining, the team whose Pokémon was originally affected by Leech Seed will lose the match.

Title: Overgrow (Ability)

Overgrow, known in Japanese as しんりょく (Fresh Greenery), made its debut as an Ability in Generation III. It is a characteristic shared by all Grass-type first partner Pokémon.

Should a Pokémon possessing Overgrow utilize a Grass-type move, that move's power experiences a 50% boost, provided the user's remaining HP is at or below one-third of its maximum. While traditionally described as enhancing move power, its technical implementation actually involves a 50% augmentation of the Pokémon's Attack or Special Attack during damage calculation for any Grass-type move employed, ultimately yielding an identical outcome.

Furthermore, a specific condition states that if the Pokémon's HP drops below 25%, the potency of Grass-type moves scales by either 2-times or 1.5-times. In the distinct game of Pokémon Conquest, Overgrow functions to heighten the user's Attack stat when their HP percentage falls beneath one-third.

Within Pokémon UNITE, Overgrow serves as Venusaur's unique Ability. When Venusaur's HP reaches 30% or less, it grants a 20% increase to all damage it inflicts.

The manga series also highlights this Ability: Red's Venusaur, Saur, was identified as having Overgrow in volume 23. Similarly, Diamond's Torterra, Tru, was shown with Overgrow in volume 40. Crystal's Meganium, Megaree, also exhibited Overgrow, as detailed in volume 42. In Volume 4 of the Scarlet & Violet arc, Scarlet's Floragato was confirmed to possess Overgrow. Additional revelations from the PokéSPedia data-book include Emerald's Sceptile, X's Chesnaught (Marisso), and Moon's Rowlet as having Overgrow. Finally, Henry's Thwackey (Twiggy) and Cheren's Snivy were also recognized for possessing this Ability.

Title: Blaze (Ability)

This page details the Ability Blaze. For the location Mt. Blaze in Pokémon Mystery Dungeon: Red Rescue Team and Blue Rescue Team, consult its specific entry. Known in Japanese as もうか (Raging Flames), Blaze is an Ability first introduced in Generation III. All Fire-type first partner Pokémon possess this Ability. When a Pokémon with Blaze uses a Fire-type move, its power gains a 50% increase if the user's remaining HP is at or below one-third of its maximum. Rather than directly boosting move power, Blaze technically enhances the Pokémon's Attack or Special Attack by 50% during damage calculation for Fire-type moves, resulting in an identical effective potency. Should the Pokémon's HP drop below 25%, the power of Fire-type moves increases by 2-times or 1.5-times. Additionally, Blaze boosts the user's Attack if the Pokémon's HP is below one-third.

In Pokémon UNITE, Blaze functions as Charizard and Cinderace's Ability, activating when the user is at half or less HP. Charizard's increases critical hit rate by 20%, while Cinderace's raises critical-hit rate by 10% and basic attack speed by 20% for a short time; furthermore, hitting an enemy with a basic attack or move places a cinder on them. Blaze was confirmed as the Ability for Blue's Charizard in volume 23. Pearl's Infernape, Chimler, was revealed to have Blaze in volume 40. Gold's Typhlosion, Exbo, had Blaze confirmed in volume 42. In volume 44, both Black's Tepig, Tep, and White's Tepig, Gigi, were disclosed to possess Blaze. The PokéSPedia data book listed Blaze as the Ability for Sapphire's Blaziken (Chic), X's Charizard (Salamè), and Sun's Incineroar (Dollar). Furthermore, Casey's Cinderace, Bit, and Violet's Crocalor were identified with Blaze as their Ability.

Title: Torrent (Ability)

The Ability known as Torrent (Japanese: げきりゅう Raging Rapids) made its debut in Generation III. Every Water-type first partner Pokémon possesses this particular Ability. Should a Pokémon possessing Torrent employ a Water-type attack, the move's power experiences a 50% boost, provided the user's current HP is at or below one-third of its maximum. Technically, rather than directly augmenting the power of Water-type moves, Torrent now elevates the Pokémon's Attack or Special Attack by 50% during the damage calculation process when a Water-type move is unleashed, achieving an equivalent practical outcome. Separately, if the Pokémon's health falls under 25%, the strength of its Water-type moves is noted to increase by either 2-times or 1.5-times. Within the Pokémon Conquest game, Torrent functions by enhancing the user's Attack stat if the Pokémon deploying it has less than a third of its total HP remaining. Specifically in Pokémon UNITE, Torrent serves as the Ability for both Blastoise and Greninja, triggering when their HP reaches 50% or lower. Blastoise's iteration of Torrent grants a 20% boost to both its Attack and Sp. Attack, whereas Greninja's version elevates its Attack by 5% and its movement speed by 12%. Volume 24 disclosed that Green's Blastoise, nicknamed Blasty, possessed Torrent as its Ability. Later, in volume 40, Platinum's Empoleon was shown to have Torrent as its Ability. And in volume 42, Silver's Feraligatr was identified with Torrent as its Ability. The PokéSPedia data book confirmed that Ruby's Swampert, Mumu, and Blake's Dewott both had Torrent as their respective Abilities. Bianca's Oshawott and Cedric Juniper's Samurott were also noted to possess Torrent as their Ability. Of all the Ability variations akin to Overgrow, Torrent stands alone as the one that none of Ash's Pokémon have ever activated throughout Pokémon the Series. Furthermore, it is unique among Overgrow variations in that no main character's Pokémon has ever activated it.

Title: Swarm (Ability)

Introduced in Generation III, Swarm (known in Japanese as むしのしらせ, or Bug Notification) is an Ability. When a Pokémon possessing Swarm unleashes a Bug-type attack, that move's effective power escalates by 50% if the user's current HP falls to ⅓ or below of its total maximum. Although the practical outcome is identical, Swarm's mechanism now involves a technical boost of 50% to the Pokémon's Attack or Special Attack during the damage calculation phase, specifically when a Bug-type move is executed, rather than directly increasing the move's base power.

In Pokémon Emerald, an overworld effect is present: if Swarm is possessed by the leading Pokémon in the player's party, the audible frequency of wild Pokémon cries in the overworld becomes higher. However, despite the presence of this overworld mechanic in Pokémon Sun, Moon, Ultra Sun, and Ultra Moon, Swarm confers no such overworld benefit in those titles. Furthermore, should a Pokémon with Swarm's HP drop below 25% of its total, the potency of its Bug-type maneuvers sees an augmentation by either 2-times or 1.5-times. Within Pokémon Conquest, Swarm functions by enhancing the user's Attack stat if the Pokémon's HP falls beneath ⅓ of its full capacity.

During the events of A Connoisseur's Revenge!, Burgundy informed Ash that his Leavanny, then still a Sewaddle, possessed Swarm as its Ability. Nevertheless, its activation was never observed while it was a Sewaddle. Volume 23 disclosed that Blue's Scizor also had Swarm as its Ability. The PokéSPedia data-book further revealed Swarm as the Ability for Moon's Grubbin and Giovanni's Beedrill. Additional Pokémon confirmed to possess Swarm include Violet's Lokix, Winona's Beautifly, Alder's Escavalier, Emmet's Durant, a Roughneck's Mothim, and a Scolipede under Colress's control. The Japanese designation for Swarm, むしのしらせ (Mushi no Shirase), which translates to 'Bug Notification,' originates from a Japanese idiom signifying 'foreboding,' 'premonition,' or 'gut feeling.' This linguistic connection mirrors the Ability's functionality, where the Pokémon, sensing an impending challenge, fortifies itself for battle. Notably, Swarm holds the distinction of being the inaugural Ability in animated media to be definitively attributed to a Pokémon without being shown in action.

Title: Rock Head (Ability)

Rock Head (Japanese: いしあたま Rock Head) is an Ability that made its debut in Generation III. For the Pokémon named "Rock Head," please refer to Bagon.

A Pokémon possessing Rock Head is immune to recoil damage, with specific exceptions being Struggle, Shadow Rush, and Shadow End. However, if a Pokémon with Rock Head executes a contact move that inflicts recoil upon a foe possessing either Lingering Aroma or Mummy, and Rock Head is subsequently overwritten by the opponent's Ability, the user will then incur recoil damage from that particular move. This Ability offers no protection against other forms of damage, such as crash damage, damage sustained from a Life Orb, or damage from a Jaboca or Rowap Berry. Furthermore, Rock Head does not prevent the user from fainting due to moves designed to cause self-fainting, nor does it mitigate the damage incurred when utilizing Steel Beam or Mind Blown.

A separate table details the moves impacted by Rock Head, alongside the Pokémon capable of possessing this Ability and mastering those moves. While certain moves are influenced by this Ability, they are presently not learnable by any Pokémon naturally endowed with Rock Head. Nevertheless, these moves can be accessed through mechanisms like Metronome, Mirror Move, Copycat, or Mimic. Alternatively, a Pokémon might utilize them after acquiring Rock Head via Abilities such as Trace or Receiver, or through moves like Skill Swap or Role Play.

Across Pokémon Black and White, Blue-Striped Basculin typically possess Reckless as their secondary standard Ability; however, a Blue-Striped Basculin with Rock Head was obtainable through an in-game trade specific to White version. In Pokémon Black 2 and White 2, wild Blue-Striped Basculin were found to have Rock Head as their secondary standard Ability, yet those bred within Black 2 and White 2 would instead inherit Reckless as their second standard Ability. By Generation VI, Blue-Striped Basculin uniformly featured Rock Head as their secondary standard Ability, and any Blue-Striped Basculin with Reckless transferred to Pokémon Bank subsequently had their Ability modified to Rock Head.

Within the internal code of Pokémon Legends: Arceus, Hisuian Growlithe and Arcanine are designated with Justified as their Hidden Ability, rather than Rock Head. Moving to media appearances, Y's Rhyhorn, nicknamed Rhyrhy, was depicted with Rock Head in Japan Mini-volume 6 of the X & Y arc. The PokéSPedia data-book documented Sapphire's Relicanth (Relly), Emerald's Sudowoodo, and Giovanni's Rhyhorn, Rhydon, and Aggron all possessing Rock Head. Additionally, Brock's Onix, Drake's Shelgon, and a Team Magma Grunt's Bagon were also shown to have Rock Head.

Title: Drought (Ability)

Known in Japanese as ひでり Drought, this Ability first appeared in Generation III. Before Generation V, it was Groudon's exclusive signature Ability. When a Pokémon possessing Drought enters battle, it immediately generates harsh sunlight. Originally, its effects mirrored the move Sunny Day but persisted for the entire battle, unless superseded by another weather condition. Currently, this summoned sunlight endures for only five turns, though it can be prolonged to eight turns if the Pokémon holds a Heat Rock. Should a Pokémon with Drought be deployed on the same turn as another Pokémon with a weather-altering Ability, the Ability of the slower Pokémon will take precedence over that of the faster Pokémon.

However, Drought will not activate in certain situations. It notably fails if the Groudon possessing it is poised for Primal Reversion; in such an instance, Primal Reversion occurs, and Groudon's Ability is replaced by Desolate Land, which then becomes the sole active Ability. Furthermore, Drought fails to activate if the battlefield is already experiencing heavy rain, extremely harsh sunlight, or strong winds.

Within the Pokémon Mystery Dungeon series, its mechanics vary by game. In Red Rescue Team, Blue Rescue Team, and Explorers of Time & Darkness, Drought converts the weather to Sunny upon entering a new floor, and subsequently every 36 turns thereafter. In Super Mystery Dungeon, the Ability makes the weather sunny when a floor is entered. This sunny state lasts for a specific number of turns (always a multiple of 66) before it dissipates. After the weather clears, Drought will reactivate after another set number of turns (also in multiples of 66).
