In the Pokémon Trading Card Game, Drought appeared as an attack on Groudon from the Legends Awakened expansion. When this attack is utilized, it enables the player to attach a maximum of two Fighting Energy cards from their hand to one of their Pokémon. A compilation of Pokémon cards featuring the Drought attack can be found below.

Title: Arena Trap (Ability)

The Ability Arena Trap, known in Japanese as ありじごく (Antlion), made its debut in Generation III. As long as the Pokémon with Arena Trap remains active in battle, it prevents all adjacent, grounded opposing Pokémon from either fleeing (including through Teleport) or being switched out. However, the effect does not apply during the turn a Pokémon with Arena Trap enters the battlefield; opposing Pokémon are not prevented from switching out on that turn. Flying-type opponents, Pokémon with the Levitate Ability, and those currently under the influence of Magnet Rise are unaffected by Arena Trap. A Pokémon can still switch out by using moves like Baton Pass, U-turn, Volt Switch, Flip Turn, or Parting Shot. Possessing a Shed Shell allows a Pokémon to switch out, though fleeing or using Teleport remains blocked. Conversely, a Pokémon with the Run Away Ability or holding a Smoke Ball can flee or Teleport, but cannot switch out. If a player chooses to flee with a fainted Pokémon instead of switching it out, they will not be affected by a wild Pokémon possessing Arena Trap. Owing to an oversight, opposing trainers will not attempt to switch out their Flying-type Pokémon or Pokémon with the Levitate Ability when encountering a player's Arena Trap, even though these Pokémon are immune. Additionally, Ghost-type Pokémon, even those normally considered grounded, are now immune to Arena Trap.

From Pokémon Emerald onward, having a Pokémon with Arena Trap in the lead party slot (even if fainted) doubles the wild Pokémon encounter rate for all species. This characteristic is also shared by the Abilities Illuminate and No Guard. However, in Emerald, Arena Trap does not influence the Rock Smash encounter rate. Within Pokémon Sword and Shield, this Ability's only effect is on the rate of hidden encounters. In Pokémon Scarlet and Violet, this Ability has no effect when outside of battle.

In Red Rescue Team and Blue Rescue Team, as well as Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky, an enemy Pokémon attacking a Pokémon with Arena Trap has a 12% chance of becoming immobilized. Flying-type Pokémon and those with the Levitate Ability remain unaffected by this. Within Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, any Pokémon positioned adjacent to a Pokémon with the Arena Trap Ability is prevented from moving, though they can still employ moves and items. Flying-type and Ghost-type Pokémon, along with those possessing the Levitate or Run Away Abilities, are unaffected.

The English, Spanish, and Italian names for Arena Trap incorporate wordplay. The term "Arena" simultaneously refers to a place of combat and, in Latin and Spanish, means sand. This dual meaning allows for interpretations like "a trap on the battlefield" or "sand trap," with the latter closely mirroring its name in other languages.

Title: Vital Spirit (Ability)

Known in Japanese as やるき (Willingness), Vital Spirit is an Ability first introduced during Generation III of the Pokémon games. This Ability prevents a Pokémon from experiencing sleep status conditions and from being affected by the move Yawn. A Pokémon possessing Vital Spirit cannot successfully use the move Rest. Should a Pokémon with Vital Spirit be asleep when in battle—for instance, if it acquired the Ability outside of battle, through an effect like Skill Swap, or was put to sleep by an Ability such as Mold Breaker—it will awaken.

If a Pokémon with this Ability enters a drowsy state from Yawn, its Ability will activate to keep it awake at the moment it would otherwise fall asleep. When a sleeping Pokémon that has Vital Spirit enters combat as a lead Pokémon, replaces a fainted Pokémon, or gains Vital Spirit via Trace, it will not be cured of its sleep ailment until it has taken a turn (e.g., used a move or switched out). However, it will be instantly relieved of sleep if it is switched in to take the place of another switched-out Pokémon, including instances involving Baton Pass.

If a sleeping Pokémon possesses Vital Spirit as its original Ability but not its currently active Ability, it will remain asleep upon being switched out. In a scenario where a sleeping Pokémon with this Ability is holding either a Chesto Berry or a Lum Berry, Vital Spirit will cure the Pokémon's sleep before the Berry can be consumed. When a sleeping Pokémon that has Vital Spirit enters a battle, its sleep status will be removed.

If a sleeping Pokémon has Vital Spirit as its original Ability but not its current one, and it is among the active Pokémon at the conclusion of a battle or is switched out, it will be cured of sleep without any message appearing. Should a sleeping Pokémon possess Vital Spirit as its original but not its current Ability, it will stay asleep when withdrawn from battle. If a Pokémon afflicted with sleep and holding a Chesto Berry or Lum Berry also has Vital Spirit, the Berry will be eaten to cure the sleep before Vital Spirit has an opportunity to activate. A sleeping Pokémon that obtains Vital Spirit through Evolution or the use of an Ability Capsule will not wake up until it is sent into battle.

Pokémon endowed with Vital Spirit are immune to being put to sleep by the Gentleman's Kirlia encountered in the Battle Pike, as well as by the Battle Arcade's game board. Commencing with Pokémon Emerald, if a Pokémon with Vital Spirit occupies the first slot in the party (even if it has fainted), there is a 50% probability that a wild Pokémon encountered will be at the upper end of its possible level range. This specific effect is shared with the Abilities Hustle and Pressure. In Pokémon Sword and Shield, Vital Spirit notably has no observable impact on the level range of wild Pokémon. Similarly, in Pokémon Scarlet and Violet, Vital Spirit does not seem to affect the level range of wild Pokémon. Norman's Vigoroth is known to possess Vital Spirit. This Ability is one of three that prevent sleep, alongside Insomnia and Sweet Veil.

Title: White Smoke (Ability)

Debuting in Generation III, White Smoke (Japanese: しろいけむり White Smoke) is an Ability. Torkoal held it as its signature Ability prior to Generation V and once more in Generation IX. This Ability prevents a Pokémon's stats from being reduced by the moves and Abilities of other Pokémon, including effects such as Scary Face, Intimidate, and Sticky Web. However, White Smoke does not offer protection against self-inflicted stat drops, for example, from moves like Overheat or Shell Smash. Its protective scope is limited exclusively to effects that lower stat *stages*; thus, it provides no defense against reductions caused by items like the Iron Ball, status conditions such as paralysis or burn, or the moves Guard Split or Power Split. Furthermore, White Smoke cannot prevent stat stages from being directly modified by Heart Swap, Guard Swap, Power Swap, Baton Pass, or Topsy-Turvy, nor does it block Spectral Thief from stealing (i.e., resetting) positive stat changes. Abilities and moves designed to bypass other Abilities, such as Mold Breaker, will disregard White Smoke's effects. Starting from Pokémon Emerald, if a Pokémon with White Smoke leads the party (even if fainted), the likelihood of encountering a wild Pokémon is decreased by 50%. This specific field effect is also shared by Quick Feet, Stench, and, since Generation VIII, Infiltrator. Exclusively within Pokémon Sword and Shield, this Ability's field effect changes to decrease the chance of hidden encounters by a significant 87.5%. In Pokémon Scarlet and Violet, White Smoke offers no benefits outside of battle.

Title: Pure Power (Ability)

Originating in Generation III, Pure Power (known in Japanese as ヨガパワー Yoga Power) is an Ability. This particular Ability is unique to Meditite and its evolved form, Medicham. A Pokémon possessing Pure Power will find its Attack stat effectively doubled. Furthermore, it extends this doubling effect to the user's effective Attack statistic when executing moves like Foul Play or Body Press, as these moves factor in Attack modifiers rather than stat stages. Damage incurred from self-inflicted confusion is likewise impacted by Pure Power. In the context of the Mystery Dungeon game series, Pure Power grants a 33.3% probability for a physical action (this includes basic attacks and items such as Iron Thorn) to inflict 50% more damage. However, the activation rate for this effect seems diminished within Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon specifically. Both Maylene's Meditite and Dahlia's Medicham have been confirmed to possess Pure Power as their designated Ability.

Title: Shell Armor (Ability)

An Ability first appearing in Generation III, Shell Armor (Japanese: シェルアーマー) grants a Pokémon immunity to critical hits. This protective effect prevents critical strikes even from attacks that are typically guaranteed to land one, such as Frost Breath, Storm Throw, Wicked Blow, Surging Strikes, and Flower Trick. Additionally, it nullifies critical hits from moves boosted by Merciless (should the Pokémon be poisoned) or by Laser Focus. In the internal data for Pokémon Legends: Arceus, Hisuian Samurott’s Hidden Ability is set as Shell Armor instead of Sharpness, and Hisuian Sliggoo and Goodra possess Overcoat as their Ability rather than Shell Armor. Tierno’s Crawdaunt was revealed to have Shell Armor as its Ability in the Japan Mini-volume 6 for the X & Y arc. The PokéSPedia data-book disclosed that Giovanni’s Cloyster utilized Shell Armor as its Ability. Lorelei’s Shellder and Wallace’s Clamperl were also confirmed to have Shell Armor as their Ability. Shell Armor, along with Protean, are the sole Abilities that multiple first partner Pokémon evolutionary lines can have as their Hidden Ability.

Title: Air Lock (Ability)

Known in Japanese as エアロック (Air Lock), this Ability made its debut in Generation III. It serves as Rayquaza's unique signature Ability. When a Pokémon with Air Lock is present in battle, all effects of weather are nullified, though the weather condition itself persists. Upon the entry of an Air Lock user onto the field, following its Ability announcement, the message "The effects of weather disappeared." will be displayed. Even when Air Lock is negating weather effects during extreme conditions such as extremely harsh sunlight, heavy rain, or strong winds, the moves Sunny Day, Rain Dance, Sandstorm, and Hail will still fail if used. Additionally, Abilities including Drought, Drizzle, Sand Stream, and Snow Warning will similarly fail to activate. Nevertheless, Air Lock successfully negates all other consequences of these specific weather conditions.

In the games Red Rescue Team and Blue Rescue Team, as well as Explorers of Time and Darkness, Air Lock functions by altering the current weather to Clear upon floor entry, and then every 36 turns thereafter. This Ability also establishes precedence over Drizzle, Drought, Sand Stream, and Snow Warning. Furthermore, it actively prevents the use of the moves Sunny Day, Rain Dance, Hail, and Sandstorm. Shifting to Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, Air Lock's mechanism involves nullifying the effects of weather directly, rather than changing the weather to Clear. In this context, Abilities like Drizzle and moves such as Rain Dance will still activate, yet their resultant weather effects will consistently be suppressed. Rayquaza is consistently found with Air Lock as its inherent Ability. Air Lock occupies the final position in the index number order among Generation III Abilities. This arrangement held true even before its renumbering, which occurred with Cacophony's removal in Generation IV.

Title: Tangled Feet (Ability)

The Ability known as Tangled Feet, or ちどりあし (Tottering Steps) in Japanese, was first introduced in Generation IV. Its primary function in the main series games dictates that when the Pokémon possessing it is afflicted with confusion, the accuracy of any incoming moves aimed at it is halved. A distinct effect is present in the Pokémon Mystery Dungeon titles Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky, where an active Tangled Feet provides a three-stage boost to evasion. In the manga's volume 40, it was revealed that Pearl's Chatot, Chatler, was endowed with this particular Ability.

Tangled Feet premiered as an Ability in the Pokémon Trading Card Game, appearing on Spinda from the Paradox Rift expansion. Within the TCG, this Ability permits the player to flip a coin if the Confused Pokémon bearing it is about to receive damage from an attack originating from an opponent's Pokémon; should the coin land on heads, that damage is nullified. A roster of Pokémon cards featuring the Tangled Feet Ability is provided below.

By index number order, Tangled Feet holds the distinction of being the initial Generation IV Ability. Prior to Generation VIII, every Pokémon capable of having this Ability was exclusively Normal-type, and all of them, with the sole exception of Spinda, additionally carried the Flying-type. Mr. Rime is presently the only Pokémon capable of possessing this Ability that is neither a Normal nor a Flying-type.

Title: Motor Drive (Ability)

This ability, known in Japanese as でんきエンジン (Electric Engine), made its debut in Generation IV. Before Generation V, Electivire exclusively possessed this particular ability. Should a Pokémon possessing Motor Drive be struck by an Electric-type attack, its Speed stat will rise by a single stage, and the attack itself will consequently deal no damage or other effect to that Pokémon. Multihit moves converted to Electric-type, for instance through effects like Electrify or Galvanize, will trigger Motor Drive a single time only. The activation of Motor Drive is prevented if the Pokémon is safeguarded from the incoming Electric-type attack. In instances where a Pokémon with Motor Drive holds a Cell Battery, the ability itself absorbs the Electric-type attack, thereby nullifying the Cell Battery's activation. It was disclosed that Volkner's Electivire possessed Motor Drive as its ability.

Title: Rivalry (Ability)

Known in Japanese as とうそうしん, or "Belligerence," Rivalry is an Ability that made its debut in Generation IV. When a creature possessing Rivalry unleashes an attack, the move's potency increases by 25% should both the target and the attacker be of the same gender. Conversely, if the target's gender opposes the attacker's, the move's strength diminishes by 25%. Furthermore, damage inflicted by confusion receives a boost from Rivalry. This Ability remains dormant, however, if either the attacking Pokémon or its foe possesses an unknown gender, exemplified by creatures like Porygon.

Within the *Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky* titles, along with the *Mystery Dungeon (WiiWare)* series, the Attack stat undergoes a modification: it decreases by one stage when the attacker's gender clashes with the target's, and conversely, it rises by one stage when their genders align. Shifting to *Gates to Infinity* and *Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon*, Rivalry enhances a move's power when striking a Pokémon that shares a common type with the user. Specifically in *Super Mystery Dungeon*, this Ability bestows a 20% power increment upon a move. Lysandre's Pyroar is noted to possess Rivalry as his Ability, according to the *PokéSPedia* data-book.

Title: Steadfast (Ability)

Known in Japanese as ふくつのこころ (Indomitable Heart), Steadfast debuted as an Ability in Generation IV. This particular Ability causes a Pokémon's Speed stat to rise by one stage each time it is affected by flinching. Importantly, this Speed enhancement only occurs if the corresponding flinching message is shown. Within the internal programming of Pokémon Legends: Arceus, Steadfast is designated as Kleavor's Hidden Ability, a departure from Sharpness. In this specific game, the Ability boosts a Pokémon's Movement Speed by one stage when it experiences being cross-eyed or cringing. For Pokémon UNITE, Steadfast functions as Lucario's unique Ability. When Lucario's HP drops to a low level, it gains both a protective shield and an acceleration in its movement speed. Several notable Pokémon have been confirmed to possess Steadfast. Sapphire's Gallade, named Kirly, was identified with this Ability in the PokéSPedia data-book. Additionally, Henry's Sirfetch'd, known as Lancelot, and Darach's Gallade also possess Steadfast as their Ability.

Title: Snow Cloak (Ability)

Snow Cloak (Japanese: ゆきがくれ, translated as Hidden in the Snow) is an Ability first introduced in Generation IV of the Pokémon series. While hail or snow is active, the precision of moves aimed at a Pokémon with this Ability is altered: it becomes 4/5 of its original value in Generation IV, or 3277/4096 from Generation V onward. A Pokémon equipped with Snow Cloak is safeguarded from any damage it would typically sustain from hail. Furthermore, when a Pokémon with Snow Cloak is positioned as the lead in the party (even if fainted) within a hail-affected area, the probability of encountering a wild Pokémon is diminished by 50%. It's worth noting that this encounter rate alteration does not apply in Pokémon Sword and Shield, nor does it affect rates in Pokémon Scarlet and Violet. In the *Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky* titles, Snow Cloak grants a two-stage increase to evasion when its effects are active. In related media, Platinum's Froslass was identified as having Snow Cloak as her Ability in volume 40. Additionally, two Beartic, each belonging to a Team Plasma Grunt, were also depicted with Snow Cloak as their Ability.

Title: Gluttony (Ability)

This entry details the Ability Gluttony. For information regarding the Ultra Beast identified as UB Gluttony in Japanese, known in English as Guzzlord (Pokémon), please refer to its respective page. Gluttony, an Ability first introduced in Generation IV, bears the Japanese name くいしんぼう (Glutton). It enables a Pokémon to consume Berries that typically activate at 25% HP or lower when their HP instead falls to 50% or below. This Ability does not, however, influence Berries triggered at different HP thresholds (such as the Oran Berry, which normally activates at 50% HP or less), nor does it affect Berries with non-HP-dependent activation conditions (examples include the Leppa Berry and Lum Berry). In Generation VII, Berries that may induce confusion, including the Figy Berry and Mago Berry, had their activation threshold adjusted from 50% HP or less to 25% HP or less, thereby making them susceptible to Gluttony's effect.

In the Explorers series and WiiWare titles of Pokémon Mystery Dungeon, Gluttony causes the Pokémon's Belly to deplete at twice the standard rate. Conversely, it also confers both benefits of the Survivalist IQ skill: a 50% likelihood of consuming Grimy Food without negative consequences, and a 10% increase in Belly restoration from all food consumption. Beginning with Pokémon Mystery Dungeon: Gates to Infinity, Gluttony's function shifted to restoring 25 HP whenever the Pokémon consumes any item, whether by eating or drinking. In Pokémon UNITE, Gluttony is Snorlax's unique Ability. It enhances the effects gained from eating Berries and boosts Snorlax's movement speed by 25% when it is heading towards Berries. Notable Pokémon revealed to possess Gluttony as their Ability include Cilan's Pansage, Chili's Pansear, Cress's Panpour, and Trish's Heatmor.

Title: Anger Point (Ability)

Known in Japanese as いかりのつぼ (Anger Pressure Point), Anger Point is a Pokémon characteristic first introduced in Generation IV. When a Pokémon equipped with this ability endures a critical hit, its Attack stat is instantly boosted to its maximum of +6 stages, irrespective of its current stage. This effect takes hold even when the Pokémon's substitute bears the impact. Conversely, there are instances where Anger Point does not activate if the Pokémon's substitute is damaged. Within volume 40 of the manga, Pearl's Tauros, known as Tauler, was confirmed to possess Anger Point as his ability. Chuck's Primeape is also depicted with Anger Point as his characteristic.

Title: Unburden (Ability)

Bearing the Japanese designation かるわざ Acrobatics, Unburden is an Ability that first appeared in Generation IV. Before the advent of Generation V, this Ability was exclusive to Drifloon and Drifblim. A Pokémon possessing Unburden will find its Speed stat doubled upon the consumption or loss of its held item. This activation condition encompasses items forcibly removed by moves such as Knock Off, Thief, Incinerate, Bug Bite, and Pluck, or when an Air Balloon bursts. However, if the Pokémon is initially not holding an item, or if its held item is exchanged for another, Unburden will not activate. The stat enhancement conferred by Unburden ceases if the Pokémon is withdrawn from battle, no longer possesses Unburden, or acquires a new held item. Should Unburden be recovered after being lost, any prior Speed increase will not be reinstated. In instances where Unburden is suppressed by Neutralizing Gas, no new boosts can be acquired; however, any existing boost will resume effectiveness once Neutralizing Gas is no longer active. Furthermore, while a Pokémon benefiting from Unburden does not possess a held item, it is capable of attacking or deploying moves two times within a single turn. Moves executed in this manner still only incur a deduction of 1 PP, notwithstanding their dual usage. Notably, N's Purrloin was documented as possessing Unburden in the PokéSPedia data-book.

Title: Heatproof (Ability)

The Ability known as Heatproof (Japanese: たいねつ Heatproof) was first introduced in Generation IV. Before Generation VIII, it was a unique trait belonging exclusively to Bronzor and Bronzong. When a Pokémon possessing Heatproof is targeted by a Fire-type attack, the attacking opponent's Attack or Special Attack stat is lowered by 50% during the damage calculation phase, which effectively halves the damage the Heatproof Pokémon sustains. In games prior to Generation IX, the power of the move itself was reduced by 50% instead. This Ability also diminishes the damage taken from the burn status condition by half. Specifically, burn damage changes from 1⁄16 to 1⁄32 of the Pokémon's maximum HP (or from 1⁄8 to 1⁄16 of max HP in games preceding Generation VII). Overall, Heatproof serves to reduce incoming damage from Fire-type attacks by either half or, in some cases, a quarter, while consistently halving burn damage.

Title: Simple (Ability)

The Ability Simple (Japanese: たんじゅん Simple) debuted in Generation IV. As of Generation IX, it is recognized as Numel's signature Ability. Simple's core function is to magnify the impact of stat stage modifications, effectively doubling their potency.

Previously, for instance, a move like Defense Curl would visually indicate a one-stage Defense increase on-screen, and the game system would internally register a +1 Defense stage for the user. However, this +1 stage's actual effect would match that of two standard stages, resulting in the user's Defense being doubled for damage computations. Correspondingly, if Tail Whip targeted a Pokémon with Simple, its Defense stat would decrease by one visible stage, yet its practical impact would mirror a two-stage drop. It's crucial to note that the overall influence of stat stages could not surpass their inherent maximum limits. For example, a Pokémon with Simple reaching +3 Defense stages would see its Defense multiplied by four in damage calculations, which is the equivalent benefit of six normal stages. While it could still employ Defense Curl up to three additional times to reach an internal +6 Defense stage, these extra boosts would yield no further practical advantage during combat calculations. This principle similarly applied to negative stage modifications, such as -3 Defense. Due to the unchanging nature of in-game stage values, a Pokémon employing Psych Up against a Simple-bearer would only replicate the internally saved stat stages, not their magnified effects. When Baton Pass was used to transfer stats to a Pokémon possessing Simple, the incoming stat stages would effectively double in power. Conversely, if a Simple Pokémon initiated Baton Pass to a recipient lacking Simple, the recipient would not inherit any stages with a magnified effect. Likewise, if a Pokémon enhanced its stats and subsequently gained Simple via a move such as Skill Swap, its previously established effective stat alterations would become doubled (provided they didn't exceed caps) for as long as it retained the Simple Ability.

Currently, Simple directly amplifies the numerical change in stat stages whenever a move, Ability, or item alters them, causing a doubling of the stage count. For instance, if Defense Curl is now used, the display will indicate a two-stage increase in the user's Defense, showing "sharply rose," and the game will record the user's Defense stage as +2, thereby doubling its Defense as anticipated. Similarly, a Tail Whip aimed at a Simple Pokémon will lead to its Defense decreasing directly by two stages. Consequently, moves like Defense Curl can only be utilized a maximum of three times before they will cease to function, as the user's Defense stage will have already reached the +6 limit. When Psych Up is employed against a target equipped with Simple, the now-doubled stat stages will be accurately copied. However, when Baton Pass is performed to a Pokémon that possesses Simple, the receiving Pokémon will *not* acquire stat stages with a doubled effect. If a Pokémon with Simple itself executes Baton Pass, it will successfully transfer the magnified stat stages. Furthermore, should a Pokémon increase its stats and subsequently gain Simple via a move like Skill Swap, its already existing stat adjustments will not be doubled, and no additional outcome will occur if it later loses Simple.

Moves executed by Pokémon possessing Mold Breaker, Teravolt, or Turboblaze will not have their stat-changing effects doubled if they target a Pokémon with Simple. The move Simple Beam possesses the capability to transform the target Pokémon's Ability into Simple. It's important to note that stat boosts stemming from the Z-Power effects of status Z-Moves remain unaffected by Simple. In contrast, stat increases generated by Extreme Evoboost and Clangorous Soulblaze *are* subject to Simple's modification. Within the narrative of Deduction Time, Hugh's Vibrava acquired the Simple Ability after being struck by Simple Beam, launched by a Team Plasma Grunt's Audino.

Title: Dry Skin (Ability)

The characteristic known as Dry Skin (Japanese: かんそうはだ, also Dry Skin) debuted as an Ability in Generation IV. Should a Pokémon possessing Dry Skin be struck by a Water-type attack, it regains one-quarter of its maximum HP, and the attack itself becomes ineffectual against it. (If the Pokémon's HP is already full when this occurs, the Water-type move will simply have no impact.) This restorative and nullifying effect will not activate if the Pokémon is protected from the incoming Water-type move. Even multi-hit techniques, such as Water Shuriken, will only trigger this particular effect a single time. Conversely, Fire-type assaults inflict an additional 25% damage upon Pokémon with the Dry Skin ability. While it is raining, a Pokémon with Dry Skin recovers one-eighth of its maximum HP at the conclusion of every turn. During periods of harsh sunlight, a Dry Skin Pokémon forfeits one-eighth of its maximum HP at each turn's end. Should a Pokémon holding an Absorb Bulb or Luminous Moss also possess Dry Skin, the ability's absorption of the Water-type move takes precedence, thereby preventing the held item's effect from activating.

Within the Pokémon Mystery Dungeon: Explorers of Time and Darkness and Pokémon Mystery Dungeon: Explorers of Sky series, Dry Skin-laden Pokémon experience a loss of 3 HP every 9 to 10 turns under Sunny conditions and take 50% extra damage from Fire-type moves. Water-type attacks restore HP equivalent to the damage they would have inflicted. Furthermore, in rainy weather, their passive HP regeneration rate is boosted fourfold, increasing from a baseline of 0.5% of maximum HP per turn to 2% per turn.

From Pokémon Mystery Dungeon: Gates to Infinity and subsequent titles, Dry Skin hinders inherent HP recovery. Pokémon with this ability now deplete 1 HP every 10 turns when the weather is Sunny, and Fire-type moves inflict 1.2 times their usual damage. Receiving a Water-type move results in the Pokémon restoring 20% of its maximum HP, and their natural HP replenishment rate is doubled during rain.

In volume 42 of the manga, Crystal's Parasect, known as Parasee, was shown to possess Dry Skin as her designated Ability.

Title: Download (Ability)

Known as ダウンロード (Download) in Japanese, this Ability made its debut in Generation IV. It was the exclusive signature Ability of the Porygon evolutionary line prior to Generation V and is once again so in Generation IX. When a Pokémon with Download enters battle, or when the Ability is gained, it provides a one-stage increase to either its Attack or Special Attack stat. This enhancement is contingent on which of the opponent's current defensive stats is lower. Should the foe's current Defense be less than its current Special Defense stat, Attack is raised; otherwise, Special Attack receives the boost. If the Defense and Special Defense stats are tied, Download defaults to increasing Special Attack.

When determining the foe's lowest defensive stat, Download accounts for current stat stages and the influence of Power Trick. However, it disregards the effects of held items, other Abilities like Marvel Scale, or the move Wonder Room. In battles with multiple opponents, Download calculates the average Defense and Special Defense by summing the stats of all opposing Pokémon, then identifies the lower of these two average values. Historically, Download did not factor in the defensive stats of opponents protected by a Substitute; consequently, if all adversaries were behind Substitutes, the Ability would not activate. Currently, however, Download *does* take into account the defensive stats of opponents even when they are shielded by a Substitute. The PokéSPedia data-book reveals that Blake's Genesect possesses Download as its Ability. A defining characteristic of all Pokémon capable of utilizing this Ability is their origin as either human-engineered or human-modified creations.

Title: Iron Fist (Ability)

This entry pertains to the Ability 'Iron Fist'. For the Paradox Pokémon bearing a similar name, please consult Iron Hands (Pokémon). Debuting in Generation IV, Iron Fist (Japanese: てつのこぶし Iron Fist) is an Ability. Prior to Generation V, it was exclusively Hitmonchan's signature Ability. This Ability causes a 20% increase in the power of designated punching moves (with the exact multiplier being approximated as 4915/4096 from Generation V onward). This power augmentation is cumulative with the Punching Glove, yielding a total power increase of 32%.

The moves positively affected by Iron Fist are enumerated in the table that follows, which also identifies the Pokémon that can possess Iron Fist and concurrently learn these moves. While the moves listed subsequently *are* affected by Iron Fist, no Pokémon with the Ability can presently learn them. However, their use remains possible through Metronome, Mirror Move, Copycat, or Mimic. They may also be employed by a Pokémon that gained Iron Fist via Abilities like Trace or Receiver, or moves such as Skill Swap or Role Play.

In the Pokémon Mystery Dungeon titles Explorers of Time and Darkness and Explorers of Sky, Iron Fist provides a more substantial 50% power increase to affected moves. Conversely, in Pokémon Mystery Dungeon: Gates to Infinity and Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, the power of punching moves sees a 20% increase.

The Ability Iron Fist was revealed for Crystal's Hitmonchan, Monlee, in volume 42. Similarly, Shauntal's Golurk was shown to have Iron Fist as its Ability. And Sun's Crabominable, Loot, was also confirmed to possess Iron Fist as his Ability.

Rock Smash, although described as a "punch," notably receives no power augmentation from Iron Fist. The English names of the following moves can be misleading, as their power is not boosted by Iron Fist: Sucker Punch, owing to its Japanese name 「ふいうち」 (Surprise Attack); and Poison Jab, known as 「どくづき」 (Poison Stab) in Japanese. Hitmonchan can learn Dizzy Punch exclusively by leveling up in Pokémon: Let's Go, Pikachu! and Let's Go, Eevee!. However, Iron Fist cannot provide a boost to this move in those specific games, as Abilities are not featured in their gameplay.

Title: Poison Heal (Ability)

Known in Japanese as ポイズンヒール (Poison Heal), this Ability first appeared in Generation IV. Prior to Generation V, Shroomish and Breloom exclusively possessed Poison Heal as their signature Ability. When a Pokémon with this Ability is afflicted by either standard poison or bad poison, it restores 1/8 of its maximum HP at the end of each turn, rather than suffering damage. Being badly poisoned does not change the rate of HP recovery for a Pokémon with Poison Heal. However, the cumulative nature of bad poison means its counter continues to increase each turn, even while Poison Heal is active. Consequently, if Gastro Acid is used on a Pokémon that has been badly poisoned for five turns, it will then begin taking poison damage at a rate of 5/16 of its max HP, instead of the initial 1/16. Should a poisoned Pokémon with Poison Heal be affected by Heal Block, it will neither regain HP nor incur damage from the poison. Poison Heal has no effect when outside of battle; therefore, in Generation IV, poison would still harm a Pokémon in the overworld, regardless of it having this Ability. Fundamentally, when the Pokémon is poisoned or badly poisoned, it recovers HP instead of losing it. In Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, a Pokémon with Poison Heal, if poisoned or badly poisoned, regains 0.5% of its maximum HP each turn, in addition to an extra 6 HP every ten turns.

Title: Adaptability (Ability)

Adaptability (Japanese: てきおうりょく Adaptability) is an attribute that first appeared in Generation IV. This Ability enhances a Pokémon's Same-Type Attack Bonus (STAB), elevating its multiplier from the usual 1.5 to 2. When a Pokémon with Adaptability undergoes Terastallization, the Ability's influence is then restricted solely to moves that match its Tera Type. Should the Pokémon's Tera Type align with one of its original typings, its STAB factor climbs to 2.25; conversely, if it's a new type, the multiplier stands at 2. Notably, Stellar-type moves are entirely unaffected by Adaptability.

For the Pokémon Mystery Dungeon titles Explorers of Time and Darkness, as well as Explorers of Sky, Adaptability operates by increasing STAB from 1.5× to 2×. However, in Pokémon Mystery Dungeon: Gates to Infinity and Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, the applied boost is adjusted, changing the STAB multiplier from 1.2× to 1.5×.

In Pokémon UNITE, Adaptability functions as the distinct trait of an Eevee preparing to evolve into a Sylveon. Upon dealing or receiving damage, this Eevee's Special Attack is temporarily raised by 5% for 1.5 seconds, with this effect accumulating up to 4 times. This particular Ability then transitions to Pixilate upon evolution.

It has also been revealed that Vee, Red's Eevee, possesses Adaptability as its designated Ability. Alphabetically, Adaptability stands as the very first Ability.

Title: Skill Link (Ability)

The Ability named Skill Link (スキルリンク Skill Link in Japanese) first appeared in Generation IV. Until Generation V, it served as the signature Ability for Shellder and Cloyster. Skill Link causes multistrike moves that usually hit a variable number of times to invariably land five strikes. Should Skill Link be removed mid-move (for example, by Mummy), the Pokémon using the move will nonetheless land the maximum number of strikes for that ongoing turn. The Ability impacts Triple Kick and Triple Axel, which ordinarily can hit up to three times, and Population Bomb, capable of striking up to ten times. When a Pokémon with this Ability utilizes any of these moves, a single accuracy check determines the success of all strikes, instead of requiring individual checks per hit. Details are also available regarding the moves impacted by Skill Link, as well as the Pokémon that can possess Skill Link and acquire those moves. While the following moves are influenced by this Ability, no Pokémon currently possessing Skill Link can learn them. Nonetheless, they can be invoked through moves such as Metronome, Mirror Move, Copycat, or Mimic. Alternatively, a Pokémon could employ them after acquiring Skill Link via an Ability like Trace or Receiver, or a move like Skill Swap or Role Play.

Title: Hydration (Ability)

The Ability Hydration, recognized in Japanese as うるおいボディ Moist Body, was first introduced in Generation IV. At the conclusion of each turn, provided it is raining, Hydration eradicates any non-volatile status condition affecting the Pokémon. When a status condition is nullified, this process takes place preceding the damage calculation for burn or poison on that particular turn. A Pokémon rendered drowsy by the move Yawn will not drift into sleep until Hydration has already activated, meaning any potential healing for that sleep will be postponed until the conclusion of the following turn. Notably, a Manaphy and Phione under Diamond's care were discovered to possess Hydration as their inherent Ability.

Title: Solar Power (Ability)

Known in Japanese as サンパワー (Sun Power), Solar Power is an Ability that was first introduced in Generation IV. While under harsh sunlight, this Ability grants a 1.5× boost to the Pokémon's Special Attack. However, this power depletes 1/8 of its maximum HP at each turn's end, so long as the harsh sunlight continues. Within Explorers of Time, Darkness and Sky, Solar Power elevates the Pokémon's Special Attack by one stage during sunny conditions. The Pokémon sheds 3HP every 9-10 turns, yet its natural HP recovery remains unaffected. For Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, Solar Power significantly enhances the Pokémon's Special Attack when the weather is sunny or in harsh sunlight. Furthermore, the Pokémon surrenders 1 HP every 10 turns and cannot naturally restore its health.

Title: Quick Feet (Ability)

Known in Japanese as はやあし Quick Feet, this Ability first appeared in Generation IV. When a Pokémon possessing Quick Feet is afflicted by a status condition, its Speed stat receives a 50% boost. Furthermore, paralysis does not impose its usual Speed reduction on a Pokémon with this Ability.

Placing a Pokémon with Quick Feet in the lead position of the party (even if it has fainted) reduces the likelihood of encountering wild Pokémon by half. This particular effect is also shared with the Abilities Stench and White Smoke, as well as Infiltrator from Generation VIII onwards. Exclusively in Pokémon Sword and Shield, Quick Feet alters this effect to instead lower the probability of hidden encounters by a substantial 87.5%. However, in Pokémon Scarlet and Violet, this Ability provides no out-of-battle benefits.

During Generation V, Venipede, Whirlipede, and Scolipede possessed Quick Feet as their designated Hidden Ability. This was obtainable by befriending Scolipede within Pleasant Forest when using an account linked to Pokémon Black 2 and White 2. By Generation VI, their Hidden Ability was altered to Speed Boost instead. Should the Pokémon suffer a detrimental status condition, its Travel Speed experiences an increase of one level, consequently rendering it immune to slowdown effects.

Green's Granbull was shown to possess Quick Feet as its Ability in volume 24. Despite Quick Feet being introduced in Generation IV, no Pokémon originating from that generation can naturally have it.

Title: Normalize (Ability)

Known in Japanese as ノーマルスキン (Normal Skin), Normalize is an Ability that debuted in Generation IV. It serves as the signature capability for both Skitty and Delcatty. This Ability causes any move executed by the Pokémon possessing it, even status moves, to convert to the Normal type, superseding their original typing. If Thunder Wave becomes Normal-type via Normalize, it gains the capacity to strike Ground-type Pokémon; conversely, against Ghost-type Pokémon, its effect is restricted to those holding a Ring Target or subjected to Foresight or Odor Sleuth. When Flying Press is altered by Normalize, its damage output becomes a dual Normal/Flying type. Even if Freeze-Dry is impacted by Normalize, it retains its super-effective property against Water-type Pokémon. Should a Pokémon with Normalize be subjected to Electrify or Ion Deluge, its moves will instead become Electric-type, overriding Normalize's effect. Although previously affected by Normalize, moves like Hidden Power, Weather Ball, Natural Gift, and Judgment are no longer influenced by it. Techno Blast, likewise, remains immune to Normalize's type alteration. Normal-type attacks unleashed by the Ability-user now enjoy a 20% power increase (specifically, a multiplier of 4915/4096), with the exception of moves that are immune to Normalize's effects. This enhanced power applies equally to moves inherently Normal-type and those converted to Normal-type by Normalize. Furthermore, Multi-Attack, Terrain Pulse, and all damaging Z-Moves are not modified by Normalize. Within Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky, and the Mystery Dungeon (WiiWare) titles, Normalize extends its effect to transform the standard attack into a Normal-type maneuver. Moreover, thrown items inflict Normal-type damage. This exception excludes items causing fixed damage, though it notably impacts Vacuum-Cut. Shauna's Skitty, Neko, was identified as possessing Normalize as its Ability in the X & Y arc's Japan Mini-volume 6 and Viz Mini-volume 12.

Title: Sniper (Ability)

Originating in Generation IV, the Ability Sniper (スナイパー in Japanese) enhances critical hits. Normally dealing 2× damage, critical hits under Sniper now instead inflict 3× damage. While initially unable to be triggered by the move Beat Up, Sniper's effect can now be activated by it. Current game mechanics dictate that Sniper no longer directly adjusts the critical hit multiplier. Instead, it applies a 1.5× multiplier to the damage *after* a critical hit has occurred, placing this calculation among the 'final' damage modifiers. Consequently, with the general critical hit multiplier having been lowered to 1.5×, critical hits under Sniper effectively deliver 2.25× damage overall, maintaining a similar impact. Within the Pokémon Mystery Dungeon series, specifically in Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky, the Sniper Ability causes critical hits to inflict 2× damage, contrasting with the standard 1.5×. In the Pokémon manga, Yellow's Seadra possesses Sniper as its designated Ability. Every Pokémon capable of having Sniper also has access to the move Focus Energy or at least one attack that boasts an elevated critical-hit chance.

Title: Magic Guard (Ability)

Known in Japanese as マジックガード (Magic Guard), this Ability made its debut in Generation IV. Before Generation V, the Clefairy evolutionary family exclusively possessed Magic Guard as their signature skill. A Pokémon with Magic Guard is protected from taking indirect damage, yet it can still be subjected to status conditions that would normally inflict this kind of harm. Beyond the damage prevention, secondary effects like being trapped while held or the Attack stat reduction caused by a burn are not negated by Magic Guard. The ability effectively negates damage from various sources, including environmental effects like weather, status conditions such as poison and burn, and move-based effects like Curse and Leech Seed. It also blocks damage from entry hazards, recoil (with the notable exception of Struggle), crash damage, and the self-inflicted harm from using Mind Blown or Steel Beam. When holding a Life Orb, a Pokémon with Magic Guard will still benefit from the item's power increase without suffering the usual HP loss. However, Magic Guard offers no protection against confusion damage, nor does it interfere with direct alterations to a Pokémon's HP that aren't classified as damage, such as those caused by Pain Split, Belly Drum, Substitute creation, or a Ghost-type Pokémon sacrificing its own HP for Curse. Despite its protective nature, moves that cause the user to faint, as well as Destiny Bond and Perish Song, remain capable of causing a Magic Guard Pokémon to faint. Should a Pokémon with Magic Guard become badly poisoned, the ability will still prevent the escalating poison damage, yet the cumulative poison counter itself will continue to increment each turn. Therefore, if Magic Guard is later suppressed or replaced, the Pokémon will immediately start taking poison damage commensurate with the accumulated value of the counter. The Gulp Missile counterattack's damage is blocked by Magic Guard, though it does not prevent the associated Defense stat reduction or paralysis effect. When a Pokémon possessing Magic Guard strikes an opponent holding a Jaboca Berry with a physical attack, or a Rowap Berry with a special attack, the attacking Pokémon will neither consume the berry nor take any damage from its effect. Historically, Magic Guard prevented a paralyzed Pokémon from being unable to use a move, though its Speed was still reduced. Similarly, it once prevented a Pokémon from being poisoned by Toxic Spikes. However, Magic Guard no longer grants immunity to being fully paralyzed, nor does it prevent poisoning from Toxic Spikes. Despite this, it continues to prevent any damage from the resulting poison, and the Speed reduction from paralysis is still applied. Outside of combat, Magic Guard provides no benefits. Consequently, in Generation IV, a poisoned Pokémon with Magic Guard would still suffer damage while traversing the overworld. This specific interaction became irrelevant in Generation V and subsequent titles, as poison ceased to inflict field damage altogether. The PokéSPedia data-book confirmed that White's Duosion, nicknamed Solly, possessed Magic Guard as his Ability.

Title: No Guard (Ability)

An Ability known as No Guard (ノーガード in Japanese) made its debut in Generation IV. Prior to the advent of Generation V, this particular Ability was exclusive to the Machop evolutionary family. Any move, whether executed by or targeting a Pokémon possessing No Guard, is guaranteed to hit. Furthermore, it permits attacks to land on Pokémon during the otherwise protected turns of moves like Fly, Bounce, Dive, Dig, Shadow Force, Sky Drop, and Phantom Force. However, it does not override inherent type-based immunities. Interestingly, a Tatsugiri benefiting from a Dondozo's Commander Ability, by being concealed within its mouth, remains unaffected by No Guard's influence.

During the Diamond and Pearl games, a specific glitch allowed a No Guard Pokémon to bypass Protect or Detect, provided the attacking move had an accuracy rating below 100%. The probability of penetrating these defensive moves was inversely proportional to the move's original accuracy: lower accuracy meant a greater chance of success. Consequently, moves with an inherent 100% accuracy, such as Swift or Aerial Ace, would invariably be blocked by Protect or Detect under these conditions. Notably, one-hit knockout moves were an exception to this rule and would still be safeguarded against. This particular battle mechanic was an unintended bug, later rectified in Platinum, restoring Protect and Detect's effectiveness against most moves.

Beyond combat, placing a Pokémon with No Guard at the front of the party (even if it has fainted) doubles the likelihood of encountering wild Pokémon by 100%. This effect is shared with abilities like Arena Trap and Illuminate. In Pokémon Sword and Shield, No Guard's out-of-battle utility was limited solely to boosting random encounter rates. However, in Pokémon Scarlet and Violet, No Guard holds no impact whatsoever outside of combat. Kubfu was initially programmed with No Guard as its Hidden Ability in Pokémon Scarlet and Violet versions 1.0.0 through 1.1.0. This particular coding, however, was subsequently removed in version 1.2.0. It's important to note that Kubfu could not be legitimately acquired in Scarlet and Violet during that timeframe.

The Ability No Guard causes the Pokémon to disregard any alterations, whether positive or negative, to both its own and its target's accuracy and evasiveness stats. Within the Pokémon Mystery Dungeon: Explorers series, this means the Pokémon's own Concentrator and Clutch Performer IQ skills are rendered ineffective. In Pokémon UNITE, No Guard serves as the Ability for both Honedge and Doublade. It results in the user both inflicting and taking increased damage. Upon evolving into Aegislash, this Ability transforms into Stance Change.

Notably, a Pokémon possessing No Guard can execute one-hit knockout moves with guaranteed success. This capability likely explains the various game design choices made to prevent the Machop evolutionary line from simultaneously having No Guard and the move Fissure, a move it could learn back in Generation I. While Machop's family can indeed carry Fissure forward into Generation VII and Pokémon Sword and Shield, such Pokémon are restricted to their Hidden Ability, Steadfast, due to Poké Transporter assigning Hidden Abilities to all Virtual Console transfers. Despite the advent of items like the Ability Capsule and Ability Patch, these tools were designed in Generations VII and VIII to prevent switching a Hidden Ability to a regular one. Pokémon Scarlet and Violet introduced the capability to use an Ability Patch to revert a Pokémon's Hidden Ability, allowing it to be sent back to Sword and Shield retaining both the modified Ability and its original moveset; however, the Machop line is absent from Scarlet and Violet. Ultimately, even if the Machop line were to be included in a future title where the Ability Patch is available, not only would Pokémon HOME not allow Fissure to be transferred to those new games, and furthermore, Pokémon moved past Pokémon Legends: Z-A are permanently unable to return to Sword and Shield.

Title: Stall (Ability)

Known in Japanese as あとだし (Going After), Stall is an Ability that first appeared in Generation IV. It serves as Sableye's signature Ability. A Pokémon possessing this Ability executes its move at the very end of its respective priority bracket, irrespective of its Speed stat or the effects of Trick Room. Should Stall be acquired, removed, or nullified during a turn, its influence on action order will not manifest until the commencement of the subsequent turn. The activation of a Pokémon's held Quick Claw negates Stall's effect on that Pokémon's turn. When several Pokémon all have the Stall Ability and employ moves sharing the same priority level, the Pokémon possessing the lowest Speed stat will act first, even under the influence of Trick Room. A Pokémon imbued with Stall will take its turn ahead of any Pokémon equipped with a Lagging Tail or a Full Incense. However, if a Pokémon having Stall is also holding either a Lagging Tail or a Full Incense, Stall's influence on turn order is nullified. In scenarios where multiple Pokémon possess Stall, or are holding a Lagging Tail or Full Incense, and execute moves of identical priority, their relative turn order is determined by Speed (with the order inverted while Trick Room is active). Within the Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky titles, as well as the Mystery Dungeon (WiiWare) series, an AI-driven Pokémon with Stall will only contemplate an offensive action if an enemy is positioned adjacently. This specific constraint does not extend to other AI decision-making; the Pokémon can still engage distant foes using ranged moves, provided at least one adversary is in an adjacent tile.

Title: Technician (Ability)

The Ability known as Technician (Japanese: テクニシャン Technician) made its debut in Generation IV. It augments the power of attacks with a base power of 60 or less, boosting them by 50%. When dealing with moves that possess fluctuating power, Technician's effect is contingent on the move's power being 60 or below at the moment of execution. For instance, moves like Low Kick and Grass Knot only receive a Technician enhancement if the adversary's weight does not exceed 110.0 lbs. (49.9 kg). This bonus does not extend to damage inflicted by confusion, which consistently maintains a power of 40. Nevertheless, if a Pokémon harms itself because of confusion stemming from disobedience, the power of that self-inflicted damage rises to 60. Curiously, despite Struggle possessing a base power of 60 or lower, and Beat Up striking with a base power of 10 per hit, neither of these moves received a Technician boost during Generation IV. The Technician effect is calculated subsequent to base power modifications from abilities like double base power moves (e.g., Gust and Pursuit), Charge, and Helping Hand; consequently, if these modifiers elevate a move's base power beyond 60, Technician's enhancement is nullified. Subsequently, Technician ceased to affect any form of confusion damage, including that caused by disobedience. Both Struggle and Beat Up are now subject to Technician's augmentation. While Technician no longer factors in base power modifiers, it continues to evaluate variable base power moves (such as Rollout, Ice Ball, Gust, Acrobatics), requiring them to be 60 or below for the boost to activate. A subsequent table details the moves influenced by Technician, alongside the Pokémon capable of possessing Technician and learning these specific moves. Despite being affected by this Ability, the listed moves can typically only be acquired by Smeargle via Sketch (excluding Struggle, Chatter, and Generation VIII moves), or utilized by a Pokémon that gained Technician through means like Skill Swap or Role Play.

Within the Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky series, Technician magnifies the damage of attacks with a base power of 4 or less by 50%, accounting for power increases from Ginseng and Space Globe. Another table specifies the moves impacted by Technician in these particular games, as well as the Pokémon that possess Technician and learn those moves. Even though these subsequent moves are affected by the Ability, they are exclusively learnable by Smeargle after it has Sketched them. In Gates to Infinity and Super Mystery Dungeon, Technician grants a damage boost to moves with a base power of 5 or lower, considering enhancements from move levels, Power Manuals, and Power Drinks. However, in Rescue Team DX, Technician similarly amplifies damage for moves with a base power of 5 or less, though it disregards base power boosts from move levels or Power Drinks.

Gold's Ambipom, named Aibo, was disclosed to possess Technician as his Ability in volume 42. In the PokéSPedia data-book, Sun's Alolan Meowth, Cent, was confirmed to have Technician as his Ability. Additionally, Chuck's Hitmontop and Geoff's Cinccino were also shown to have Technician as their respective Abilities.

Title: Leaf Guard (Ability)

An Ability introduced in Generation IV, Leaf Guard (Japanese: リーフガード Leaf Guard) offers a specific safeguard. While harsh sunlight persists, it prevents the Pokémon from being afflicted by non-volatile status conditions, including those caused by items such as Flame Orb and Toxic Orb, and also blocks Yawn. However, it does not influence any status conditions the Pokémon had already acquired prior to the weather being established. Should harsh sunlight be generated in the exact turn when a Pokémon, made drowsy by Yawn, would otherwise fall asleep, the Ability will activate to ensure the Pokémon remains awake. Rest typically allows the user to fall asleep for two turns. Nevertheless, if a Pokémon with Leaf Guard attempts to use Rest while under harsh sunlight, the move will now fail.

Title: Klutz (Ability)

The Ability Klutz (Japanese: ぶきよう Clumsy) debuted in Generation IV. Before Generation V, it served as the exclusive signature Ability for Buneary and Lopunny.

Klutz prevents a Pokémon's held item from applying its effects. This Ability, however, does not nullify item effects pertaining to prize money, experience points, effort values, or friendship; examples include the Amulet Coin, Lucky Egg, Power Weight, or Soothe Bell. The Speed stat reduction imposed by the Macho Brace and Power items (such as the Power Bracer) also remains unaffected by Klutz.

**Regarding specific interactions, prior to Generation VII:**
The Iron Ball's Speed drop was not negated, though its grounding effect was. Klutz prevented a Pokémon from using Natural Gift, yet it did not prevent the use of Fling. If a Pokémon with Klutz utilized Bug Bite or Pluck, it would consume the target's held Berry without gaining the Berry's effects.

**Starting from Generation VII:**
Klutz now prevents the Pokémon with this Ability from using Fling. Should a Klutz Pokémon use Bug Bite or Pluck, it will now receive the effects of a consumed held Berry. All effects of the Iron Ball are now negated by Klutz. Nevertheless, Klutz still does not negate the Speed drop from the Macho Brace and Power items.

If a Pokémon with Klutz uses Judgment, Techno Blast, or Multi-Attack, the move will default to Normal-type.

Should a Pokémon already be locked into a move by a held Choice item (Choice Scarf, Choice Specs, Choice Band), if that Choice item is removed while its effects are negated by Klutz, the Pokémon will become subject to the Choice item lock glitch.

Klutz does not impede the form-changing effects of items, but it still negates their other associated effects. For example, if a Giratina holding a Griseous Orb has Klutz, it will remain in its Origin Forme but will not receive the 20% power boost for its Dragon- and Ghost-type moves. Klutz also does not prevent a Pokémon from Mega Evolving with a Mega Stone, undergoing Primal Reversion with the Red Orb or Blue Orb, or utilizing a Z-Move with a Z-Crystal.

Klutz has no impact outside of battle. Items such as the Everstone, Cleanse Tag, Scarves, and Soothe Bell are unaffected by Klutz.

**In Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon:**
Klutz additionally prevents the Pokémon from utilizing or throwing items. This particular restriction does not apply if the Pokémon serves as the team leader. Furthermore, Klutz protects the Pokémon from being afflicted by the puppet status condition.

Title: Mold Breaker (Ability)

An Ability first introduced in Generation IV, Mold Breaker (Japanese: かたやぶり, literally "Mold-Breaking") allows a Pokémon to ignore the effects of all ignorable Abilities on other Pokémon when executing a move. This disregard applies for the entire duration of that move's execution. Ignorable Abilities are typically those that might negatively impact a move's success rate, damage output, or overall effect when present on a target or its allies. For instance, a Pokémon with Mold Breaker utilizing Earthquake can successfully strike opponents that possess Levitate.

When a Pokémon with Mold Breaker entered battle in Generation IV, the message "<name> has Mold Breaker!" was displayed. From Generation V onwards, this message was updated to read "<name> breaks the mold!"

Regarding its specific interactions: Flower Gift is capable of increasing the Attack of allies with Mold Breaker, even if the Mold Breaker ally is attacking the Pokémon that provides Flower Gift. However, a Pokémon with Mold Breaker attacking does not inherently receive an Attack boost due to an ally's Flower Gift.

If a Pokémon with Mold Breaker employs a move that forces the target to switch out (such as Roar or Dragon Tail), any ignorable Abilities are overlooked when entry hazards are applied to the incoming Pokémon. As an example, a Pokémon with Levitate forced into the field by such a move will be affected by both Spikes and Toxic Spikes (unless other factors keep it ungrounded). Furthermore, a Poison-type Pokémon with Levitate switched in this way will still remove Toxic Spikes from its side of the field (assuming it is not ungrounded for other reasons).

Mold Breaker now ignores Aura Break but no longer bypasses the effects of Dark Aura and Fairy Aura.

In an observed discrepancy from Pokémon the Series: Diamond and Pearl, Roark's Rampardos's Mold Breaker was depicted as preventing paralysis from Ash's Pikachu's and Paul's Elekid's Static, despite Static remaining unaffected by Mold Breaker within the game mechanics.

Title: Super Luck (Ability)

Initially appearing in Generation IV, the Ability known as Super Luck (きょううん in Japanese, translating to Good Luck) was introduced. As of Generation IX, Super Luck serves as the signature Ability for Murkrow and Honchkrow. The Ability boosts the critical hit ratio by one stage, quantitatively increasing moves' critical hit chances by 15%.

Solely in Pokémon HeartGold and SoulSilver, if a Super Luck Pokémon breaks a rock and uncovers an item, the game's item selection shifts to the one positioned below it in the list, unless the 5% rarity item was due. This mechanism ensures the most common item is never found. When a Super Luck Pokémon leads the party (even if fainted), the odds of encountering wild Pokémon holding items improve, changing from 50%/5% to 60%/20%. This item-finding benefit is also shared with the Compound Eyes Ability. However, in Pokémon Scarlet and Violet, Super Luck offers no effect on items carried by wild Pokémon.

Within the realm of Pokémon UNITE, Absol possesses Super Luck as its Ability, granting its user a 15% surge in critical hit ratio. Y's Absol, Solsol, was confirmed to have Super Luck in Japan Mini-volume 6 for the X & Y arc. The PokéSPedia data-book revealed N's Pidove also displayed Super Luck, and Hugh's Pidove has likewise been shown to possess this Ability. The prevalence of this Ability among bird-like Pokémon is potentially rooted in Japanese cultural beliefs, where avian creatures often symbolize good fortune.

Title: Aftermath (Ability)

The Ability known as Aftermath, whose Japanese designation is ゆうばく (Induced Explosion), first appeared in Generation IV. Should a Pokémon possessing Aftermath be knocked out by damage from a move that makes physical contact, the attacking Pokémon will sustain damage equivalent to ¼ of its own maximum HP. This effect, however, will not trigger if a Pokémon with Damp is active on the battlefield or if the Pokémon was defeated by a move that dealt direct damage. In the Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky series, as well as the Mystery Dungeon (WiiWare) games, a Pokémon's fainting can occasionally unleash an explosion similar to Selfdestruct. This detonation inflicts half HP reduction on team members, 40 HP reduction on hostile Pokémon, and destroys various items. Conversely, in Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, Aftermath activates when the user is struck by a contact move from an adjacent foe, dealing damage equal to 25% of the assailant's maximum HP. Furthermore, within Super Mystery Dungeon, Aftermath will also cause the Pokémon to explode if it is hit by another explosion. This secondary blast inflicts damage equivalent to 25% of the Pokémon's maximum HP to itself and surrounding Pokémon, while simultaneously obliterating all walls, items, and special tiles. Fantina's Drifblim is confirmed to have Aftermath as its Ability. In the manga "Hareta's Heart's Desire," Flint's Drifblim was revealed to possess this Ability, though Hareta's Misdreavus subsequently employed Skill Swap on it. It's notable that every Pokémon capable of having Aftermath can also learn the move Explosion. This strong resemblance between Aftermath and Explosion is more pronounced in languages other than English, particularly in the original Japanese name, ゆうばく Yūbaku (Induced Explosion). This inherent similarity also explains why the Damp Ability affects Aftermath's functionality. Before its establishment as an Ability, Aftermath made an appearance as a Pokémon Trading Card Game attack.

Title: Anticipation (Ability)

The Ability known as Anticipation (Japanese: きけんよち, or "Danger Premonition") made its debut in Generation IV. This Ability prompts the Pokémon to "shudder" upon entering combat, or if it acquires Anticipation mid-battle, provided an opposing Pokémon possesses a damaging attack that would be super effective against the Anticipation user, a one-hit knockout technique, or either Self-Destruct or Explosion. The act of shuddering itself carries no practical in-game consequence; rather, the displayed Ability message serves solely to inform the player about potential moves the adversary might carry. Moves such as Hidden Power, Judgment, Weather Ball, and Natural Gift are considered Normal-type. Should the foe possess Scrappy or Normalize, or if Gravity is active, Anticipation factors these conditions into its assessment of whether an opposing move would indeed be super effective. Anticipation is not activated by Counter, Metal Burst, or Mirror Coat. Subsequently, Selfdestruct and Explosion are now classified alongside other Normal-type moves, consequently preventing them from triggering this Ability. Conversely, Counter, Metal Burst, and Mirror Coat are now recognized as attacking moves of their respective types, meaning they *can* activate Anticipation. Techno Blast registers as a Normal-type move. Synchronoise is considered a standard Psychic-type attack. Later, Anticipation ceased to consider the influence of Scrappy, Normalize, or Gravity on type effectiveness when deciding whether to activate its message. Hidden Power began to be treated as its actual effective type instead of Normal-type. Both Revelation Dance and Multi-Attack are regarded as Normal-type moves. Freeze-Dry functions as a regular Ice-type move. Flying Press is categorized as a conventional Fighting-type move. Aura Wheel is identified as an Electric-type move. In Inverse Battles, Anticipation accounts for the altered type chart, allowing even Normal-type moves (including Self-Destruct and Explosion) to potentially trigger it. This Ability does not, however, consider the impact of strong winds on type interactions, nor does it recognize how opposing Abilities like Aerilate might modify their move types. During Generation V, Ferrothorn was an exception, possessing no Hidden Ability. Within the Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky series, as well as the Mystery Dungeon (WiiWare) titles, a Pokémon with Anticipation can discern if its foe possesses any super effective moves when initiating a standard attack. From Gates to Infinity onwards, Anticipation has enhanced the likelihood of dodging both super effective and one-hit knockout attacks. For Pokémon UNITE, Anticipation functions as the Ability specific to an Eevee destined to evolve into Espeon. If Eevee would be afflicted by a hindrance (such as slow), that hindrance is nullified, granting Eevee temporary immunity to all hindrances. For multi-hit hindrances, like Pikachu's Volt Tackle, only the initial strike is prevented. This particular Ability operates on a 45-second cooldown period. Upon its evolution into Espeon, this Ability transforms into Magic Bounce.

Title: Forewarn (Ability)

Known in Japanese as よちむ (Prophetic Dream), Forewarn is an Ability that made its debut in Generation IV. This Ability identifies and displays the opponent's most powerful move. Should multiple moves possess identical power levels, the selection among them occurs randomly. During engagements involving multiple adversaries, Forewarn exclusively highlights the single most powerful move present across all opponents' known techniques. If several opponents share a move of equal highest power, a random selection determines which specific move is revealed. The Ability has the capacity to disclose a move from a non-adjacent foe, even if that move would typically be beyond the range to strike the Forewarn-bearing Pokémon. Damaging attacks lacking a defined power value (such as those dealing direct or variable damage) are nonetheless attributed one for this purpose. Even if an opposing Pokémon possesses only status-category moves, Forewarn will still assign a power value to one of them. When facing a Dynamaxed opponent, the Ability will exclusively consider and assign a power value from the Pokémon's original, pre-Dynamax move set. Within the Pokémon Mystery Dungeon Explorers series and the Pokémon Mystery Dungeon (WiiWare) titles, a Pokémon exhibiting Forewarn intermittently manages to evade incoming attacks, including those typically guaranteed to hit. Beginning with Pokémon Mystery Dungeon: Gates to Infinity and subsequent entries, Forewarn indicates the positions of both items and Pokémon on the current floor, specifically when the Pokémon is afflicted by the Sleep, Napping, or Nightmare status conditions. Volume 44 of the manga details how Black's Munna, known as Musha, was shown to possess Forewarn as its inherent Ability.

Title: Unaware (Ability)

This entry pertains to the Ability known as Unaware, which directly interacts with stat modifications. For the distinct Ability that shares a similar conceptual meaning, please refer to Oblivious (Ability). Designated as てんねん (Airhead) in Japanese, Unaware is an Ability that made its debut in Generation IV. Before Generation V arrived, it exclusively belonged to Bidoof and Bibarel as their signature Ability.

When a Pokémon possessing this Ability either executes or receives a damaging move, the opposing Pokémon's stat stages are disregarded throughout both damage and accuracy determinations. To be precise, if the Unaware Pokémon is attacking with a damaging move, the target's evasion stat stages are not considered for accuracy calculations, and its Defense and Special Defense stat stages are overlooked during damage calculations. Conversely, should the Unaware Pokémon be on the receiving end of a damaging move, the attacking Pokémon's accuracy stat stages are ignored for accuracy calculations, and its Attack and Special Attack stat stages are disregarded for damage calculations.

However, Unaware does not prevent certain moves—specifically Gyro Ball, Electro Ball, Stored Power, Punishment, and Power Trip—from incorporating stat modifications that influence their resultant power. Furthermore, this Ability holds no bearing on instances where a Pokémon inflicts self-damage due to confusion. It's important to note that Unaware does not negate the damage reduction caused by a burn, as burning status halves the move's overall power rather than directly reducing the user's Attack stat. Skyla's Swoobat has been confirmed to possess Unaware as its Ability. Within the game Pokémon Rumble World, the achievement of capturing every Pokémon whose standard Ability is Unaware bestows the "Ditzy Friend" title upon the player.

Title: Tinted Lens (Ability)
Originating in Generation IV, Tinted Lens (Japanese: いろめがね Tinted Glasses) is a Pokémon Ability. It causes a Pokémon's "not very effective" moves to deal double their usual damage. This power increase applies regardless of whether the type effectiveness is ½× or even less. Interestingly, Hisuian Braviary's Hidden Ability is coded as Defiant in Pokémon Legends: Arceus's internal data, rather than Tinted Lens. Within the Pokémon Mystery Dungeon series, Tinted Lens functions differently: in Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky, it raises the damage of "not very effective" attacks by approximately 20%. In Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, this boost is further enhanced to 40%. The name of this Ability is conceivably a nod to the common expression "viewing the world through rose-tinted glasses," which denotes finding good where none truly exists.

Title: Filter (Ability)

The Ability known as Filter (フィルター, also "Filter," in Japanese) first appeared in Generation IV. Prior to Generation VI, it exclusively belonged to Mime Jr. and Mr. Mime, functioning as their signature capability. This Ability lessens the harm taken from supereffective attacks by 25%. For instance, an opponent's move that would ordinarily inflict 2× damage will instead deal 1.5× damage. In the games Pokémon Mystery Dungeon: Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky, Filter similarly causes supereffective moves to inflict 25% less damage. However, its effect in Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon is to diminish incoming damage by approximately 12.5%. Within Pokémon UNITE, Filter is Mr. Mime's unique Ability, which, after the Pokémon uses a move, provides a temporary reduction in damage received. The PokéSPedia data-book confirms that Emerald's Mr. Mime is endowed with Filter as its Ability, and Sabrina's Mr. Mime is also identified as having Filter as its Ability.

Title: Slow Start (Ability)

Known in Japanese as スロースタート (Slow Start), this Ability made its debut in Generation IV. Before Generation IX, it served as Regigigas's exclusive signature Ability. Its effect causes the user's Attack and Speed stats to be reduced by half for the initial five turns of combat. Should the Pokémon be withdrawn from battle, this turn counter will be reset. Techniques that replace or suppress Slow Start (for example, Skill Swap or Worry Seed) will nullify its modifiers to Attack and Speed.

As Slow Start is not categorized as a stat stage modification, it still effectively halves the Attack stat for the user's Foul Play and Body Press, even though these moves typically rely on a stat other than the user's Attack. When the user is equipped with a typed Z-Crystal and executes a Z-Move derived from a special move, Slow Start additionally decreases the Special Attack stat, but only for the duration of that specific move. Notably, in the Japanese releases of Pokémon Diamond and Pearl, dispelling Slow Start did not revert the Attack and Speed reduction; the five-turn wait remained mandatory. This behavior was altered in the international localizations and also in Pokémon Platinum.

Pokémon Legends: Arceus deviates from the norm as Abilities are absent from standard gameplay. Nevertheless, Slow Start was reimagined as a unique status condition solely for Regigigas. Upon entering combat, Regigigas acquires this Slow Start status for its initial five turns, resulting in its Attack and Speed stats being halved. Owing to the distinct damage calculation methodology in Arceus, Slow Start's influence on Attack has a significantly diminished impact on overall damage output compared to other titles. In Pokémon Rumble World, Regigigas possesses the Slow Start ability, which lowers its attack power and movement speed until ten opposing Pokémon are vanquished, with this count resetting on each new screen. A similar effect is observed in Pokémon Rumble Blast, where it appears as the Special Trait "Slow-Starting". Upon entering a dungeon floor, the Pokémon's Travel Speed is diminished for either 7 or 5 turns. However, its Attack stat remains unchanged.

Volume 40 of the manga disclosed that Diamond's Regigigas, known as Reg, possessed Slow Start as its Ability. This was in the context of Pocket Monsters Diamond and Pearl: Regigigas Major Capture Operation!!

Title: Scrappy (Ability)
First appearing in Generation IV, Scrappy (known in Japanese as きもったま, or 'Spunk') is an Ability. This particular Ability allows its user to successfully land damage-dealing moves of the Normal and Fighting types against otherwise immune Ghost-type Pokémon. Beginning with Generation VIII, Scrappy also provides a protective effect, rendering the Pokémon immune to the Intimidate Ability. Curiously, within the game's internal data for Pokémon Legends: Arceus, Hisuian Decidueye's designated Hidden Ability is Long Reach, not Scrappy. According to the PokéSPedia data-book, X's Kangaskhan, nicknamed Kanga, was shown to possess Scrappy as her Ability. Both Professor Oak's Kangaskhan, Kanga, and Whitney's Miltank, Mil Mil, were also stated to have Scrappy as their respective Ability.

Title: Storm Drain (Ability)

Known in Japanese as よびみず (Pump Priming), Storm Drain is an Ability that first appeared in Generation IV. Its primary effect causes all single-target Water-type attacks initiated by other Pokémon, including teammates, to be directed towards the Ability-bearer, provided it's a valid target. Water-type moves become infallible against a Pokémon possessing Storm Drain, with the exception of turns where the Pokémon is semi-invulnerable from moves like Dive or Fly. An interesting quirk is that Surf specifically ignores the semi-invulnerable state of Dive, still hitting. Redirection of Dive by Storm Drain is limited to the turn on which Dive inflicts damage. Should another Pokémon draw focus (e.g., through Follow Me), Water-type attacks will be aimed at that Pokémon instead of the Storm Drain user. When a Pokémon holding an Absorb Bulb or Luminous Moss is targeted by a Water-type move while having Storm Drain, the Ability nullifies the move, preventing the held item's effect from activating. Certain moves, like G-Max Hydrosnipe, and specific Abilities are designed to bypass other Abilities; these will ignore Storm Drain and strike their original target. Within the context of Triple Battles, Storm Drain fails to redirect Water-type attacks if the Pokémon is beyond the move's range. Historically, Water-type Judgment and Natural Gift were immune to Storm Drain's redirection.

Beyond its primary redirection capability, Storm Drain additionally grants the Pokémon complete immunity to all Water-type moves. Furthermore, whenever struck by a Water-type attack, the Pokémon's Special Attack stat increases by one stage. Even if hit by multi-strike Water-type attacks (like Water Shuriken, or those influenced by Parental Bond), this Special Attack boost will only trigger a single time. The Special Attack increase does not occur if the Pokémon is protected from the incoming Water-type move. Subsequently, Storm Drain gained the ability to redirect Water-type Judgment and Natural Gift. However, Abilities such as Propeller Tail and Stalwart, along with the move Snipe Shot, can bypass Storm Drain's targeting effect, though the Water-type immunity remains intact against them.

In the overworld of Pokémon Sword and Shield, having a fainted or active Pokémon with Storm Drain at the head of the party provides a 51% likelihood of encountering a Water-type Pokémon, if a suitable one is available. This chance is slightly lower in Pokémon Brilliant Diamond and Shining Pearl, at 50%. Conversely, in Pokémon Scarlet and Violet, Storm Drain holds no function outside of battle, as its previous field utility has been superseded by Meal Powers. Within the Mystery Dungeon series, specifically Explorers of Time and Darkness, a Pokémon with Storm Drain draws in and completely nullifies all hostile Water-type attacks across the entire floor. This effect was modified in Pokémon Mystery Dungeon: Explorers of Sky to be limited to the current room only. In Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, Storm Drain additionally boosts the Pokémon's Special Attack by one stage upon taking a hit from a Water-type move.

Title: Ice Body (Ability)

Debuting in Generation IV, the Ability known as Ice Body (Japanese: アイスボディ, or Ice Body) provides unique benefits. When weather conditions are hail or snow, any Pokémon possessing this Ability recovers 1/16 of its total HP at the close of every turn. Furthermore, it inherently nullifies damage that would typically be inflicted by hail.
