To put this in perspective, the standard rate of HP recovery for Pokémon under normal circumstances is 0.5% of their maximum health per turn. While the baseline recovery is 0.5% of max HP per turn, Ice Body is stated to boost HP restoration by either two-fold or four-fold when hail is active. Notably, a Vanillite belonging to a Team Plasma Grunt was once confirmed to possess Ice Body as its designated Ability.

Title: Solid Rock (Ability)

This Ability, known in Japanese as ハードロック (Hard Rock), was first introduced during Generation IV. Solid Rock serves to reduce the harm taken from supereffective moves by 25%. For example, an attack that would normally inflict 2× damage will instead deal 1.5× damage. In the *Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky* series, Solid Rock similarly lessens supereffective damage by 25%. However, within *Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon*, this effect is diminished to approximately 12.5% damage reduction. A notable trait shared by every Pokémon with Solid Rock is the existence of at least one 4× weakness. Camerupt is the unique Pokémon with Solid Rock that does not suffer a 4× weakness to Grass. Counterintuitively, given its name, Solid Rock was initially assigned to more Ground-type Pokémon than Rock types in its debut generation.

Title: Snow Warning (Ability)

Regarding this topic, Snow Warning (ゆきふらし Snowfall in Japanese) is an Ability that was introduced in Generation IV. For details on the location in Pokémon Channel, refer to Mt. Snowfall. Prior to Generation VI, it served as the unique Ability for Snover and Abomasnow.

Upon a Pokémon possessing Snow Warning entering a battle, it immediately initiates a hailstorm. This triggered weather condition mirrors the move Hail in its effects, yet rather than dissipating after five turns, it persists throughout the entire battle unless superseded. Should a Pokémon with Snow Warning be deployed simultaneously with another Pokémon whose Ability influences weather, the Ability of the Pokémon with lower Speed will take precedence over the faster one. In such an instance, the resulting hailstorm will persist for only five turns. This five-turn limit can be extended to eight turns if the Pokémon is holding an Icy Rock. However, Snow Warning will not activate if extreme weather conditions like heavy rain, extremely harsh sunlight, or strong winds are already in effect. Snow Warning currently produces a snowstorm in place of hail.

Within the world of Explorers of Time and Darkness, Snow Warning shifts the weather to Hail upon traversing a new floor, and subsequently every 36 turns thereafter. For Super Mystery Dungeon, the Ability brings about a hailstorm immediately upon entering any given floor. The hail persists for a variable duration (always a multiple of 66 turns) before dissipating. Once the weather has cleared, Snow Warning will reactivate following another waiting period, also a multiple of 66 turns.

In Pokémon UNITE, Alolan Ninetales utilizes Snow Warning as its unique Ability, which conjures falling snow onto opponents, inflicting damage and temporarily reducing their movement speed. Enhanced attacks and abilities (with the exception of Aurora Veil) unleash additional snow, and repeatedly striking the same foe with this snow will result in a 1-second freeze. A cooldown period of 8 seconds applies to this Ability. Notably, Candice's Abomasnow and Snover possess Snow Warning as their designated Ability. Paralleling the move Hail, which was introduced a generation after Sunny Day, Rain Dance, and Sandstorm, the Ability Snow Warning likewise emerged a generation later than its counterparts Drought, Drizzle, and Sand Stream.

Title: Honey Gather (Ability)

This Ability, known in Japanese as みつあつめ (Honey Gather), made its debut in Generation IV. Before Generation V, Combee exclusively possessed this specific Ability. Should the player emerge victorious from a battle, a Pokémon within their active party featuring Honey Gather, provided it is not currently equipped with an item, stands a chance to acquire Honey. This mechanic operates similarly to Pickup, occurring regardless of whether the Pokémon was actively involved in the combat or even capable of battling. The probability of a Pokémon employing this Ability to find Honey is tied to its current level. This likelihood begins at a 5% rate for Pokémon ranging from levels 1 to 10, then incrementally increases by 5% for every subsequent ten levels, ultimately reaching a maximum 50% chance at levels 91 through 100. Within the Pokémon Mystery Dungeon: Explorers franchise entries and the Pokémon Mystery Dungeon (WiiWare) titles, Honey Gather offers an enhanced probability of locating Nectar within Treasure Boxes. By contrast, in Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, a Pokémon possessing Honey Gather may discover Nectar upon ascending or descending to an alternative dungeon floor. Every Pokémon species capable of manifesting Honey Gather is also able to learn the move Sweet Scent. Notably, Honey Gather holds the distinction of being the inaugural signature Ability confined solely to a Pokémon that has not reached its final evolutionary stage. It stands as one of merely four Abilities to possess this unique characteristic, alongside Battery, Wimp Out, and Ball Fetch.

Title: Frisk (Ability)

Known in Japanese as おみとおし (Unobstructed Sight), Frisk is an Ability that debuted in Generation IV. This particular Ability enables the Pokémon to discern the opponent's held item the moment it joins the battle. Should an opposing Pokémon possess a held item, a message confirming its identity will promptly display subsequent to the Frisk-user's entry into the fray. Conversely, if the foe lacks an item, no such notification will manifest. During Double or Triple Battles, a randomly selected and previously unrevealed adversary has its held item exposed. The item brought to light might originate from an opponent not directly adjacent to the Frisk user. Presently, Frisk unveils the held items of every opposing Pokémon, irrespective of their battlefield placement, proceeding from left to right relative to the opposing team's viewpoint. Within the internal programming of Pokémon Legends: Arceus, Hisuian Typhlosion possesses Flash Fire as its Hidden Ability, as opposed to Frisk. According to the PokéSPedia data-book, Blue's Pumpkaboo was shown to possess Frisk as its Ability. Additionally, Frisk was identified as the Ability of Caitlin's Gothitelle, Sird's Banette, and Professor Oak's Stantler, known as Antch.

Title: Reckless (Ability)

The Ability known as Reckless (Japanese: すてみ Life-Risking) made its debut in Generation IV. Prior to Generation V, it served as the unique signature Ability of Hitmonlee. Reckless enhances the power of moves that inflict recoil or crash damage by 20%, with the sole exception of Struggle. Given that recoil damage is calculated proportionally to the damage dealt, Pokémon wielding Reckless will consequently incur greater recoil damage when employing moves augmented by this Ability. An accompanying list details the specific moves affected by Reckless, as well as the Pokémon capable of possessing Reckless and learning those particular moves.

Some moves, although impacted by this Ability, are not currently learnable by any Pokémon that naturally has Reckless. Nevertheless, these moves can be called forth using Metronome, Mirror Move, Copycat, or Mimic. Alternatively, a Pokémon might employ them after acquiring Reckless through another Ability like Trace or Receiver, or via a move such as Skill Swap or Role Play.

In the Generation V games, Starly does not possess a Hidden Ability. However, a Starly with its Hidden Ability flag activated will evolve into a Staravia that *does* have Reckless as its Hidden Ability, and such Staravia can produce Starly offspring also bearing the Hidden Ability flag when bred. Furthermore, any Starly with its Hidden Ability flag enabled will have its Ability converted to Reckless upon transfer to Pokémon Bank.

Regarding Blue-Striped Basculin in Pokémon Black and White, Reckless functions as their second standard Ability; an exception is an in-game trade in White, which provides a Blue-Striped Basculin with Rock Head. For Pokémon Black 2 and White 2, wild Blue-Striped Basculin possess Rock Head as their second standard Ability, whereas Blue-Striped Basculin *bred* within these games will feature Reckless as their second standard Ability. By Generation VI, Blue-Striped Basculin consistently have Rock Head as their second standard Ability, and any Blue-Striped Basculin carrying Reckless that is transferred to Pokémon Bank during this generation will have its Ability automatically altered to Rock Head.

Within the spin-off titles Explorers of Time and Darkness and Explorers of Sky, Reckless delivers a more substantial 50% increase to the damage of affected moves. Later, in Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, both the damage and the corresponding recoil damage (including crash damage in the event of a Jump Kick missing) of these specific moves are boosted by 20%. It is important to note that the Ability has no effect on the moves Struggle or Final Gambit. Finally, Alder's Bouffalant was explicitly shown to possess Reckless as its Ability.

Title: Multitype (Ability)

This Ability, known as マルチタイプ (Multitype) in Japanese, was introduced in Generation IV and is uniquely associated with Arceus. A Pokémon possessing Multitype will have its form and corresponding type altered based on the specific Plate it is currently holding. Attempts to use Gastro Acid, Worry Seed, or Simple Beam on a target with Multitype will prove ineffective. Furthermore, moves such as Role Play, Skill Swap, Entrainment, and Doodle cannot succeed if Multitype is present on either the user or the target. Abilities like Trace, Power of Alchemy, and Receiver are unable to replicate Multitype. It resists replacement by Mummy or Lingering Aroma, suppression by Core Enforcer or Neutralizing Gas, and transference via Wandering Spirit. Moves designed to remove items, specifically Thief, Covet, Trick, Switcheroo, and Knock Off, are ineffective against a Multitype Pokémon, irrespective of its held item. Upon transformation into Arceus, a Pokémon will assume the exact form and type of the Arceus it copied. Should the transformed Pokémon possess a Plate distinct from the target's, it will instantly adopt the form and type associated with its own Plate; if it holds no Plate, it immediately reverts to Normal Arceus, unless its initial transformation was already into Normal Arceus. In the event of a Pokémon transforming into Arceus, it will match the target Arceus's form, independent of its own held item. Multitype's influence on items has shifted; instead, Arceus becomes immune to item-manipulating effects that would either equip it with a Plate or remove one it is already carrying. Furthermore, Multitype causes Arceus's type and form to adjust if it is holding a Z-Crystal corresponding to a specific type. Should the Pokémon undergo Terastallization, Multitype's effects are rendered void. In the data of *Explorers of Time, Darkness and Sky*, this ability is present but non-functional, retaining only its name and accompanying description within the Japanese localization. The aforementioned description was later utilized in the WiiWare game titles. Within *Super Mystery Dungeon*, a Pokémon possessing Multitype, along with its allies, inflicts amplified damage with their moves, as if benefiting from a same-type attack bonus. Nevertheless, the standard type-effectiveness calculations for these moves remain unchanged.

Title: Flower Gift (Ability)

Debuting in Generation IV, Flower Gift (Japanese: フラワーギフト) is an Ability that is exclusive to Cherrim. When harsh sunlight conditions are active, this unique Ability elevates the Attack and Special Defense stats of the Pokémon possessing it, along with its active allies, by a significant 50%. While Flower Gift initially did not trigger Cherrim's form change, it now, in addition to its stat-boosting properties, causes Cherrim to shift from its Overcast Form to Sunshine Form. This transformation takes place either when harsh sunlight becomes active during Cherrim's battle participation or when Cherrim is deployed into battle under existing harsh sunlight. Should Cherrim's Flower Gift be lost or suppressed (e.g., via Gastro Acid), it will revert to its Overcast Form. An exception exists for Dynamaxed Cherrim: if a Sunshine Form Cherrim undergoing Dynamax loses Flower Gift, it will maintain its Sunshine Form even after its Dynamax state concludes. Furthermore, the move Role Play will fail if its target possesses Flower Gift, and likewise, Entrainment cannot succeed if the user itself has this Ability. Flower Gift resists replication by other Abilities such as Trace, Power of Alchemy, Receiver, or Wandering Spirit. In the Pokémon Mystery Dungeon titles Explorers of Time, Darkness, and Sky, an active Flower Gift provides a one-stage boost to the team's Attack and Special Defense. Although Abilities are not featured in the gameplay of Pokémon Legends: Arceus, Cherrim's Sunshine Form in that particular game exhibits enhanced base stats, serving as a direct simulation of Flower Gift's effects.

Title: Bad Dreams (Ability)

This power, known in Japanese as ナイトメア (Nightmare), debuted in the fourth generation of Pokémon games. It serves as the exclusive signature Ability for the Pokémon Darkrai. Upon the conclusion of every turn, any opposing Pokémon that is adjacent and asleep suffers harm equivalent to one-eighth of its total HP. Notably, Bad Dreams deals its harm *after* a Pokémon rendered drowsy by the move Yawn finally succumbs to sleep, meaning damage is applied on the very turn the Pokémon falls asleep from this drowsiness. The Ability's effect takes place subsequent to a Pokémon's chance to awaken via the Abilities Hydration, Shed Skin, or Healer. Within Pokémon Legends: Z-A, Abilities are not present during standard gameplay encounters. Nevertheless, during the specific Rogue Mega Evolution confrontation with Darkrai, Pokémon afflicted with drowsiness incur damage amounting to 1/25 of their maximum HP each second. As Darkrai undergoes its Rogue Mega Evolution in the battle's subsequent phase, this damage escalates to 1/16 of total HP and is applied with greater regularity. Upon capture, Darkrai itself does not keep this particular damaging effect. In a separate context, at the close of every turn, hostile Pokémon occupying the same room and suffering from a sleep-related status condition (excluding Yawning) sustain a fixed 15 HP of damage. By its index number, Bad Dreams holds the position as the final Ability introduced in Generation IV. Interestingly, the Japanese designation for this Ability matches the English designation of a move that has a comparable impact. Furthermore, both in Japanese and English, the moniker of this Ability is identical to Mega Darkrai's classification category.

Title: Pickpocket (Ability)

Known in Japanese as わるいてぐせ (Bad Sticky Fingers), Pickpocket is an Ability that first appeared in Generation V of the Pokémon games. Upon being struck by a contact move, a Pokémon possessing this Ability will take the item currently held by its attacker. However, activation is prevented if the Pokémon with Pickpocket is already holding an item, or if it is rendered unconscious by the incoming attack. Any items acquired via this Ability in Trainer battles are not retained once the combat concludes. Should a Pokémon with Pickpocket endure a contact-making multistrike move (like Fury Swipes), the item transfer occurs only following the last hit. When a single contact move strikes multiple Pokémon featuring this Ability, Pickpocket activates for the fastest among them, provided it lacks a held item.

The activation timing for Pickpocket places it after Magician, Sticky Barb, and item-stealing moves such as Thief and Covet. Furthermore, it triggers after the attacking Pokémon consumes a single-use item, for instance a Focus Sash, as a direct result of the received attack. This sequencing ensures that the Pokémon with Pickpocket consistently acquires the item. It also resolves after Knock Off, Bug Bite, and Pluck, meaning the stolen item isn't lost, and if its own item was removed by these moves, it can promptly steal a replacement. Items such as the Life Orb and Shell Bell will apply their effects prior to being taken. Despite being stolen, the King's Rock and Razor Fang can still cause the Pokémon with Pickpocket to flinch.

Wild Pokémon are unable to pilfer items from the player's Pokémon. Pickpocket is ineffective against Pokémon endowed with the Sticky Hold Ability, and it is also incapable of stealing Mail or a Z-Crystal. Specific items are immune to Pickpocket when associated with certain Pokémon: a Griseous Orb if either Pokémon is Giratina; a Plate if either Pokémon is Arceus; a memory if either Pokémon is Silvally; a Drive if either Pokémon is Genesect; a Mega Stone if either Pokémon is capable of Mega Evolving with it; a Blue Orb if either Pokémon is Kyogre; a Red Orb if either Pokémon is Groudon; the Rusted Sword if either Pokémon is Zacian; the Rusted Shield if either Pokémon is Zamazenta; or Booster Energy if either Pokémon possesses the Protosynthesis or Quark Drive Ability. Notably, within the internal programming of Pokémon Legends: Arceus, Hisuian Sneasel's Hidden Ability is designated as Poison Touch rather than Pickpocket. By index number order, Pickpocket holds the distinction of being the first Ability from Generation V.

Title: Sheer Force (Ability)

Sheer Force (Japanese: ちからずく Sheer Force), an Ability originating in Generation V, enhances the base power of all damaging attacks possessing any secondary effects by approximately 30% (specifically, a multiplier of 5325/4096). However, this power increase comes at the cost of those additional effects being nullified. Notably, Sheer Force empowers Order Up even in the absence of Commander activation, and any resultant stat increases still apply as usual.

What constitutes an "additional effect" for Sheer Force includes: decreasing the target's stats, augmenting the user's stats, inflicting a status condition on the target (whether volatile or persistent, though excluding being bound), and causing the target to flinch. Consequently, damaging moves with any chance (from a slight possibility to a 100% certainty) of triggering these effects will see their power magnified, but their capacity to generate said effects will be suppressed. Moreover, Anchor Shot, Ceaseless Edge, Eerie Spell, Genesis Supernova, Secret Power, Sparkling Aria, Spirit Shackle, and Stone Axe are specifically recognized as having supplementary effects that Sheer Force can negate. An exception exists for Thousand Waves; despite its effect being otherwise identical to Anchor Shot and Spirit Shackle, it is not affected.

Recoil damage and self-inflicted stat reductions are not categorized as additional effects by Sheer Force. Similarly, various inherent properties of moves—such as a high critical-hit ratio, guaranteed accuracy, fluctuating or scaling power, a mandatory charging turn, or the ability to remove screens (Reflect, Light Screen, Aurora Veil), among others—are not considered secondary effects. If a move possesses one of these inherent properties in conjunction with an additional effect, only the additional effect will be disregarded, while the move itself still receives the power boost. For instance, Flare Blitz causes recoil to the user and has a chance to burn the target; with Sheer Force, Flare Blitz's damage is boosted by 30%, the user still takes recoil, but the target cannot be burned.

Effects triggered by Fling when specific items are held are not treated as additional effects for Sheer Force's purposes. Scale Shot is also explicitly deemed not to have additional effects under Sheer Force.

A crucial point is that if, and only if, a move benefits from Sheer Force's power boost, several effects that typically occur post-attack are circumvented. These include the activation of Eject Button, Red Card, Shell Bell, Kee Berry, and Maranga Berry; the recoil damage from Life Orb; the Abilities Color Change, Pickpocket, Wimp Out, Emergency Exit, Berserk, and Anger Shell; and Meloetta's form change opportunity when using Relic Song after acquiring Sheer Force. Other effects remain unaffected; thus, a Pokémon with Sheer Force holding a Life Orb, when using a Sheer Force-boosted move, gains the passive damage increase from Life Orb (as it's neither a secondary move effect nor on the list of skipped effects), yet avoids Life Orb's self-damage. Sheer Force has no impact on Max Moves, neither augmenting their power nor removing their secondary effects.

A comprehensive table detailing moves influenced by Sheer Force, along with Pokémon capable of having Sheer Force and learning those moves, typically accompanies this information. Some moves, despite being affected by this Ability, are currently unlearnable by any Pokémon with Sheer Force. However, such moves might still be utilized via Metronome, Mirror Move, Copycat, Mimic, or by a Pokémon that gained Sheer Force through an Ability like Trace or Receiver, or a move like Skill Swap or Role Play. Sheer Force does not exhibit any effects outside of battle.

Concerning Darmanitan's Zen Mode, while it inherits all of Standard Mode's Abilities upon transformation, it can only transform if it possesses the Ability Zen Mode. Consequently, Sheer Force is unattainable on a Darmanitan in Zen Mode.

In a seemingly contradictory detail, Sheer Force has been stated to raise the power of all damaging moves that have any additional effects by 20%, with their additional effects being ignored.

In anime and manga appearances, Drayden's Druddigon was noted to possess Sheer Force in "A Cold Reception." Black's Braviary, Brav, was revealed to have Sheer Force in volume 44, and N's Gurdurr was confirmed with Sheer Force in the PokéSPedia data-book.

When initially unveiled in CoroCoro Comic, the Ability was known in Japanese as ちからづく (Invigorate). In the actual games, it is named ちからずく (Sheer Force) in Japanese. Both terms share the same pronunciation but feature different spellings.

Previously, Sheer Force incorrectly boosted Jet Punch despite the move lacking any additional effects, an oversight that was later rectified. Curiously, Electro Shot, despite having no secondary effects, receives a boost from Sheer Force due to an internal flag. This same internal flag is also set for Drum Beating, Pyro Ball, Breaking Swipe, Apple Acid, Grav Apple, Spirit Break, and Strange Steam, even though their internal "move quality" values would inherently qualify them for a Sheer Force boost anyway.

Title: Contrary (Ability)

Making its debut in Generation V, the Ability known as Contrary (あまのじゃく Amanojaku in Japanese) has a unique effect. For any Pokémon possessing this Ability, all alterations to its stat stages are reversed. Should an effect typically diminish a stat, it will instead be augmented; conversely, an effect that would normally boost a stat will cause it to decline. However, Contrary does not apply to stat modifications that don't involve stat stages, such as the effects of Guts or paralysis. This inversion mechanism extends to changes the Pokémon inflicts upon itself, like those from Curse or Leaf Storm.

Contrary's effect activates solely at the moment stat stage changes occur; it does not retroactively reverse pre-existing alterations. Consequently, if the Pokémon loses Contrary or its stats are conveyed via Baton Pass, any current stat stage modifications will persist unaltered. Furthermore, stat stages transferred to a Pokémon with Contrary through Baton Pass will not undergo inversion. Mist (whether naturally occurring or from a Guard Spec.) accounts for Contrary's inversion prior to deciding whether to prevent the stat change. For instance, should a Pokémon with Contrary be affected by String Shot while shielded by Mist, its Speed will increase. Conversely, if the same Pokémon is hit by Swagger under Mist's protection, its Attack will remain unchanged.

When a Pokémon with Contrary performs Belly Drum, its Attack stat will instead be reduced to -6 stages (despite the in-game message stating that its Attack was maximized). If the user's Attack is already at -6 stages, Belly Drum will still consume HP. Regardless of Contrary, Belly Drum will fail if the user's Attack stat has already reached +6 stages. Any stat stage alterations inflicted by a move from a Pokémon with Mold Breaker, Teravolt, or Turboblaze will not be inverted on a Contrary target. Similarly, Flower Veil considers Contrary's inversion prior to blocking a stat change.

Even when receiving stat boosts from other players' O-Powers, these will still be inverted. However, stat stage increases stemming from the Z-Power effects of status Z-Moves are an exception and will not be inverted by Contrary. Conversely, the stat stage boosts from Extreme Evoboost and Clangorous Soulblaze *are* subject to inversion. Should a Pokémon with Contrary be hit by Strength Sap, the user will recover HP up to the target's effective Attack stat value at +5 stages, even if the target's Attack is currently at +6 stages. When a Max Move that would typically lower stats hits a Pokémon with Contrary, or if a Pokémon with Contrary employs a Max Move that would usually raise stats, the change will be inverted, yet the descriptive message for this stat modification will not appear. The Japanese designation for Contrary, Amanojaku, originates from a character in Japanese folklore known for acting contrary to commands.

Title: Unnerve (Ability)

Introduced in Generation V, Unnerve (Japanese: きんちょうかん Tension) is an Ability. It renders opposing Pokémon unable to consume their own held Berries. Usage of Natural Gift, however, remains unimpeded. When a Pokémon possessing Unnerve enters combat, the Ability displays a message. Should entry hazards exist, their activation occurs subsequent to this notification. The chance of wild Pokémon responding to calls for help is 1.2× higher when up against a Pokémon with Unnerve. Intimidate and Pressure possess this identical effect. When the move Teatime is employed, Unnerve's effect of preventing opposing Pokémon from consuming held Berries is nullified. Within the Mystery Dungeon series, Unnerve halts adjacent foes from ingesting food items or drinking health drinks. Black's Galvantula, Tula, was shown to possess Unnerve as his Ability in volume 44.

Title: Defiant (Ability)

Defiant, known in Japanese as まけんき (Competitive Spirit), is an Ability that was first introduced in Generation V. When an opposing Pokémon causes one of a Pokémon's stats to be lowered, its Attack stat immediately increases by two stages. This boost will not occur if the Pokémon itself lowers its own stats (for example, with Close Combat) or if an ally diminishes its stats. However, Defiant *will* activate if its stats are lowered due to its barrier breaking during a Max Raid Battle. Should multiple stats be reduced at once (such as by Memento or Venom Drench), Defiant will trigger for each individual stat that is affected. Conversely, if a single stat is lowered by more than one stage (as is the case with Charm or Eerie Impulse), Defiant will only activate once. The activation of Defiant takes precedence over a held White Herb. If, after Defiant's effect, the Pokémon no longer has any negative stat stages, the White Herb will not get the chance to activate. In the internal code of Pokémon Legends: Arceus, Hisuian Braviary's Hidden Ability is identified as Defiant, rather than Tinted Lens. Before Generation IX, Piplup, Prinplup, and Empoleon had Defiant as their Ability instead of Competitive. Grimsley's Bisharp is shown to possess Defiant as its Ability. Furthermore, this Ability can cause a minor, harmless text glitch when activated in Pokémon Mystery Dungeon: Gates to Infinity.

Title: Defeatist (Ability)

The Ability known as Defeatist (Japanese: よわき Timid) made its debut in Generation V. It serves as the exclusive Ability for both Archen and Archeops. This Ability causes the user's Attack and Special Attack stats to be halved whenever their HP falls to 50% or less of its maximum value. Simultaneously, Defeatist also reduces the power of the Pokémon's moves by one-third under the identical low-HP condition. The PokéSPedia data-book disclosed that N's Archeops possessed Defeatist as its Ability. For the Pokémon Rumble games, Skittish acts as a Special Trait counterpart to Defeatist, decreasing the harm caused to foes when the Pokémon's health is low.

Title: Cursed Body (Ability)

The Ability known as Cursed Body (Japanese: のろわれボディ Cursed Body) made its debut in Generation V. When a Pokémon possessing this Ability is struck by a damaging attack, there exists a 30% probability that Cursed Body will incapacitate the attacker's move for a total of 4 turns, including the turn of its activation. The move Struggle, however, cannot be disabled. Cursed Body can come into effect even if the attacking Pokémon is protected by a substitute or if the Pokémon with Cursed Body is knocked out; conversely, it will not activate if the incoming attack hits the Cursed Body Pokémon's own substitute. If Cursed Body disables a move that hits multiple times before all its strikes are completed, it will not prevent the remaining hits from executing. Cursed Body is capable of disabling a move that initiates another move, provided the called move successfully inflicted damage. Should a Pokémon unleash a damaging Z-Move on a Cursed Body Pokémon, that specific damaging Z-Move can be disabled (without impacting its base move), temporarily preventing it from benefiting from a second use via Rotom Powers, but otherwise yielding no substantial consequence. Nevertheless, if Cursed Body were to disable a status Z-Move (such as Z-Nature Power), the underlying base status move is what becomes disabled. Prior to Generation VII, Gengar's Ability was Levitate rather than Cursed Body. X's Gengar, Garma, was confirmed to have Cursed Body as his Ability within the PokéSPedia data-book. Furthermore, a Team Plasma Grunt's Frillish was shown to possess Cursed Body as her Ability.

Title: Healer (Ability)
For the Special Ability encountered in Pokémon Battle Trozei, refer to the List of Special Abilities in Pokémon Battle Trozei. Healer (Japanese: いやしのこころ Healing Heart) is an Ability that debuted in Generation V. Upon the conclusion of each turn, Healer possesses a 30% likelihood of remedying an adjacent ally's status ailment. If the Pokémon is situated near multiple allies afflicted with status conditions, it is granted an independent 30% opportunity to heal each one, allowing for the restoration of more than one ally within the same turn. Should a status condition be cured, this process occurs prior to any burn or poison damage being applied during that specific turn. Within the internal programming of Pokémon Legends: Arceus, Incarnate Forme Enamorus's Ability is recorded as Healer, rather than Cute Charm. The PokéSPedia data-book revealed White's Alomomola, Nancy, to have Healer as her designated Ability.

Title: Friend Guard (Ability)
This Ability, known as Friend Guard (Japanese: フレンドガード), made its debut in Generation V. It functions by diminishing the damage taken by other allied Pokémon from any attack by a quarter (25%). Nevertheless, moves that inflict direct damage are not affected by this reduction. The Pokémon with Friend Guard itself does not receive this damage mitigation. When multiple allied Pokémon possess Friend Guard, their effects accumulate multiplicatively. For example, if both of a Pokémon's allies have Friend Guard in a Triple Battle, the damage that Pokémon sustains is multiplied by 9/16 (56.25%), which translates to a total damage reduction of 43.75%.

Title: Heavy Metal (Ability)

Making its debut in Generation V, Heavy Metal (Japanese: ヘヴィメタル Heavy Metal) is an Ability. It functions by doubling the weight of the Pokémon that possesses it. The effect of Autotomize on weight calculations takes precedence over other modifiers, including that of Heavy Metal. Heavy Metal is, uniquely, the only effect capable of causing a Pokémon's weight to exceed 999.9 kg. Despite this, the ability has no bearing on the Heavy Ball's functionality, as the item assesses a Pokémon based on its species' inherent weight, not its currently altered mass. Within Pokémon UNITE, Heavy Metal is designated as Duraludon's Ability. In this game, it confers immunity, preventing the user from being thrown or shoved by the initial attempt to apply such displacement.

Title: Light Metal (Ability)

This Ability, known as ライトメタル in Japanese, debuted in Generation V. It causes the Pokémon's weight to be halved, with the result rounded down to the nearest tenth. For instance, a Metang's standard 202.5 kg becomes 101.2 kg when Light Metal is active. Autotomize's weight reduction is always processed before other modifying effects, including Light Metal. A Pokémon's weight cannot decrease below 0.1 kg. The Heavy Ball remains unaffected, as it factors in the Pokémon's species' base weight instead of its current modified weight. Given Grass Knot and Low Kick's damage calculations, Metagross will still suffer maximum damage from these moves even with Light Metal.

Title: Multiscale (Ability)

Known in Japanese as マルチスケイル (Multiscale), this Ability made its debut in Generation V. When a Pokémon possessing Multiscale is at full HP, the incoming damage from any offensive move is halved. However, this mitigation does not apply to techniques that inflict a fixed amount of direct damage. Should the user be struck by a multi-hit attack while at peak health, only the initial strike will benefit from the damage reduction; subsequent hits will inflict their usual amount of damage. Specifically in Pokémon UNITE, Dragonite gains Multiscale as its Ability upon evolving from Dragonair. Its effect decreases incoming damage by 20% for a duration of 2.5 seconds. This protective effect is available for activation every 15 seconds.

Title: Harvest (Ability)

The term "Harvest" points to this entry. For the island located in the Decolore Islands, please see Harvest Island. If your interest lies in the different kinds of harvests found in Pokémon Legends: Arceus, direct your attention to Jubilife Village → Farm. Known in Japanese as しゅうかく (Harvest), this Ability made its debut in Generation V. It exists solely as a Hidden Ability. Should the Pokémon consume a Berry, there is a 50% probability that the Berry will be re-obtained at the conclusion of every turn. The specific Berry that was held remains tracked, even if it isn't restored or if the Pokémon departs the battle. During periods of harsh sunlight, Harvest's activation becomes guaranteed. Berries expended via Fling and Natural Gift are eligible for recovery by Harvest. Conversely, Harvest cannot restore Berries that have been given to another Pokémon, those obliterated by Incinerate, dislodged by Knock Off, or consumed through Bug Bite or Pluck. Furthermore, it cannot recover a Berry if a Pokémon possessing Pickup retrieves it. When multiple Pokémon possess either Pickup or Harvest, the Pokémon with higher Speed will retrieve an item before others. A Pokémon can only benefit from Harvest's effect once per turn. If a restored Berry is immediately consumed, Harvest will be unable to restore it again until the subsequent turn. Harvest is capable of restoring Berries that were initially owned by a different Pokémon but were then transferred and consumed by the Pokémon with Harvest. Nonetheless, a Pokémon can only track one consumed item (which includes non-Berries) at any given moment. Should a Pokémon consume a Berry, then acquire and consume a second item, the initial Berry cannot be restored, even if the second item is recovered and replaced. If the Harvest-user picks up an item after consuming a Berry, Harvest can still bring back the previously consumed Berry, provided the Pokémon holds no item and has not consumed any other items (including non-Berries) since the initial Berry consumption. Berries belonging to a target hit by Bug Bite or Pluck are not considered the user's last consumed item and thus cannot be recovered by the user's Harvest. Within Pokémon Sword and Shield, positioning a Pokémon with Harvest in the lead party slot (even if it has fainted) creates a 51% likelihood of triggering an encounter with a Grass-type Pokémon, assuming such an encounter is possible. For Pokémon Brilliant Diamond and Shining Pearl, this chance stands at 50%. In Pokémon Scarlet and Violet, this Ability lacks any function outside of combat, as its previous overworld effect has been superseded by Meal Powers.

Title: Telepathy (Ability)

While Telepathy can also refer to Pokémon capable of telepathic communication in human language (see Talking Pokémon), this article details the Ability. Known in Japanese as テレパシー (Telepathy), this Ability made its debut in Generation V. A Pokémon equipped with Telepathy will automatically evade any damaging attacks unleashed by its allies. This protective effect covers multi-target assaults like Discharge, single-target offenses aimed solely at the Telepathy user (such as Thunderbolt), and even moves that might ordinarily provide healing to an ally, like Pollen Puff or Present. It offers no defense, however, against non-damaging status moves employed by teammates, exemplified by Teeter Dance or Confuse Ray. Should Telepathy successfully intercept an incoming allied attack, the accompanying in-game notification states: "<name> avoids attacks by its ally Pokémon!" Instances of Telepathy in other media include Spinel's Beheeyem, which demonstrated the Ability via Skill Swap in The Rising Volt Tacklers vs. The Explorers!, Henry's Oranguru named Fanguru, and Colress's Beheeyem. Among all Abilities, Telepathy holds the distinction of being the most frequently encountered as a Hidden Ability.

Title: Moody (Ability)

Moody (Japanese: ムラっけ Caprice), an Ability first seen in Generation V, functions exclusively as a Hidden Ability. Each turn's conclusion triggers its effect: the Pokémon with Moody randomly boosts one of its stats by two stages, then randomly lowers a *different* stat by one stage. This Ability will not attempt to decrease a stat already at its minimum of -6, nor will it try to elevate a stat that has reached its maximum of +6. If all stats are already at +6, Moody will only apply a random decrease to one stat; similarly, if all stats are at -6, it will only provide a random increase to one stat. Moody is capable of affecting Attack, Defense, Special Attack, Special Defense, Speed, accuracy, and evasion.

Title: Overcoat (Ability)

Overcoat (Japanese: ぼうじん Dustproof) is an Ability that premiered in Generation V. Any Pokémon possessing Overcoat will not suffer damage from the weather conditions sandstorm and hailstorm. Currently, Overcoat also provides protection from powder and spore moves, as well as Effect Spore. In Pokémon Legends: Arceus's game code, Hisuian Sliggoo's and Goodra's designated Ability is Overcoat rather than Shell Armor. Six of the Pokémon capable of manifesting this Ability are already immune to one of the hazardous weather conditions due to their inherent type (this count rises to seven if Wormadam's Sandy Cloak and Trash Cloak forms are treated as separate Pokémon). Additionally, four of the Pokémon that can have this Ability are inherently immune to powder moves by virtue of their type.

Title: Poison Touch (Ability)

Debuting in Generation V, the Ability known as Poison Touch (or どくしゅ Underhanded Trick in Japanese) carries a distinct effect. When a Pokémon possessing this Ability executes a contact-making move, its target faces a 30% probability of being afflicted with poison. However, this likelihood is reduced to 20% specifically within Japanese editions of Pokémon Black and White. This poisoning opportunity functions entirely separately from any inherent chances a move might have to poison its target. Should a target be holding a Berry designed to heal status conditions, a fascinating sequence can occur: a move's built-in effect might initially cause a status, the Berry would then cure it, and only subsequently would Poison Touch inflict its own poison. Furthermore, for multistrike attacks, the poisoning chance from Poison Touch is assessed independently for every single hit. Even when facing moves like Dragon Tail or Circle Throw, the Ability can still successfully poison the affected Pokémon. It's possible for Poison Touch to poison a Pokémon with Mummy just prior to Mummy replacing Poison Touch. Certain Pokémon abilities, such as Immunity, Shield Dust, or Leaf Guard (when under harsh sunlight), prevent poisoning from Poison Touch, with Sunsteel Strike being the sole exception for contact moves.

During link battles involving a Japanese Pokémon Black or White game alongside a non-Japanese Black or White version (or any Black 2 or White 2), the determining factor for the poisoning rate is the game version of the player whose Pokémon possesses Poison Touch. Consequently, a Pokémon with Poison Touch belonging to a player using a Japanese Pokémon Black or White game will have a 20% chance to poison, while those from Pokémon Black 2 and White 2, or non-Japanese Black and White versions, will operate with a 30% chance. Within Pokémon Legends: Arceus's internal programming, Hisuian Sneasel's designated Hidden Ability is Poison Touch, diverging from Pickpocket. The PokéSPedia data-book confirmed that Moon's Alolan Grimer possessed Poison Touch as its Ability. The Japanese, Korean, and Chinese appellations for this Ability are all wordplays: 毒手 (Dokushu) signifies treachery or an underhanded scheme in common parlance, yet its constituent characters, 毒 (doku) and 手 (shu), directly translate to "poison" and "hand/touch."

Title: Regenerator (Ability)

Known in Japanese as さいせいりょく (Regenerative Power), Regenerator is an Ability first appearing in Generation V. When a Pokémon with Regenerator withdraws from battle, it recovers 33% of its total HP. This recuperation occurs regardless of whether the switch is initiated by the user through actions like Baton Pass or Volt Switch, or by an opponent employing disruptive moves like Whirlwind or Dragon Tail. The healing effect is contingent on Regenerator being the Pokémon's active and unsuppressed Ability at the moment of its departure from combat. For instance, a Pokémon that acquired Regenerator via Trace or Role Play will still regain health upon switching out, unlike a Pokémon natively possessing Regenerator but subsequently nullified by Gastro Acid. Notably, Heal Block has no influence on Regenerator's ability to restore health. Within the Pokémon Mystery Dungeon series, Regenerator functions differently, restoring 20% of the Pokémon's maximum HP when it descends to a new floor.

Title: Big Pecks (Ability)

Starting from Generation V, the Ability known as Big Pecks (はとむね, translated as Pigeon Breast, in Japanese) was introduced. This power protects the Pokémon possessing it by preventing any other Pokémon from decreasing its Defense stat stage. However, it offers no safeguard against the Pokémon itself lowering its own Defense, as demonstrated by moves like Shell Smash. Moreover, Big Pecks does not interfere with Spectral Thief's capability to steal (effectively reset) positive stat changes. Effects that reduce Defense through means other than stat stages, such as Guard Swap, Heart Swap, or Guard Split, function without being impacted by Big Pecks. This Ability can be circumvented by Pokémon that have Mold Breaker, Teravolt, or Turboblaze. The PokéSPedia data-book identified White's Vullaby, Barbara, and Y's Fletchling, Fletchy, as having Big Pecks as their Ability. Furthermore, Skyla's Unfezant and Cheren's Unfezant were also shown to utilize Big Pecks. The moniker "Big Pecks" is a wordplay, simultaneously referring to a bird's pecking motion and the pectoralis major, commonly called "pecs," which are the main muscles in the chest. Similarly, Big Pecks's Japanese name, Hatomune, can also be understood to signify "a proud heart."

Title: Sand Rush (Ability)

Known in Japanese as すなかき (Sand Paddle), Sand Rush is an Ability that first appeared in Generation V. While a sandstorm is active, Pokémon with this capacity experience a twofold increase in their Speed stat. Creatures possessing Sand Rush are also immune to the damage typically inflicted by a sandstorm. Beyond its defensive properties against sandstorm damage, Sand Rush also facilitates the execution of a singular move twice within a turn while active in a sandstorm, requiring the expenditure of just one PP. Clay's Excadrill has been identified as having Sand Rush as its specific Ability.

Title: Wonder Skin (Ability)

This Ability, known as ミラクルスキン (Miracle Skin) in Japanese, was initially introduced in Generation V. Wonder Skin causes status moves aimed at the Pokémon possessing it to have their accuracy decreased to 50%. However, if the targeted move's original accuracy is already at 50% or less, this effect does not alter it. It is possible for other influences to adjust the accuracy further even after Wonder Skin's effect has been applied. Wonder Skin has no impact on moves that do not undergo an accuracy check, nor does it affect moves executed by Pokémon with the abilities Mold Breaker, Teravolt, or Turboblaze. In contrast to Abilities that grant full immunity from status conditions (for instance, Immunity or Keen Eye), the "Pokémon's Ability" notification message is not shown when Wonder Skin activates.

Title: Analytic (Ability)

Debuting in Generation V, Analytic (known as アナライズ, or Analyze, in Japanese) is an Ability that provides a strategic advantage. When a Pokémon possessing Analytic carries out its move after all other combatants have already performed their actions, the strength of its attack is boosted by 30%, which is precisely approximated as a factor of 5325/4096.

In the intricate setup of a Triple Battle, the activation of this boost has specific considerations. If the Pokémon with Analytic aims at a Pokémon occupying a flank position, and that target then swaps with an ally from the middle, Analytic's move will always deal normal damage to the Pokémon that ends up in the flank slot. However, if the Analytic Pokémon targets a central Pokémon, and an ally on a flank moves to fill that middle spot, the power of Analytic's attack still depends on whether the original middle Pokémon (which has now moved to a flank) had already acted when the Analytic user makes its move.

Should an opposing Pokémon faint before it has a chance to execute its turn, Analytic's power enhancement is determined by whether that Pokémon's turn would have occurred before the user's that turn, considering its Speed and the move it selected. It's important to note that modifications to Speed or priority originating from conditions like paralysis, held items such as Power Weight, or specific Abilities like Stall are not factored into this calculation in Generation VIII; however, these modifiers were taken into account in all earlier generations.

Title: Illusion (Ability)

Illusion (イリュージョン in Japanese) is an Ability that made its debut in Generation V. It serves as the exclusive Ability for Zorua, Zoroark, and both of their Hisuian variants. When a Trainer's Pokémon equipped with Illusion enters combat, its visual facade shifts to mirror the last active, non-Egg Pokémon remaining in that Trainer's lineup. Should the Illusion-wielding Pokémon itself be the final conscious, non-Egg Pokémon in the party before its deployment, Illusion will not alter its look.

This Ability meticulously copies the outward characteristics of the Pokémon it imitates, encompassing its species, form, and Shininess, along with its nickname (or its species name if no nickname is present), the specific Poké Ball it originated from, and its gender. However, the level of the Pokémon being impersonated is not replicated. The entire effect is purely cosmetic; actual game mechanics, such as those governing Attract, will still reference the Pokémon's true gender. On the display interface, an opponent's Pokémon concealed by Illusion will reflect the type of the Pokémon it is mimicking, even if affected by moves like Soak.

The disguise dissipates if the Pokémon's Ability is altered or suppressed, or if it incurs damage from an offensive move. Importantly, indirect damage sources—such as those from a Life Orb, weather conditions, status ailments, recoil, and similar effects—will not cause the illusion to break. If a Pokémon with Illusion is struck by a multistrike move, the illusion will be dispelled after the initial hit. Should an Illusion user unleash a Z-Move, its disguise will vanish just prior to the move's execution. Role Play is ineffective if the target possesses Illusion, and Skill Swap similarly fails if either Pokémon involved has Illusion. Furthermore, Illusion cannot be copied by Trace, Power of Alchemy, or Receiver. If a Pokémon acquires Illusion through Imposter or Transform, it will not adopt a disguise. A Pokémon cannot Transform while under Illusion's effect, nor can it Transform into another Pokémon that is disguised by Illusion.

Under certain conditions, wild Pokémon can also utilize Illusion to conceal themselves. In Pokémon Black and White, during a specific event encounter in Lostlorn Forest, the wild Zoroark will appear disguised as Entei, Raikou, or Suicune, with the specific Legendary beast determined by the Pokémon that triggered the event. If a wild Pokémon with Illusion successfully summons an ally for assistance in an SOS Battle, it will assume the appearance of that newly arrived ally.

If Illusion would cause a Pokémon to masquerade as another Pokémon of the identical species, the Ability will not activate the disguise at all. Illusion is now capable of disguising a Pokémon as another Pokémon of the same species that also possesses Illusion. When a disguised Pokémon Dynamaxes while its imitation target has the Gigantamax Factor, it will Dynamax into the standard form of that Pokémon, not its Gigantamax form. If disguised as Zacian, Zamazenta, or Eternatus, the Pokémon will be unable to Dynamax.

In most scenarios, if the Pokémon Terastallizes while disguised, its illusion will persist. Nevertheless, it will Terastallize into its own Tera Type rather than the Tera Type of the disguised Pokémon. When disguised as Ogerpon or Terapagos, the Pokémon's illusion will fade before it Terastallizes. If a Pokémon with Illusion is disguising itself as an already Terastallized Pokémon, its illusion will not reflect the Terastallized state. Should the last conscious non-Egg Pokémon in the party be a Terastallized Ogerpon or Terapagos, Illusion will not activate when the Pokémon is switched in. Illusion will also fail to activate if the Pokémon wielding Illusion is Terastallized and the final Pokémon in the party is Ogerpon or Terapagos.

If a Pokémon is already outside its Poké Ball and the player initiates a battle with a wild Pokémon, Illusion will not become active at the start of the encounter. Wild Zorua and Zoroark will now consistently employ Illusion to appear as another wild Pokémon in the immediate vicinity, unless the game is reloaded after one has already spawned. However, this disguise immediately wears off once the battle commences, accompanied by the standard Ability activation message.

In Pokémon Mystery Dungeon: Gates to Infinity, this Ability operates similarly to the core series for opponent Pokémon. A Pokémon with Illusion can transform into another Pokémon found on the same floor of a Mystery Dungeon. For partner Pokémon, this Ability has no functional impact.

Within Pokémon Super Mystery Dungeon, leader or allied Pokémon endowed with this Ability will take on the form of Kecleon. An opposing Pokémon with Illusion will disguise itself as another enemy Pokémon present on the same floor. As long as the illusion remains active, the Pokémon benefits from a 40% boost to the power of its moves. The disguise is lost when the Pokémon suffering from Illusion is directly hit by a damaging move, or when Illusion is nullified or replaced, causing the Pokémon to revert to its true form. Indirect damage or HP loss, such as from Substitute, weather, status conditions, or traps, will not disrupt the illusion.

In Pokémon Conquest, Illusion functions akin to its core series counterpart. A Pokémon with Illusion begins a battle appearing as one of its allied Pokémon. The Ability solely disguises the Pokémon itself, not the Warrior it may be linked with. Wild Zorua invariably commence with Illusion as their inherent Ability. Upon entering a wild Pokémon kingdom location, Zorua will appear in the battle preview as a duplicate of another wild Pokémon present. For instance, if the preview indicates one Starly, Zorua might manifest as a second Starly, only revealed upon battle initiation. A Pokémon's Illusion will wear off if the user executes its first attack, takes direct damage, or, in the case of wild Pokémon, when a Warrior starts the minigame to link with them. Nonetheless, a disguise can be identified before committing to an attack or link attempt: when anticipating damage against a concealed target, the preview will calculate damage based on the target's true typing. Similarly, the link medal for a disguised wild Pokémon will reflect the link compatibility of its genuine self, not that of the disguise. If a Pokémon with Illusion is defeated by a single hit, it will vanish without the illusion breaking. When Illusion is employed by a player's Pokémon, computer-controlled opponents are not deceived and will act as though no disguise is applied. In Generation V, if Entrainment is used on a Pokémon with Illusion while it is disguised, when its Ability activates, the banner will state that the Ability belongs to the Pokémon that served as the disguise. If the Pokémon's Ability is subsequently changed, these messages will accurately identify the Pokémon to whom the Ability truly belongs upon activation.

Although Abilities are not featured in Pokémon GO, wild Zorua will appear in that game disguised as the player's Buddy Pokémon, an unmistakable nod to this Ability.

Title: Imposter (Ability)

Known in Japanese as かわりもの (Changer), Imposter is an Ability first appearing in the fifth generation of Pokémon games. It serves as the exclusive Hidden Ability for the Pokémon Ditto. Upon entering combat, the Pokémon instantly morphs into its opposing foe. This transformation replicates numerous aspects of the target, encompassing its visual characteristics like form, gender, and whether it is Shiny, alongside its types, Ability, moves, statistical values, and even its cry; notably, the user maintains its own HP, level, assigned nickname, and any held item. Despite the in-battle change, the Pokémon's original look and details persist within party and summary displays, and its initial sprite is shown in the battle menu and when reviewing its move list. This transformed state endures until the Pokémon is withdrawn, is knocked out, or the encounter concludes.

Activation of Imposter is prevented under specific circumstances: if the adversary is already transformed, concealed by Illusion, protected by a Substitute, or is a Starmobile. Imposter's effect is triggered exclusively upon the Pokémon's deployment into battle; it will not function if acquired after the Pokémon is already present on the battlefield. Should the Ability fail to activate during a Pokémon's initial entry, it will not attempt to do so again until that Pokémon is recalled and then re-sent out. Should a Pokémon possessing Imposter Terastallize into the Stellar type, the Ability will cease to activate. The Imposter Ability cannot be bypassed.

During Double Battles, Triple Battles, or Battle Royals, Imposter causes the Pokémon to transform into the foe positioned directly across from it. For Horde Encounters, Imposter targets the opponent situated in the furthest right slot. Within an SOS Battle, the Ability will mimic the foe found in the leftmost position. If, in any of these scenarios, no adversary occupies the specified position, the Pokémon will refrain from transforming. When a raid boss in a Max Raid Battle or Tera Raid Battle possesses Imposter, it will convert into the Pokémon of the battle's host. Nevertheless, specifically in Max Raid Battles, the raid boss's Imposter will not activate if the host Pokémon is Zacian, Zamazenta, or Eternatus. The move Role Play will be unsuccessful if its target possesses the Imposter Ability. Entrainment will similarly fail if the move's user is the one with Imposter. Imposter is impervious to replication by abilities such as Trace, Power of Alchemy, or Receiver.

Title: Infiltrator (Ability)

Debuting in Generation V, the Ability known as Infiltrator (Japanese: すりぬけ, or 'Slip Through') provides a significant advantage in battle. When a Pokémon uses moves while possessing Infiltrator, these attacks ignore the defensive screens and status protections of targets, specifically Reflect, Light Screen, Safeguard, and Mist. Additionally, most offensive actions, with the distinct exceptions of Transform and Sky Drop, taken by an Infiltrator Pokémon will bypass the effects of an opponent's Substitute. Even the protective barrier of Aurora Veil on opposing Pokémon is similarly circumvented by Infiltrator-powered moves.

Beyond combat, in Pokémon Sword and Shield, placing a Pokémon with Infiltrator in the foremost slot of your party (regardless of whether it has fainted) dramatically decreases the odds of initiating a random encounter by 87.5%. A historical point of interest is that during the Generation V games, Litwick, Lampent, and Chandelure were designated Shadow Tag as their Hidden Ability, rather than Infiltrator. However, obtaining these Pokémon with their Hidden Ability was not possible within Generation V itself.

Title: Mummy (Ability)

Known as ミイラ Mummy in Japanese, this Ability made its debut in Generation V. It serves as the signature Ability exclusive to Yamask and Cofagrigus. When a Pokémon wielding Mummy is struck by a contact move, the attacking Pokémon's Ability is subsequently transformed into Mummy. The assailant's original Ability is restored upon being switched out, fainting, or at the conclusion of the battle. If a Pokémon with Mummy takes damage from a multi-strike contact move (for instance, Fury Swipes), the attacker's Ability will convert to Mummy after the initial hit. Should a Pokémon with Rock Head employ a recoil-inducing contact move against a Mummy user, its Ability will first change to Mummy, and only then will it incur the recoil damage. In cases where a Pokémon with Parental Bond uses a contact move on a Mummy-equipped opponent, its Ability becomes Mummy after the first strike, yet the move will still continue to strike a second time as normal. If a Pokémon with Skill Link executes a multi-hit contact move against a Mummy-possessing target, its Ability will change to Mummy after the first hit, with the move proceeding to strike the maximum possible number of times nonetheless. However, certain Abilities remain impervious to Mummy's effect: As One, Battle Bond, Comatose, Commander, Disguise, Gulp Missile, Ice Face, Lingering Aroma, Multitype, Power Construct, RKS System, Schooling, Shields Down, Stance Change, Zen Mode, Zero to Hero, and Mummy itself. It has been confirmed that Gorm's Cofagrigus possesses Mummy as its Ability. The mechanism of this Ability potentially draws inspiration from the Japanese expression ミイラ取りがミイラになる (mīra-tori ga mīra ni naru), which translates to "the mummy-taker becomes the mummy".

Title: Moxie (Ability)

An Ability known as Moxie (Japanese: じしんかじょう Overconfident) was first introduced in Generation V. When a Pokémon endowed with Moxie directly causes another Pokémon (even an ally) to faint through a damaging attack, its Attack stat is boosted by one stage. Nevertheless, if its Attack has already reached the maximum of +6 stages, Moxie's effect will not occur. This Ability activates after the Pokémon receives an Attack increase from the move Fell Stinger. Should a Pokémon with Moxie defeat a foe possessing Mummy by using a contact move, the attacking Pokémon's Ability will convert to Mummy *before* Moxie gets an opportunity to activate. Moxie will still take effect even if the fainted Pokémon's defeat brings the battle to an end. However, if the Pokémon fainting concludes the battle, Moxie will not activate. Clay's Krokorok is depicted as having Moxie as its Ability. Litleo and Pyroar are the only Pokémon with Moxie that tend to prefer Special Attack over physical Attack, despite their physical Attack being the higher of the two stats.

Title: Justified (Ability)

Known in Japanese as せいぎのこころ (Heart of Justice), Justified is an Ability first introduced in Generation V. When a Pokémon possessing Justified is struck by a damaging Dark-type move, its Attack stat is boosted by one stage. This Ability provides neither immunity nor damage reduction against such attacks, and it will not activate if a Dark-type move hits a Substitute. However, Dark-type moves that strike multiple times, such as Beat Up, will trigger Justified for each individual hit, consequently increasing the Attack stat by an equivalent number of stages. Within Pokémon Legends: Arceus's internal code, Hisuian Growlithe and Hisuian Arcanine are designated Justified as their Hidden Ability, rather than Rock Head. The PokéSPedia data-book confirms that Blake's specific Keldeo, named Kelden, possesses Justified. Justified holds the unique distinction of being the sole Ability whose name begins with the letter 'J'. Outside of Inverse Battles, no Pokémon with this Ability is inherently weak to the Dark type. Only three Pokémon capable of having Justified—Growlithe, Arcanine, and Gallade—do not resist Dark-type moves. Gallade is unique among Justified users for having a type (Psychic) that is vulnerable to Dark, yet its secondary Fighting type effectively neutralizes this weakness.

Title: Rattled (Ability)
Rattled (Japanese: びびり Jitter) is an Ability that first appeared in Generation V. A Pokémon endowed with this Ability will have its Speed stat raised by one stage when it is hit by a damaging attack of the Bug, Dark, or Ghost type. It should be noted that no immunity is granted against these specific move types. For multistrike attacks of Bug, Dark, or Ghost typing, Rattled will activate its effect with every individual strike that connects. An additional trigger for Rattled's activation occurs if a Pokémon with the Ability has its stats reduced by Intimidate. Within Pokémon Legends: Arceus's internal data, Basculegion is internally designated with Rattled as its Ability, rather than Swift Swim. Interestingly, while the Bug, Dark, and Ghost types that prompt this Ability's activation are all highly effective against Psychic-type Pokémon, no Psychic-type Pokémon is able to possess Rattled.

Title: Magic Bounce (Ability)

The term "Magic Mirror" frequently pertains to this Ability, though for the Pokémon Conquest item of that name, consult the List of items in Pokémon Conquest. This Ability, known in Japanese as マジックミラー (Magic Mirror), was first introduced in Generation V. It possesses the unique property of reflecting specific status-category moves back towards the Pokémon that originally used them. The roster of moves susceptible to this reflection is identical to those affected by the move Magic Coat.

Even when reflected, these moves retain their potential to miss their target. Should Parting Shot be intercepted and sent back, the initial user of Parting Shot will experience a stat reduction, concurrently with the Magic Bounce holder being forced to switch out. Even if a Pokémon possessing Magic Bounce would ordinarily be immune to a move due to its inherent type (such as a Ground-type opposing Thunder Wave) or species (like Dugtrio against Telekinesis), the Ability will nonetheless cause the move to be reflected. Nevertheless, its activation is prevented if the Magic Bounce user is already safeguarded from the incoming move. The Ability's reflection can occur even if the incoming move would inherently fail (for instance, Disable on a Pokémon that hasn't acted or is already disabled) or would otherwise have no discernible effect on the target (such as Feather Dance when the target's Attack stat is already at -6). Magic Bounce will not trigger if the originating Pokémon possesses the Abilities Teravolt, Turboblaze, or Mold Breaker. It is incapable of reflecting a move that has already been reflected by either Magic Coat or another instance of Magic Bounce. Should a Pokémon with Magic Bounce be in the semi-invulnerable phase of a two-turn move like Fly, the Ability's effect will be suppressed.

When a move targets multiple Pokémon individually (e.g., Heal Block or Growl), it will impact all Pokémon without Magic Bounce as usual; subsequently, each Pokémon equipped with Magic Bounce will reflect the move individually. In scenarios where a move targets an entire side of the field (like entry hazard moves), if even one Pokémon on that side utilizes Magic Bounce to reflect the move, the entire side becomes immune to its effects; furthermore, even with multiple Magic Bounce users on that side, only a single Pokémon's Magic Bounce will activate. This Ability is capable of reflecting status Z-Moves (though not their associated Z-Power effects) as well as moves executed through Dancer or Instruct. Upon reflection of Z-Parting Shot, the Pokémon that subsequently enters the battle will not receive HP restoration.

Within Battle Royals, should several Pokémon possess Magic Bounce when a hazard-setting move is deployed, only the Magic Bounce of the Pokémon with the highest Speed will take effect. Even a Dynamax Pokémon, upon reflecting Parting Shot, will still be compelled to withdraw from battle. The Ability will not activate if the opposing Pokémon possesses Mycelium Might.

In the context of Pokémon UNITE, Magic Bounce serves as Espeon's Ability post-evolution. When Espeon is targeted by a hindrance (including slowing effects), that hindrance is nullified, and Espeon gains temporary immunity to all hindrances. Concurrently, this Ability inflicts damage upon the enemy responsible for the attempted hindrance and imposes a 30% slow for one second. For multi-hit hindrances, such as Pikachu's Volt Tackle, only the initial hit will be negated. A cooldown of 45 seconds applies to this Ability.

Within Japan Mini-volume 6 of the X & Y arc, Y's Absol, Solsol, was depicted as possessing Magic Bounce as its Ability upon Mega Evolution. No Pokémon naturally holds Magic Bounce as a standard Ability; its acquisition is limited to Hidden Ability status or via Mega Evolution. As the Ability for three distinct Mega-Evolved Pokémon, Magic Bounce shares the distinction with Tough Claws as the most frequently occurring Ability among Mega-Evolved forms. In Pokémon Scarlet and Violet, Magic Bounce represents the sole mechanism available for reflecting status moves, given that Magic Coat is unusable in those titles.

Title: Sap Sipper (Ability)

Originating in Generation V, the Ability known as Sap Sipper (Japanese: そうしょく, or Herbivorous) grants a unique response to certain attacks. Should a Pokémon possessing Sap Sipper be struck by any Grass-type maneuver, it gains immediate immunity to that attack's effects. Concurrently, its Attack stat receives a boost of one stage. Even multi-hit techniques, like Bullet Seed, will only augment the Pokémon's Attack once, regardless of the number of strikes. It's important to note that the move Aromatherapy does not trigger Sap Sipper's effects. Similarly, if the Pokémon is safeguarded from a Grass-type attack, Sap Sipper remains inactive. As might be expected, the majority of Pokémon capable of using this Ability are species drawing inspiration from herbivores.

Title: Prankster (Ability)

This Ability, named Prankster (いたずらごころ, or Prank Desire, in Japanese), was first introduced with Generation V. It grants a +1 increase to the priority bracket of all status-category moves. Historically, moves whose priority was raised by Prankster were not impeded by Quick Guard. However, as of a recent update, any status moves performed by a Prankster Pokémon can now be blocked by Quick Guard. Dark-type Pokémon are presently unaffected by hostile Pokémon's status moves that receive a priority increase from Prankster. This protective effect extends to moves brought forth by actions like Assist or Nature Power, which call upon other moves. Nevertheless, this immunity does not cover instances where a move is repeated due to a Prankster-boosted Instruct, nor does it apply to moves that gain an earlier turn order via a Prankster-enhanced After You. It's important to note that allied Dark-type Pokémon remain susceptible to status moves initiated by the same user with Prankster. Dark-type Pokémon can still utilize Magic Bounce or Magic Coat to send moves back; if a status move with Prankster-elevated priority is bounced by Magic Bounce or Magic Coat, it will affect Dark-type Pokémon, unless the Pokémon executing Magic Coat also possesses the Prankster Ability. Furthermore, status moves that target every Pokémon on the field, along with those that deploy entry hazards, will hit successfully, irrespective of any Dark-type Pokémon being present. A specific exemption applies to Perish Song and Rototiller: these moves cannot affect Dark-type adversaries if their priority is enhanced by Prankster. Even Status Z-Moves, such as Extreme Evoboost, benefit from Prankster's priority boost. The subsequent table provides a list of moves that are impacted by Prankster, alongside the Pokémon possessing this Ability that are also able to learn said moves. While certain moves, detailed below, are indeed affected by this Ability, no Pokémon that naturally possesses Prankster can currently acquire them. Nevertheless, these moves can still be utilized if a Prankster Pokémon learns them through Mimic, or if a Pokémon acquires Prankster through an Ability-modifying move or Ability. When a Pokémon with Prankster executes a status move, that move will be performed twice consecutively within the same turn, yet this only depletes 1 PP. Specifically within Pokémon Mystery Dungeon: Rescue Team DX, any moves influenced by Prankster are rendered ineffective against Dark-type Pokémon.

Title: Sand Force (Ability)
The Ability designated Sand Force (Japanese: すなのちから Sand Force) was first introduced in Generation V. When a sandstorm is active, it elevates the power of Rock-, Ground-, and Steel-type moves employed by the Pokémon possessing this Ability by 30%. Such a Pokémon also takes no damage from the sandstorm, provided it would normally be affected. Furthermore, under sandstorm conditions, Sand Force enhances the power of Rock-, Ground-, and Steel-type moves utilized by the Pokémon with this Ability by 2-times. Similarly, any Pokémon with this Ability is immune to sandstorm damage it would otherwise sustain.

Title: Iron Barbs (Ability)

Known in Japanese as てつのトゲ (Iron Thorns), this Ability made its debut in Generation V. Before the advent of Generation VII, it exclusively characterized Ferroseed and Ferrothorn as their signature Ability. Should a Pokémon possessing Iron Barbs be struck by a contact-based attack, the aggressor is dealt damage equivalent to 1/8 of its total HP. In scenarios where both the ability-user and the attacker are simultaneously incapacitated by Iron Barbs, the attacking Pokémon's faint condition takes precedence. When facing a multistrike contact move, for instance Fury Swipes, each individual strike will trigger Iron Barbs's effect. Alternatively, upon receiving a contact move, the attacking Pokémon sustains damage equal to 1/10 of its own maximum HP. Likewise, if a Pokémon endowed with this Ability is hit by a multistrike contact move (e.g., Fury Swipes), the Ability will activate with every single hit. However, this Ability fails to activate if the incoming move results in the Iron Barbs Pokémon being knocked out. Its Japanese designation matches that of the Iron Thorn item found within the Pokémon Mystery Dungeon franchise. The Japanese name for Iron Barbs also corresponds precisely to the English name of the Pokémon Iron Thorns.

Title: Zen Mode (Ability)

Known in Japanese as ダルマモード (Daruma Mode), Zen Mode is an Ability first introduced in Generation V. It serves as the signature Ability for Darmanitan and is also one of the distinctive signature Abilities for Galarian Darmanitan. When a Darmanitan possesses Zen Mode, it gains the capacity to alter its form during combat, a transformation dictated by its current HP. Each distinct form comes with its own specific types, base statistical values, Effort Value yield, physical height, and overall weight. No other Pokémon is affected by this Ability, even if it undergoes transformation to become Darmanitan.

Should a Standard Mode Darmanitan, endowed with Zen Mode, find its HP at 50% or below by the conclusion of a turn, it will transition into its Zen Mode. Conversely, if a Zen Mode Darmanitan, also with the Zen Mode Ability, finishes a turn with more than half its HP remaining, it reverts to its Standard Mode. Outside of combat, Darmanitan consistently remains in its Standard Mode. Upon being withdrawn from battle, a Zen Mode Darmanitan immediately reverts to its Standard Mode.

Regarding types: Unovan Darmanitan is Fire-type in Standard Mode, while Galarian Darmanitan is Ice-type. When in Zen Mode, Unovan Darmanitan becomes a Fire/Psychic dual-type, and Galarian Darmanitan transforms into an Ice/Fire dual-type. Should Darmanitan lose the Zen Mode Ability or have it suppressed by an effect like Gastro Acid, it will default to its Standard Mode and become incapable of further form changes. If Darmanitan acquires Zen Mode, it will initiate a form change at the turn's conclusion if its health points are less than half.

The move Role Play will not succeed if its target possesses Zen Mode. Similarly, Entrainment fails if Zen Mode is held by the user. The Ability Trace is unable to duplicate Zen Mode. Moves such as Gastro Acid, Worry Seed, and Simple Beam will all fail when targeting a Pokémon with Zen Mode. Role Play, Skill Swap, Entrainment, and Doodle are all unsuccessful if either the user or the target possesses Zen Mode.

Zen Mode resists attempts to be copied by Trace, Power of Alchemy, or Receiver; cannot be supplanted by Mummy or Lingering Aroma; remains unsuppressed by Core Enforcer or Neutralizing Gas; and cannot be exchanged by Wandering Spirit. Zen Mode Darmanitan and Galarian Zen Mode Darmanitan can exclusively be found possessing the Hidden Ability Zen Mode, with their standard Abilities remaining dormant. Among Hidden Abilities, Zen Mode stands alone as the sole one capable of triggering a form change. In both Pokémon animation and the Pokémon Adventures manga, Zen Mode is the Hidden Ability that appears most frequently.

Title: Victory Star (Ability)

The Ability known as Victory Star (Japanese: しょうりのほし Victory Star) made its debut in Generation V and is Victini's distinctive signature capability. This power elevates the accuracy of both the user's and its allies' moves during battle, excluding one-hit knockout attacks. This precision boost is approximately 10% (more precisely, a factor of 4506/4096) above the original accuracy. Notably, Victory Star's effects can accumulate, applying multiplicatively; for instance, if two allied Pokémon possess this Ability, the collective accuracy enhancement reaches 21%.

In the Super Smash Bros. series, Victini emerges from a Poké Ball, becoming available after one playthrough of 100-Man Smash. Its inherent Victory Star ability bestows knockback immunity and an attack power increase upon the fighter (and any teammates) who summons it. Subsequently, Victini reappears as a Poké Ball summon, granting the summoner immediate access to their Final Smash, facilitated by its Victory Star influence.

Furthermore, a Victini utilized by Blake was shown to possess Victory Star as its Ability. Within the narrative of The Birth of the Best Pokémon Trainer!, Cheren pointed out that Monta's Victini also had Victory Star, though it remained inactive because Serperior's Gastro Acid had nullified its effects.

The Pokémon Trading Card Game saw Victory Star's introduction as an Ability on Victini cards within the Noble Victories expansion. In the TCG, if a Pokémon featuring this Ability is active, a player, after resolving coin flips for an attack, gains the option to disregard all outcomes and re-flip the coins a single time. However, players are restricted to activating only one Victory Star Ability per turn. A compilation of Pokémon cards featuring the Victory Star Ability follows.

Title: Turboblaze (Ability)

Debuting in Generation V, Turboblaze (Japanese: ターボブレイズ Turboblaze) is an Ability that serves as the signature trait for Reshiram and White Kyurem. When a Pokémon possessing Turboblaze executes a move, the effects of all ignorable Abilities on any Pokémon are disregarded for the duration of that move's execution. To clarify, ignorable Abilities primarily encompass those that, if held by a target or its allies, could detrimentally influence a move's success rate, damage output, or overall effects. For instance, a Pokémon with Turboblaze can employ Overheat and successfully strike foes with Flash Fire, bypassing its immunity. Upon a Pokémon with Turboblaze entering battle, the system will display the notification: "<name> is radiating a blazing aura!". While Turboblaze continues to bypass Aura Break, it no longer negates the effects of Dark Aura or Fairy Aura. Volume 51 confirmed that Black's Reshiram possesses Turboblaze as its Ability.

Title: Teravolt (Ability)

Debuting in Generation V, the Ability known as Teravolt (テラボルテージ in Japanese) was introduced. It serves as the exclusive, signature Ability for Zekrom and Black Kyurem. When a Pokémon wielding Teravolt unleashes an attack, the effects originating from any Pokémon's ignorable Abilities are entirely nullified for that specific move's duration. (To clarify, "ignorable Abilities" broadly describes most Abilities that could potentially hinder a move's success rate, damage output, or overall effects if active on the target or its allies.) For instance, a Pokémon equipped with Teravolt using Thunder can successfully strike opponents that possess Lightning Rod. The moment a Pokémon featuring Teravolt makes its entrance, the distinctive message "<name> is radiating a bursting aura!" is displayed. Currently, Teravolt no longer disregards Dark Aura and Fairy Aura; however, it retains its capacity to ignore Aura Break. Volume 51 of the manga confirmed that N's Zekrom possessed Teravolt as its designated Ability. In terms of index numbering, Teravolt holds the final position among all Abilities from Generation V.

Title: Aroma Veil (Ability)

This passive skill, known in Japan as アロマベール (Aroma Veil), first appeared in the sixth generation of the Pokémon series. Before Generation VIII, Spritzee and Aromatisse were the sole Pokémon to possess this unique characteristic. It shields the Pokémon possessing it and all its allies (including non-adjacent ones) from afflictions like Taunt, Torment, Encore, Disable, the Cursed Body ability's effect, Heal Block, Psychic Noise, and infatuation.
